UNDER
THE
GUMTREE
the "Poputar" Omar.
( Rubaiyat of Omar
B Khayyam, in Macmil-
■ lan’s new edition at half-
a-crown, seemed likely to
be a great joy, and I sent for it.
I had never read Omar, and
knew nothing about him save
gossip. When I got the book, I
.understood what Matthew Arnold
meant when he complained of
the disgraceful way the journey-
man work of bookmaking is done
in England. The editor is
anonymous. The preface, pp.
1-25, j.s. also anonymous, .and
much of 'it extracted from a
review (date not given) by Pro-
Professor Cowell. After the
preface comes a translation in
though one may conjecture that the author’s name
is Fitz Gerald. As one edition of the original text
of Omar (preface p. 17) contains 464 verses,
one gathers that the “ translation ” is a selection.
And apparently it is a translation in a Shakes-
pearian sense (‘•Bottom, thou art translated !”),
for we read in a note on p. 111, by the anonymous
editor, that the most audacious verse of ali is
essentially invented. Here it is ;
Lxxxr.
Oh, Thou, who Man of baser Earth didst make,
And ev’moith Paradise devise the Snake:
For all the Sin wherewith tke Face of Man
Is blacken'd—Alan’s forgiveness give—and take!
And in said note we find Professor Cowell quoted
to effect that “there is no original for the line
about the snake, ” and that in the last line “ Fitz-
Gerald” mistook the ineaj'iing of a certain verse ;
and ,when informed of his mistake did not care to
correct it!
After the translation we get anonymous notes,
and then, if you please, another translation of
Orftar called “ The first edition. ” Then a sort of
comparative table showing the alterations in the
successive editions; then an editor’s note of half' a
page,; and. our book (111 pp.) comes to an end.
Have I conveyed clearly how entirely anonym-
ous and third-hand»-tohe whole business is? And
may a common person say how the book should
have been written ? First, I would have the editor
give his name, and tell us plainly who Omar was,
not inferentially, quoting a quotation from Mirk-
hond as quoted in Cdliutta Review, but of his own
knowledge. That is, let him go to the original
authorities, as I presume Mirkhond did. Then
let him saywhat language Omar used, and
what are the recognised editions of his ‘ ‘ Rubaiyat, ”
Then a few words about Fitz Gerald ; who he was,,
what knowledge he had of the language Omar
wrote in, and the extent to which his translation
really reproduces Omar. After this, let us have
the translation as finally revised, and all the pre-
vious editions can be dispensed with. Then some
notes. Th9.se. we have are very inadequate in
parts, and in parts rambling and superfluous.
Without notes, Omar, as a personality, is unintel-
ligible by the vulgar; and you must remember
that there is a cheap edition for the million, of
which it is boasted that one shop in the Strand
(London) sold 300 copies in one day.
Let me clench the nail I have driven, by saying
that when we get on page 89 a heading Kuza-
nama, and there is no note, we (the million) feel
insulted and baffled. What can we do? We
don’t know whether it hr Arabic or Persian. We
can’t look it up. A painstaking man can get
at. a French, German, or Latin dictionary;
even a Greek or Hebrew one; but Persian ?
From internal evidence I should say that Omar
wrote or collected; and edited some sets of qua-
trains, not attempting to construct a continuous
poem. On these Fitz Gerald has based a sort of
medley. How much of it Omar wrote himself I
dare not even guess. How much Fitz Gerald
altered it Ido not certainly know. All this the
editor should have explained. That some of the
quatrains stand in the very first rank of English
poetry is, I suppose, as plain and clear as any
matter of literary judgment can be. And, in one
sense, this being so, it matters nothing what
materials Fitz Gerald worked on ; that is, had he
called if an original poem I should not have cared.
But it seems very misleading to use Omar’s name
and put in his mouth modern ideas he would not
have understood. And I do not comprehend the
way Fitz Gerald is kept in the background. We
are not even told his initials, or whether he is
still living.
IGNOTUS.
. [ Ignotus’s” complaint is for the most part
justified. Reverence for Fitz Gerald’s whims or
reasons has been carried too far. His literary
executor and editor, W. Aldis Wright, persists in
regarding the “ Omar ” chiefly as an expression of
r itzGerald’s personality : what we are much more
concerned with is its value as a contribution to
literature. Fitz Gerald’s motives in publishing
anonymously, and in preserving anonymity,
matter little. He modestly or timorously dis-
trusted himself and his work; he was full of fads
and fancies.
... As to the publication of my name [he wrote to H.
Sehiitz-Wilson in 1882], I believe I could well dispense
with it, were it other and better than it is. But I have
some unpleasant associations with it; not the least of
them being that it was borne, Christian and Surname, by
a man who left college just as I went there. . . . What
has become of him I know not; but he, among other
causes, has made me dislike my name. . . .
Further, Fitz Gerald was sensitive; and held
stinging memories of a severe review (of his trans-
lations from Calderon) which the Athenceum
printed six years before “Omar” was issued—a
review followed by the book’s withdrawal from
circulation.
What Fitz Gerald did with Omar is concisely
stated by Nathan Dole—
He ingeniously wove into a life-cycle of agnosticism a
number of originally disconnected and isolated quatrains,
informing the whole with the unity of his own personality
and with the brilliancy of his peculiar genius. He took
the Persian’s thought and the Persian’s manner, but made
it his with just such high-handed, modest, one might
almost say unconscious, art of lordly rapine as Shake*
spe'are displayed toward the predecessors whom he robbed
to glorify.
FitfcG •raid's ifiea-’of a translator's jJrivitegdSrwhs-
partly set forth in the advertisement prc.ixed to
his versions of Calderon. lie wijote—
I do not. btflievh an exact, translation of this poethati:be
very successful; .retaining . . . conceits that were a
fashion of the day ; or idioms that, true and inlclliyible
to one nation, check the current of sympathy in others to
which they are unfamiliar . . .
I have, while faithfully-trying: to retain what was fine
and efficient, sunk, reduced;:altered, and replaced, much
that seemed not ; simplified ‘some perplexities, and cur-
tailed or omitted scenes that seemed to mar the breadth
of general effect, supplying such Omissions by some.lines
of after-narrative. ...
And Fitz Gerald’s “ Rubaiyat ” is neither Fitz-
Gerald nor Omar, but Fitz Omar.
“ Ignotus ” is mistaken, however, in thinking
that variorum versions can be dispensed with,
even in a “ popular ” edition. For Fitz Gerald,
“like the ass of genius he was,” has frequently
revised for the worse—and one naturally prefers
to have the better at hand for solace and quota-
tion. Some persons have re-edited all the editions
for their own satisfaction and private circulation,
selecting and fusing what seemed best in each ;
but there is doubtfully anyone with sufficient
authority, and assurance, to do as much for the
public at present.
In a private letter “Ignotus” confesses to
puzzlement at Fitz Gerald’s use of capitals sq
a note by A. B. Miall, in Lond. Academy, may
helpfully be added
“Speaking of Fitz Gerald’s ‘Omar/have many
of his Readers considered to what a Degree the
Success of his Version, delightful though it be, is
Due to his Employment of Capital Letters ?
Literature now, to its great Hurt, it may be,
appeals little to the Ear, but chiefly to the
Eye ; and whether this he because Reading Aloud
is a Lost Art, as our Elders would have; or that
our Taste is so far bettered' that it will not suffer
this Art in patience, which yet so far subsists as
to make a natural Delivery the Rarest of Things:
however this be, it is often seen that Poetry is
most admired which in Sound is indeed Un-
musical, and Prose which has no natural
Nuance ; for the '.reason that they charm
the Understanding by instantly Impressing
the t: 'Visual Sense, in such Manner that the
Mind has no Need to go searching about to
apprehend the Meaning. This I believe to be the
Explanation of the Continued Admiration of
Many for the Pqems of such Writers as Pope and
Addison, which many others count as poor Stuff :
biit by the Fact that all the Nouns are pointed out
with such Clearness to the Lye, such work will
impress the Sluggish, Mind far more keenly and
vividly than Work which does not so Solicit the
Eye, and thus tbyrp are Persons of feeble Wits
who reckon the Literature of the Last Century far
superior to that of this: as a Woman will con-
sider another Woman’s Letter to be far the superior
of a Man's, as it is so Emphasised, by Italics and
Extravagant Words , as to penetrate the mind In-
stantly : And with Literature it is far other than
with Jewels, for the Generality rate most Highly
that which is Comprehended with Least Pain.
“ This Printing of Words by means of Capital
Letters will also give a Passage a Balance and a
Rhythm which do not by Right belong to it; each
Capital giving the Mind a little Pause : So that I
have seen Passages highly Applauded' by famous
Critics, which stripped of their Nobility of Capi-
tals had fallen into the most vulgar Journalese ;
no more, compared with their former State, than
is a member of society compared with a member of;
Society.
“As a Case in Point, consider the following
Quatrain, done almost faithfully into English
from Omar ’ •
i.
“.Stay me .with flagons, comfort-me with wine,
This amber face make like a ruby shine,
And when I die with wine my body wash,
My coffin wattle of the roots of vine.
ii.
“ Stay me with Flagons ! Comfort me with Wine 1
This Amber Face make like a Ruby shine !
And when I die with Wine my Body wash,
My Coffin wattle of the Roots of Vine ! ”
And that is all about Omar for the present.]
[This is published because, with reservations made,
it has value and interest as representing the lot of
a second-flight journalist in London. For people
• who knew Mr. Martin in Australia it is unneces-
sary to add-one word. To the new generation
which never heard of Joseph, it is explained that (as
might be gathered from recent statements and
commentaries) _ Mr. Martin is a : ■ second
Sladen —a Britisher with ordinary brains, more
than ordinary energy, and super-extraordinary
cheek. Both are. excellent persons in their way ;
but it is not the best literary way.' As editor of
“ Who’s Who, ” Sladen has found a niche where
he writes.2o,ooo letters a year —and no poetry.
He is “ doing' splendidly and as an energetic
compiler Mr. Martin would rival him. He
also should “drop literature,” and persuade some
publisher to place him in charge of a similar
reference-book—say, ‘ ‘ What’s What. ” For Mr.'
Martin’s creative work is of no permanent
account whatever; for the most part it kernels
the commonplace in a husk of crude form. As
journalism, much of it is readable and very little
rememberable. The “Life of Robert Lowe” is
the rubble tomb of a reputation : it is one of
literature’s ironies that “brilliant Bob” should
have been interred by a biographer with
the vivacity of an underdone muffin. Mr.
Martin’s good-natured impudence is shown by
the manner in which he argues from his own ex-
perience to Lawson’s Loudon prospects—-as if he
and Lawson stood on the same plane of power or
possibility! As poet and prose-writer, Lawson
has a streak of dissatisfied genius : Mr. Martin is
complacent with an inferior talent. The one
writer is alloyed gold ; the other, thinly-silvered
brass. Very likely Brass would succeed much better
in London ; very likely Lawson would be beaten
by rebuffs which Mr. Martin, would receive stolidly.
“ You must kno w how to box, ” said Jem. Mace ;
“but that’s only the beginning: Fighting is give-
and-take : you want a Quick Lye, afad you want a
Thick Hide.” 'And the much-advised and
patronised Lawson (nobis baud culpa iiboris)
is thin-skinned for two. That does not alter
the fact that Lawson would attack London with
authentic capacity and a valid performance :, Mr.
Martin had neither. There.is pursuing literature
and there is—pursuing literature ; and Mr.
Martin’s is the other kind of pursuit. Let us
surround with an appropriate brass rule the im-
pregnable moral ivlnch is the conclusion of the
whole advertisement ' fi ■;>
Always cultivate -Modesty oe Soul, with
a Due Amount oe Intellectual Sklk-Oon-
oeit. This is the True Secret oe [My]
Literary Genius.
A. Patciiett Martin.

“Pursuing Literature” in London.
When I read Henry Lawson’s jeremiad on
“Pursuing Literature in Australia” ( Bulletin ,
Jan 21st, 1899) —the moral of which is that the
young Australian genius should rush to London or
commit suicide—it struck me that many readers
(including Mr. Lawson) might like to hear a plain
tale of literary and journalistic experience, extend-
ing over some 15 years, in England.
I can vividly recall the time when, m Mel-
bourne, having helped to found and edit for some
eight years The Melbourne Review, with but indif-
ferent pecuniary results (though with infinite local
glory) I determined to transfer my activities to
London. I may add that 1 was never, of a
foolishly sanguine temperament, and not more
conceited than a very young man should be. I
saw that no money could be made in Melbourne
by writing except for the newspapers ; and I was
perhaps unwisely encouraged to try my fortunes
m England by one or two rising English authors,
chief among whom was my constant correspondent
the late It. L. Stevenson, then resident in Edin-
burgh. Moreover, to the genuinely literary
aspirant in all new lands—even in America to this
hour—there inevitably comes the feeling of deep
disgust with one’s actual environment, and the
accompanying overpowering desire to dwell in a
land of literary and intellectual traditions. It
was this feeling, far more than the desire to find a
market for my wares, which drew me to England,
though, no doubt, in my glorious youthful self-
confidence I felt that once in London fortune
would smile upon me. However, neither the
Authors’ Society nor the Literary Agent was then
in existence; and I entered on the great sea of
London literary life in a state of infantile sim-
plicity, with a volume of essays and poems under
my arm, and a very scanty roll of bank-notes in
my breeches pocket.
I found the reality vastly different from the
youthful dream. Yet I cannot honestly say, in
looking back over the last fifteen years, that I
have anything to complain of, or that my lot could
have been pleasanter had I remained with Marcus
Clarke, Garnet Walch, Julian Thomas, Brunton
Stephens, and many lesser-known men, with
whom I had been associated on the Melbourne
Review and other literary ventures. But I dis-
tinctly warn readers that “pursuing literature ” in
London is by no means all beer and skittles, any
more than it is in Australia. Very shortly after
“settling down” in London, I found that my
youthful visions and day-dreams must be utterly
abandoned. Messrs. Griffith and Farran under-
took to issue my book of poems and essays,
“Fernshawe,” for which I duly penned what
'§• 'gnteofttl “ Introduction”
to “the English reader.” This handsomely
got-up work contained what Lawson would call
“ the cream ”of my Australian.literary work ; and,
glancing over it again, after all these years, I
must frankly assert that there :are, some pages
in it of which lam not at all ashamed. Result:
Excellent press notices, many friendly; letters
from distinguished authors; but after months of
weary waiting—a sale of some to, 4o eo,flips;
which, of course, did not' pay the mere adver-
tising expenses. ■
There’s nothing like a “facer.” After my
“ Fernshawe ” experience I was no longer Under
any delusion as to the fortune to be acquired by
publishing poems and essays in London. I .delete*
fully,put away-under lock and key a fresh volume
of rhymes, and, like Dr, Johnson,, “ took a walk
down Fleet-street.” It was evident that I must
seek niv daily bread (to say nothing of the awful-
priced English meat) in what Matthew Arnold,in
his superiority styles the common arena of the
British press. So I took off my singing robes and
threw my harp away, and seized a journalist’s
quill. I had various most unpleasant experiences
—with “Society” papers chiefly, which, in the
matter of paying contributors, are often as un-
satisfactory as Lawson’s workmen’s organs. I did
any and everything —from collecting (or prefer-
ably inventing) fashionable items about the aris-
tocracy—it is wonderful how easily one picks up
the trick in England—to wri ting speeches and
addresses for provincial aldermen and suburban
magnates. One of my efforts in the latter cate-
gory was a most terribly florid and rhetorical ad-
dress on “Enthusiasm” which'l wrote in a
Bloomsbury lodging-house from “notes” on my
unpaid bills. It was delivered to a crowded and
delighted audience of Christian young men in
Liverpool. My price was £lO cash.
I was beginning to make some headway among
the ruck of Fleet-streeters, and had become quite
a. well-known Savage Clubite, where I regularly
dined at the famous (and cheap) Saturday night
dinners—upon more than one occasion presiding
over that august assembly with the weirdly
humorous assistance of E. j. Odell,-who used to
recite my verses, “My Cousin from Pall Mall,”
with rich humour. About this, time, an old Mel-
bourne press acquaintance, Mr. Philip Mennell,
arrived m London to take over the Age agency.
In a somewhat informal way I became a kind, of
assistant to him. We used to sleep in the dirty
old office in Fleet-street—l, on a particularly hard
sofa, while Mennell reposed generally on the floor,
with the London Directory for his pillow !
Having been sub-editor of the. Age,' Mennell
thoroughly understood'what David Syme expected
of him in London. He'Was almost 1 loyal and effi-
cient servant of the paper, working all the hours of
God’s day and night, and, with my somewhat
irregular assistance, maintaining a battle against
all the forces of the Argus, with their staff of well-
paid men, and numerous outside helpers and influ-
ence. We rose usually about 4 a. in. from our
broken slumbers, and at once plunged into the
business off the day, securing our “early” copies
of the Lo ’.cion dailies hot from the presses, and
then toga .her,making up our morning cablegrams,
which yrs took, ourselves into the city and des-
patched from Broad : street about G. o’clock. We
had not only to fight the embattled hosts of the
ATv/ttS single-handed • but we had to compress
the news into about one-third the number of-words
at the disposal of our rivals ; and woe betide
poor Mennell if by any chance (or by design, as
often happened) any small item of news was
absent in the Age and present in the Argus cable-
grams. The great David used to prepare a cate-
gorical list of “omissions,” which we received
months afterwards by post, and pondered over in
bur solitary and gloomy hours. It was an unend-
ing grind—the hours generally from 4 a.m. till 12
p.m. In course of time,Mennell grew very sick
of the Age, and resigned. For many years past lie
has been a prosperous financial man and journa-
list, editing his, own paper, The British A ustrala-
sian, and doing many kindly acts, in a cynical
fashion, to unfortunate Australians in London.
After a while, I began to get my “second wind. ”
My first real success, of course, was the article on
Adam Lindsay Gordon in Temple Bar, February,
1884. I subsequently wrote a number of literary
and social essays in various magazines, and. con-
tributed to the Academy and Athenaeum pretty
regularly. Then the old yearning returned, and I
wanted to bring out- another substantial volume.
As luek would have it, I turned my mind to poli-
ties rather than to poetry this time, and wrote
“Australia and the Empire.” Something caused
me to look up the early records of the late Lord
Sherbrooke, and I opened my new book with a
chapter headed, “Robert Lowe in Sydney.” Lord
Sherbrooke was then out of public life, though he
often attended the House of Peers, but had
become a vigorous literary eontroversalist in the
monthly reviews ; and when he read that early
chapter of his own half-forgotten life, he wrote
very kindly to me and expressed his great pleasure
and, what I valued more, his intellectual appre-
ciation of my effort. Subsequently he made me
his literary executor, and I wrote his “ Life and
Letters,” in two big volumes which Longmans
published in 1893. The book, of course, is not a
popular or great financial success ; but in my
three years’ work I realised some £3OO directly,
and perhaps .£5OO subsequently. The writing of
Lord Sherbrooke’s life also led to my going on the
Spectator staff, under the late Mr. W. H. Hutton,
and to much magazine writing in the National
Review and elsewhere. In short, it gave me more
of an established and recognised literary status
than I could have hoped for.
I have written and published (generally on com-
mission) numbers of minor works, including “True
Stories of Australian History,” for which I got
£3O; and “Over the Sea,” £25. I have in my
rash vain-glory brought out a book of verses (old
and new) entitled “ The Withered Jester,” with a
superb frontispiece by Phil May, from which—
though a £1 Is. edition-de-luxe, limited to GO
copies, sold out, and a fair number of the ss. copies
—I have up to date received not one shilling. The
publishers, J. M. Dent and Co., are not very san-
guine of financial success.
some -time I- edited for ITutehtnsofis, and
aiterwards for Methuen, a monthly periodica]
(■abort Li-erary Opinio%l which is chiefly treasured
by me because* it brought me info relations' with
tlie late Christina •Rbs'setti. She bfcame my chief
contributor, and wrote a deal of her most exquisite
later verse in Literary Opinion. When I knew
Miss Rossetti she was of course at the zpnfth of
her fame ; but she told me that her literary income
for the greater part of her life was always under
£no a year. What will Lawson think of that ?
Remember that CJrristina Rossetti’s name was a
household word in America as well as in England ;
and that, owing to her profound and mystical
piety, such books,as “ Time Elies,” and the sm.J
voliunes of devotional verses, were read by thou-
sands upon thousands for their pure religiosity,
rather than for the magic of the verse. Yet her
earnings were under a pound per week. And
Mane Corelli—ye gods !
I could tell Mr. Lawson of dozens of well-known
English writers who have brought out book after
book, either at a loss, or without a penny of profit.
The fact is, English Literature, like the English
Church, has a number of wealthy “incumbents”
who labour assiduously for honour and glory,
without a thought of personal reward. A great
friend of mine has published several books of nigh-
class poetry from which ho has never received or
expected one farthing. It is said that Aubrey de
Vere —Tennyson’s friend, a most beautiful Catholic
poet—has always actually been out of pocket to
the tune of A,300 annually,'’ on his poetic pro--
ductions. To such men money is of no con-
sequence ; but it does not improve the chance of
the poor struggling rhymester, who not only has
to compete against the long roll of English poets
from Chaucer to Tennyson, but also against a
small army Of wealthy amateurs who are often
men of . distinct literary power, and always of great
social influence.
To wind up this sermon, the' only class of con-
temporary ' literature in England (or elsewhere)
that means big success from: the “gate-money ”
standpoint is popular, up-to-date fiction, and play-
writing. If a man can bring out a stirring story
in print or on the stage that immediately appeals
to the non-literary. crowd and not to the mere
critics and litterateurs, he may make as much
money as a fairly successful stockbroker. Tenny-
son is an exception to the rule that financially
Poetry is a drug in the market—but unfortunately
not the satnekind of drug as Blank’s Fruit Salt,
or !)ash’s Yellow Pills.
My advice to any young Australian writer
whose talents have been recognised (to quote Mr.
Lawson) is to “sit tight,” and stay where he is.
To write hard and diligently for all his soul is
worth, and to send the-best of his surplus to Eng-
land and America. In these days it should be
as easy to do one’s literally business from Sydney
as from London or New York. Write, Write,
TTWte—and fie as Australian as you intelligently
can. The very fact of an Australian writer
.writing from Australia may be his best recom-
mendation, Above all, do not ignore the ever-
hungr.y American market. And always cultivate
' modesty of soul, with a due amount of intellectual
self-conceit.’ This is the true secret of literary
genius.
A. PATCHETT MARTIN.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A cheerful spectacle, recently, at Wil-
mington, U.S., was the flogging of 15 offenders
before a crowd of spectators, including many
women, who looked eagerly on at a beastliness
which Delaware State law ordains shall take place
in public.
“Roma”: A tip for the Papacy Stakes.
Before me is the full list of Cardinals for 1899, as
complete as the entries for Melb. Cup, and much
more easily reducible. First, it is useless to argue
with people who talk about anything but an
Italian. That cuts the card down. Next elimin-
ate the aged and other impossibles—the Newmans
of the Red Hat. There are about a dozen left.
The first rank is that of Card. Bishops ; but the
second rank, of Card. Priests, is equally eligible.
The third, and last, rank—Deacons—may be
scratched out, as a rule, for Papal elections. The
first man who catches my eye is Vicar-General
Parocchi, aged 66; next, Secretary of State
Rampolla, aged 56, These two, and Jacobim,
aged 62, are in every tip. Satolli, aged 60, the
successful delegate to the U.S. of America, goes
strong. After this, one only looks round for
dangerous outsiders—like Svarnpa, the brilliant
Archbishop of Bologna and youngest Cardinal,
aged 48; Ferrari, Archbishop of Milan, aged 49 ;
Prisco, Archbishop of Naples, aged 63; and
Stefano, Papal Chamberlain, aged 71.
The New Song.
Hands of a people
Building, and bringing
Her sons together,
Each to each clinging ;
Heart of a people
Exulting, singing :
Our corn is in stook;
And laden the barge
Floats down the river
That meets the sea-marge.
Our welfare is one,
The least with the large.
By our deserts grey,
By the dry creek-bed,
By the girth-high grass
Where our herds are fed,
By the open door
In barriers’ stead,
We have given our pledge—
And our love is sure
While the racing rip
And the tide endure —■
And we front the world
With a Hope secure.
PROSPEOT GOOD.
In Memoriam.
So you have reached the distant shore, my
brother,
While many who set out with you abreast
A re still at sea, and hailing one anoth t,
And battling for the harbor lights of Rest!
Ah, for the hush that fell upon the water
When you had passed the Pillars of the Blest,
And when the pilot saw your bark and brought
her
To moor within the everlasting Rest!
Ah, for the port! And valiant souls or craven,
We all must steer the course towards the West.
We all are struggling for the same great haven ;
We all are hoping for the same sweet Rest.
The gorgeous liner, gilt from keel to spanker,
The crazy lugger, rolling storm-distressed,
Are destined both to reach the port, and anchor
Beside the city of eternal rest.
Be fair the wind, or foul —what does it matter ?
The worse the storm, the sooner ends the quest.
And though the waters roar and rave and chatter —
The port draws near. One thing is certain : Rest!
VON KOTZE.
Errant Wild Rose.
Here by the sliprails,
On the silent, shadowed track,
O, errant Wild Rose,
He waits with the Moon
And lingering evening star —
Star so like thine eyes.
Warm December wind
In the cornfield whispering,
He hears but thy song,
And the boom of kine
To the milker’s distant call,
And the hounds’ far bay,
And the crested dove
Crooning its love overhead
In the green and gold.
Impatient he waits
For thy dove-song in his ear
O errant Wild Rose !
The low star lingers,
And the brave, kind moon is high,
And a heart beats fast.
The sweet wind calls thee,
And the wheat-ears beckon thee,
Tas.
And thou comest not.
BARKIS.

yankee Doodle
IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York; The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an oid man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his sou, and grandson. ' The old father,
vhth silvery flovving locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily,’ the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light 'of battle was m
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on thaWfateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, JULY 29, 1899.
Premier Reid’s Overdraft.
An unsavory little row, with mud and person-
alities in it, which broke out last week in
N.S.W. Assembly, centred to a great extent
round Premier Reid’s overdraft. G. H. Retd,
like most Australasian Premiers, has an over-
draft ; or perhaps it would he safer to say that
he has an overdraft and most other Austra-
lasian Premiers badly want to have one. And
G. H. Reid’s assets are unhappily so very
weak security for any kind of an overdraft that
the charge of using corrupt political devices to
get the money came along almost as a matter of
eourse. Nobody listened to the charge in a
serious spirit, and there was no evidence
wort h calling such behind it, and the muddy
little row dropped again very unobtrusively.
But members gained at least one item of infor-
mation out of it. They learned that Premier
Reid was in debt when he took office ; that his
overdraft has been growing ever since ; and
that he apparently looks at the liability with a
despairing eye, and doesn’t know what on
earth to do with it.
• * • •
Of course nobody who knows anything of
political life expected for a moment that Pre-
mier Reid wouldn’t have an overdraft if he
could get one. Political salaries in Australia
are so poor and squalid that almost all public
men end in something approaching beggary.
What a procession of ghosts in rags they
make ! Think of a few in recent years : John
Robertson, overwhelmed with debt and dying
insolvent, even the charitable grant made him
by Parliament having gone as a sop to his
creditors ! Parkes, pursued by duns and
bailiffs, and dying worth about ss. in cash !
Vogel, living in Ids last, years on a sine-
cure billet at £3OO a year found for him
by the Maoriland Government, and worth about
£5O all told when he went where the good Jews
go ! Berry, subsisting on an annuity reluct-
antly supplied to him by the Turner Ministry
—which Ministry, to its unspeakable shame,
refused to arrange for the continuance to the
wife of the almosfc-nonagenarian statesman,
if she outlived him, of the poor little monthly
dole. Douglas, mouldering as a Police
Magistrate at Thursday Island, at the place
where semi-civilisation ends and plain barbar-
ism begins ! James Munro, collecting rents in
a Melbourne suburb 1 Seddon, who admits
that, after his long term of office, his private
income isn’t up to the income-tax limit ! Reid,
with his hopeless overdraft ! Almost the only
notable Australian politician of late years who
wouldn’t be really glad all over to find half-a-
crown in the street is G. R. Dibbs, and he went
broke when in office, like the rest —only his
country kindly threw him out of the heart-
breaking billet, and he bad the luck to find a
situation in the Savings Bank, and his old and
lengthy bones now rest in % calm haven. But
Dibbs had such luck as comes to few politicians.
♦ * * *
Take the case of the ordinary politician—the
man who isn’t in office. His country gives him
various sums, ranging from nothing in West-
ralia up to £3OO a-year in N.S.W. Subscrip-
tions to charities and the like in his electorate
are a practically unavoidable tax. The expense
of hiring halls wherein to periodically address
his free aud independent supporters ; travelling
expenses—those that are not covered by his
free pass—hotel expenses, small loans to stone-
broke constituents, drinks for thirsty electors,
extra cost of living in town if he is a
country member, are all so much off his little
salary. They probably absorb half of it even
in N.S. W„, where the rate is highest. If he
had a billet, he almost certainly had to give it
up, in order to have time for his Parliamentary
duties. If he had a business, it probably gave
him up, provided he attended to his Parlia-
mentary work conscientiously—only a man like
George Turner, with a capacity for incessant
labor from early morning to early next morning,
can serve two masters and serve them both rea-
sonably well. And when the ordinary member
is thrown out by his grateful country he gener-
ally finds himself beginning the world afresh,
older and poorer than he was before, with his
business connection or his billet lost, and with
this difference—that instead of being Smith,
the rising grocer, or engineer, or whatever it
w r as, he is now only Smith, the broken-down
politician. As a rule he deserved to be thrown
out for not being a better politician. But also,
as a rule, he was as good a politician as anyone
could reasonably expect to hire at the price.
* * * *
The politician who isn’t thrown out often
comes to a worse end. C. H. Reid is a fair
sample of this kind. After a long and efficient
official civil service career he was called to the
bar in 1879 and went into politics in 1880, with
an immensity of ambition hidden behind a mask
of fat and indifference. He spent about a
dozen years as a private member and one
year as a subordinate minister in a cheap
and nasty Government before his chance came,
but he was pursuing the Dead Sea apple of
“ hoffis ” all the time. Therefore he never had
leisure to be a successful barrister. He might
never have been one in any case, but any chance
that there was in that direction he sacrificed to
politics. He never even became a legal hack
of any standing, and consequently small, com-
fortable, legal billets, such as magistracies and
subordinate Judgeships and Crown-Prosecutor-
ships, passed hnn by. When he was tolerably
old, and had become a fixed public institution
as George Reid, the unsuccessful barrister,
the office which three stone-brokers—Robert-
son, Parkes, and Dibbs —had vacated in quick
succession really came in his way. He had
.£1370 a year—probably after paying his share
of the salary of the Government Whip and all
the various charitable and other calls incidental
to his office he had really £BOO or £9OO, or even
less. He was expected to live in a manner
. more or less befitting his high office—
the Premier of an important province can’t
w r ell hang out in a terrace house in Pad-
dington or Waverley. He had accumulated
heavy debts while pursuing the phantom of
hoffis, and he had to pay the interest on these.
He grew steadily poorer ; for, after all, a Pre-
mier can’t live on £BOO or £9OO a year and yet
keep up the dignity which is supposed to
attach to his billet ; and the last shadowy rem-
nants of a law-practice that was never much
more than a shadow left him through want of
opportunity to attend to it.
* * • •
In the ordinary course of events, his career
should be about over now. He is growing old,
not in the best of health, and has been five
years in office. The 12 Premiers who have
bossed N.S.W. averaged about 3J years each,
so George Reid has had a fair innings. And
if he doesn’t find a comfortable, permanent
Federal or other billet his prospect is to be an
unemployed old man, a broken-down politician,
a decayed barrister with no practice, and too old
to begin constructing one, a man with heavy
debts and no income—to put it shortly, a man
who has “ dropped out.”
* • • *
The career of a successful politician is very
often—much too often—something like this :
Seven or eight years as a private member, dur-
ing which period he has to neglect his business,
either wholly or in great part, in order to
attend to public affairs. The net remuneration
is about £l5O a year at the outside. Then five
or six years of office, during which time his
salary possibly runs to £9OO or £lOOO a year
net, and about this time the last vestiges of his
private business usually disappear through
sheer inability to attend to it. And then an
indefinitely prolonged old age—the period of
failure and neglect, when it is too late to start
the world afresh. This is the career of the
successful politician ; that of the unsuccessful
one is much the same, except that the com-
paratively bright interval in the middle is left
out. The country wants its ablest men to run
the Government. It wants to buy the very
best talent it can get, for the difference to the
country, in visible cash alone, between a first-
rate and a merely third-rate Premier and Trea-
surer, may run to a million or two during the
life of one Ministry. And this is how, in
N.S.W. at all events, it bids for the services
of the very best men :
Premier (man who is supposed to run the coun-
try) after paying proportion of Whip’s salary
and other expenses incidental to his position,
say per year, at a liberal estimate ... ...
Sheriff (man who is responsible for hangings) ... <5O
Coroner (man who sits on dead bodies) .. ... ”50
Stipendiary Magistrate at Sydney (man who lines
drunks) ... ... ... •••
District Court Judge ... ... •••
Clerk of the Peace ... ... _ ■■■ ••• '5O
Governor of Darlinghurst Gaol (with quarters,
fuel, light, and servant) ... .. ••• £OO
Chief of Police ... ... ••• ••• 920
Government Printer ... ... ... “00
And so on indefinitely.
• • * •
And these men, it must be remembered, have
life billets, subject to good behaviour —not a
job which is liable to be cut short any day by a
vote of censure or a general election. They
don’t spend years and years working for next
to nothing a-year while climbing up to a poorly-
salaried place, which may only last three
months when they get there. And many of
them have pensions as well. If George Reid,
when he began his Bar career 20 years ago, had
fixed his eye on a police magistracy, or a com-
fortable place as coroner, where he could make
humorous remarks about dead bodies, or
had aimed at being a Clerk of Petty
Sessions in a big town, he would probably
have made a larger average salary than
he was done by aiming at the highest office in
the State. Anri yet his case is a good one
compared to that of some politicians. Con-
sider Edmund Barton, who has let a lucrative
practice go to the four (or more) w'inds of
heaven while he spent his time in trying to
build up an Australian Commonwealth. And
Barton had no official salary ; a great part of
the time he hadn’t even the scanty salary of a
private member or a private member’s free
railway pass.
• • * *
Politics don’t pay—at least for the average
man they are not in the same street with being
a coroner or manager of a branch insurance-
office.
The Snake of Religious Dissension Lifts
its Head in Victoria.
The persistent pressure by religious organisa-
tions to make the State do the work which they
exist to do, and which gives the only justifica-
tion for their existence, has recently climaxed
in an attempt to re-introduce religious teaching
in Victorian State schools. Victorian reli-
gionists have always the opportunity of teach-
ing their own children their own particular
creed ; and since they profess to consider their
creeds of such vital importance, it is singular
that they should wish to transfer the teaching
to the perfunctory charge of State schoolmas-
ters. Moreover, the Victorian religionists
support a small army of professional exponents
of religion, who may be supposed to be
particularly competent to give religious in-
struction in their Sunday-schools and else-
where. Yet it is desired to transfer the role of
instructors to a hard-worked and underpaid
body of men who have no special sympathy
with religion, and who cannot reasonably be
expected to take the least pains in teaching it.
The State school programme is already burdened
with more subjects than the time and energy of
masters and pupils will permit to be profitably
taught ; yet we are perpetually confronted
with the strange spectacle of parent and parson
shirking their obvious duty in a matter which
is considered of vital importance to the
children, and endeavoring to transfer their
responsibility, or a portion of it, to the over-
loaded State 1
• » m m
The leader of the new crusade is Alfred
Deakin, one of the failures of Victorian
politics. Deakin is a kind of minor Glad-
stone, whose intellect is always biassed bv his
emotions, and whose excellent oratory is singu-
larly barren in cold print. In the Victorian
Assembly he recently advocated that elemen
tary unsectarian religious instruction should-
be given in State schools; and, though the
Assembly would not take the responsibility of
changing the present system, it was voted by a
majority of two to let the people express their
opinion on the matter. Possibly it was unwise
to stir up the strife anew ; or possibly it was
wise. The similar South Australian referendum
has definitely settled the question there ; and
the Victorian electors may be relied upon to
give such a vote as will end discussion for a
long time to come.
* * * *
Deakin asks for elementary unsectarian in-
struction because he knows there is little chance
of getting that, and no chance at all of getting
any more. But unsectarian religious instruc-
tion satisfies no sect, as Deakin is very well
aware. Not only the Roman Catholics, but
other denominations also, protest that, if
religion is taught at all, it should be taught
earnestly and thoroughly ; and the patter of
colorless moralities is of no use. But the “ un-
sectarian ” idea is relied upon to introduce the
thin end of the wedge. If that can be gained,
it will be easier to continue the crusade, and,
from religious nullities, to proceed to religious
dogmas. So Mr. Deakin thinks.
* * * *
Victoria is thus asked to sanction the lighting
of the old religious feud-fires, and to make the
machine of Government once more instrumen-
tal in sowing the dissensions which have made
so much misery and retarded so much progress.
It is useless to say that unsectarian teaching
cannot have this result. The Victorian re-
ligious community is divided into two great
classes—Catholic and Protestant. The Catholics
are always pleading for grants in aid of denomi-
national education ; they are always refusing
to send their children to “ godless ” schools ;
they are always, where possible, establishing
their own schools, and declaring that it is un-
just that they should be taxed to pay for the
support of the schools of the State. While no
religious instruction at all is given in
State schools this Catholic cry need not be con-
sidered. The State schools are open to all
denominations alike ; and about history and
geography, or grammar and arithmetic, no con-
flict can arise. If the Catholics refuse to per-
mit their children to attend secular schools it is
their own fault, and complaint is groundless.
But directly so-called “unsectarian ” instruc-
tion is introduced, the Catholic complaint is
justified. Deakin advocates the singing of
a hymn, the saying of a prayer, the
reading of the Bible. But the Catholics
have their own hymns, say their own
prayers, read their own Bible. . Anything
else is anathema ; and their conscientious
objection will have force. If the instruction is
not Catholic, no religious instruction will
please the Catholics ; as their clergy have de-
clared time after time. It is of no use telling
them that the teaching is unsectarian if the
lesson is read from a Bible wliic is not
their Bible, or the hymn is sung or
the prayer said in the way which is not
their way. While the State holds itself aloof
from all religious parties it is safe ; for no
party is justified in refusing to support it.
Directly it countenances any form of religion it
gives an excuse for religious strife and a justifi-
cation for civic opposition. It is not the State’s
business to teach religion ; and it is not to the
State’s interest to set its citizens by the ears. If
Deakin and his followers want religion taught,
let them teach it in their homes and in their
churches. Then they can teach a vital, not a
colorless religion ; they can teach the particular
religion which they themselves prefer ; and they
can still meet the advocates of other religions,
or of no religion, on the common ground of
citizenship upon which the State is founded.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Rich Pauper.
The demand of the Sydney morning papers for
an early “newspaper train” to the north is a
beautiful example of the rich man howling for
alms at the poor man’s door. The S.M. Herald
and the Telegraph are both rich properties;
the Herald especially is one of the richest
concerns in Australia, and its owners are among
the very richest capitalists in the Seven Pro-
vinces. The people already subsidise these
wealthy concerns to the extent of many thou-
sands a year by carrying their papers free
through the post, and by supplying them at
great loss with early special trains to convey
their goods to the country districts. Every work-
man, every tired little seamstress in the big
Sydney “ rag ’’-shops, every widow with a
small annuity or without one, is taxed to
furnish alms and charitable aid and bounties to
the rich Fairfaxes, and the Daily Telegraph
Co., and the other Sydney organs of public
opinion. And now, instead of being grateful
for what they have already received, they
demand more State bounties in the shape
of another early-morning train. The reason
why there are no large and prosperous papers
on the southern line is that the big Sydney
dailies are brought down there early, at the
country’s expense, and distributed in time to
compete with the local publications. The
reason why there are big local papers at New-
castle and Maitland is that there is no early
morning train up there to deliver the Sydney
papers (the country somehow failed to supply one
gratis, and the Sydney press was so accustomed
to being pauperised that it never dreamt
of paying for its own newspaper train as is
done in Great Britain). But now it is cordially
suggested that this lamentable oversight should
be rectified, and that the country should pay
some thousands a year more to help the Sydney
dailies in their competition with their New-
castle and Maitland contemporaries. The
business men and public of Newcastle and
other Northern towns didn’t ask for an early
morning newspaper train. A feeble bogus agi-
tation was worked up by agents of the Sydney
press —that is to say, men were asked if they
would like to have their Sydney papers earlier
in the day, and they mostly said, without
thinking much about the matter, that they
didn’t care if they did, just as they might have
said that they didn’t object to having a full
moon every night ; and this was represented as
the great voice of the North crying out for more
State bounties to be bestowed on the Herald
and Telegraph, and refusing to be comforted.
If the N.S. W. railway department has ,£IO,OOO
a year or so to throw away, the farmer—whose
turnips and potatoes are not carried free by
post—wants concessions much worse than the
already heavily-protected and heavily-bountied
Sydney papers.
The “ People’s Privileges ” in Tasmania.
The most stagnant section of Tasmania’s Par-
liament is becoming alarmed about the Consti-
toosh’n. The Tasmanian Constitoosli’n shuts
out five-sixths of the adult male population
from any power to make or unmake or control
the Upper House, and it also shuts out about
one-fourth of it from any voting power for
the Lower House. Certain amendments having
been recently proposed, the particularly slug-
gish crowd is of opinion that “ this charter of
the people’s privileges should be better safe-
guarded.” It is so much disturbed about this,
and moans so terribly about it in its sleep, that
it lately proposed to enact that the Consti-
toosh’n shall only be alterable if an absolute
majority of both Houses votes in the affirma-
tive on the second reading of any Amendment
Bill, on every separate clause of the Bill in
committee, and on the third reading. This is
such a tremendous order that if it was
enacted it isn’t likely the Tasmanian
Constitution would be altered very often.
There is a fine, large, rich, oily hypocrisy
about the idea that the community’s blessed
privilege of being mostly deprived of all
control over a good half of its own Government
wants safeguarding ; and that the sacred right
of not being allowed to alter its own Constitu-
tion, or decide on its own form ofjGovernment,
needs to be carefully preserved ; and that the
people are crying out for their inalienable birth-
right of being chained up, lest they should
break out and do themselves an injury. Every
time Toryism attempts to do something un-
usually smellful in the way of depriving the
community of its liberties, it says that it rises
in defence of the sacred privileges of the people.
Still, if the people like to agree with it as
to what is a privilege, then the idea is all
right. Everything depends on the definition of
that one word. There was a time, in the long,


long ago, during the had, old convict era, when
certain execrable tyrants, who are still at large,
deprived the people of their sacred right to be
flogged on the mere order of a magistrate, and
this inroad on their birthright has never
been repaired even to the present day.
In fact, the whole history of Australasia
has been a series of dastardly encroach-
ments of the same order, from the
date when the right to be publicly whipped
was withdrawn! from female convicts —the male
ones retained it to a much later period—down
to last year when Maoriland, by its execrable
Old Age Pension Law, deprived the aged
settler of about the last remnant of his ancient
freedom —the proud and valued right to have
nothing to eat in his old age. In view of this
general staving in of the bulwarks of the
Constitoosh’n, Tasmanian legislator Fowler
and his supporters had at least some show of
reason on their side. When so many other
rights have gone, the islander’s right to be
deprived of any vote for his own Upper House,
and to have as little vote as possible for his
House of Assembly, and his privilege of being
thrown out on his ear if he attempts to take
any serious share in making his own laws, may
also go at any moment, unless strong patriotic
fossils rally in its defence. Tasmania, thank
God, is full of just the right kind of fossil to
do the rallying.
Premier Turner's Cowardice.
Immediately the Victorian Parliament met for
the present session, Bromley, M.L.A., intro-
duced a short Bill providing that the hours of
work of the Melb. tram employees shall be
eight, and their wages not less than seven shil-
lings, per day. The second reading came on in
due course, and an irrational and spineless
House wandered vaguely round the subject and
finally put off tlie evil day till 16th Aug. The
co.’s petition against the Bill, which seems to
greatly agitate the Assembly, is a choice piece
of literature. After alluding to the in-
terest hill and sinking-fund which it has
to pay practically its rent —the co. be-
wailed the fact that it has also to keep
the lines in repair—as if any employer could
afford to let his machinery go to ruin ! It
then observed, with many painful groans, that
it had undertaken immense financial obligations
(to its own great profit), and that the proposed
extra expenditure would be forced on the com-
pany in violation of the contract it had entered
into. Which is very plausible, but, so
far as concerns the eight hours, quite un-
true. For section 62 of the original Melb.
Tramway and Omnibus Co.’s Act, 1883, 47
Vic. 765, distinctly provides, in so many words,
that “the day’s labor of any—driver or con-
ductor—shall be eicjht hours ”; and the co.
lias habitually worked such employes ten hours
per day. During the four hours’ discussion
re the second reading, although an authorita-
tive declaration was repeatedly desired, the
Victorian Govt, carefully refrained from taking
any part, and Ministers busied themselves with
deputations and in the lobbies. So the case
stands thus : A grasping corporation has re-
peatedly and persistently broken its agreement
and defied the law, and still continues to do so.
And a spineless Premier and his backboneless
Ministers stand by and look on, refusing even
to take steps to enforce the recommendations of
their own Commission, refusing to enforce the
agreement made with the co., and allowing a
private member to do the best he can to right a
grievous wrong.
The Western Bobby.
“ Wurrym ” on Westralian policemen :
Dear Bulletin.—A par. in “ Plain English” (June 10)
prompts me to ask, “Is the Queensland bobby a more
unspeakable being than he of any other province ? ” The
Vic. force is mostly Dungey in its ideas, that of Maoriland
the late commission shows is not quite all it should be,
while as for the Westralian it is absolutely the most use-
less force on the whole continent. The system of adminis-
tration in this last is unique. The Commissioner is a
‘•Colonel” by virtue of having held such rank in a volun-
teer-force, and his main idea is semi-military display.
Mounted police with lances furnish forth the body-guards
for Sir Jerrysmitii at opening- of Parliament and like cere-
monies. Said Commissioner has issued a book of police-
regulations, in which there are three distinct warnings to
the fooree to have no communication with members of
the press. Militaryism and suppression are the main
points of the administration of W.A. police-force. To the
former idea may be attributed the presence of a number
of officers and men who have had unpublishable histories
in other forces, and to the latter the gross lack of disci-
pline and esprit de corps. Take the following as an ex-
ample of how the force is administered. Constable
C. was charged by two young women with hav-
ing made improper overtures to them on dif-
ferent occasions, and with threatening them and
attempting to levy blackmail. Neither girl knew the
other, and each made her charge independently.
Each identified the man separately, though elaborate
precautions were taken to prevent collusion by the
accusers—far more elaborate than are taken for the
identification of prisoners on trial. And the result ?
Prosecuted, of course ? Lord, no ! Dismissed the force ?
Still No. What then ? Transferred to another district—
that’s all. You can guess the effect on the other mem-
bers of the force. The men worth anything are dis-
gusted, while the hard bargains are secure in their belief
that should they be found out they will be dealt with
like X. This bobby, it is averred, on another occasion
offered a woman t& to come and give evidence in support
of a charge he had made against a man of having
criminally assaulted the woman who was, as it was after-
wards learned, a personal friend of the prisoner, and who
denied every statement made by the constable. The
latter was eventually ordered by his superiors to compen-
sate the old man he had arrested by paying him £5.
There have been during the past year three robberies
under arms in broad daylight in or near W.A. towns. In
one case, four miles from Perth, a man was shot in the
shoulder. Also, there have been two murders with par-
ticularly striking characteristics (in one case the body was
scientifically dismembered and the parts thrown dovvn
different shafts ; in the other, a woman was murdered
within cooey of where several men were working).
Besides these the robbery of £9OO from the G.P.0.,
a number of garotting cases and other crimes
of violence remain “ as they were ” and ever will
bp—in the same tangled state as at the time of their com-
mittal. The three factors which militate against the
W.A. force becoming anything but an ornamental body
are: Too much Jarrah Jack, too much P.W.D.G. billy-
goat, and the employment of good men at second-class
pay, while the bounders of the force draw first-class pay.
As'to the first : the Commission dare not say nor do any-
thing without first referring to Forrest. As to the
second : well, in all Australian forces either that or “ the
influence of the elargy. ” is felt. The third factor is,
I believe, mostly the result of the great reverence felt in
W.A. for back numbers. A man who has been a stolid
ass for 25 years is considered more worthy of promotion
than the active youngster who has only been a year in
the country, and has during that time done more brilliant
work than Corporal Potgut has during his whole term of
service. Not hut what there are real good men of long
service in the force, to one of whom, a native of Westra-
lia, is due a marked improvement in the metropolitan
force of late. But one swallow doesn’t make a summer,
and the local authorities do not heed the gentle strokes
administered by the local papers. It is the nasty things
said in the East that make Jarrah Jack and his following
squirm.
Tlie Victorian Close Season for Votes.
The N.S.W. Federal vote has taught the Vic-
torian at least one lesson. It has shown him
the possibility of having elector’s rights issued
within reasonable Lime of the date of use, and
lias revealed unto him the real extent of his
own sheep-like stupidity, and abject want of
courage, and conservative fear of demanding
reasons. For years past, the Vic. voter has not
been able to obtain bis elector’s right after the
end of November in each year. Occasionally
an additional date has been gazetted as the
closing time for the issue of rights, but this
has been done with no regularity, and it lias
invariably been arranged with the air of grant-
ing some immense concession. Until recently
six months’ residence in one electorate was
necessary to qualify, and the result was
that the man who moved into a new house
in June was disqualified for all elections
for about a year ahead. Why any particular
day of the month should he fixed for ihe
shutting-down of the electoral-rights issue is
inexplicable. It does not benefit Government,
it assists in the perpetuation of no vested
interests, and it conveniences nobody but the
electoral-registrar. The time for the closing of
electoral-registers should be as close to election-
time as is consistent with the prevention of
fraud. Nine men out of ten take but little in-
terest in politics, save when a big question is
being debated or an election is at hand, and, at
the very time when they are most desirous of
talking with force, they are debarred from
exercising their votes.

A Flea for Rotten Ships.
Sydney Telegraph, which is steadily against
every kind of State enterprise, is half-inclined
to suggest that the N.S. W. coffin-ship question
should he tackled—-not by increasing and im-
proving the supervision—but by abolishing the
inefficient supervision that already exists. In
its issue of July 22 it broached t he idea in an
insinuating whisper thus :
There is nothing so dangerous as a system of State
supervision whieh is inefficiently or negligently adminis-
tered. If there were no State guarantee, members of the
public could take precautions of their own. What people
have a right to insist on is that where their own sus-
picions are disarmed by an official certificate, that certifi-
cate should be strictly correct. No Marine Board at all
would have been better than the Marine Board of the
past. Without the Board, ship-proprietors would have
taken care themselves to keep their vessels in order, and
anyone not doing so would be sure to lose his trade, and
to be promptly hanged if he were proved guilty of sacri-
ficing life by criminal negligence.
Now, why would ship-owners take more care to
keep their vessels in order when there was uo
obligation to do so, than when there is at least
a partial obligation ? There was a time when
there was no legal obligation, and the only re-
sult was that the coffin-ships of the ante-
Plimsoll days were a twenty times worse
disgrace than they are now. The public didn’t
take its own precautions ; when the public was
going anywhere by sea it couldn’t dive under
the ship, in the first place, and inspect the
plates and planking of its bottom, and crawl
into the boiler to see if it was sound. And it
didn’t hang the owner of the rotten ship ; when
the law wouldn’t go so far as to order the con-
demnation of a rotten vessel it wasn’t likely to go
so much further as to hang the owner. The
owners didn’t lose their trade ; coffin-ships
were so many that if the public had withdrawn
its trade from them it would have had no trade
left. At present land-boilers are free from
State interference, but their owners don’t,
therefore, take care to keep them in order, nor
does the public take precautions of its own,
nor is the owner of the rotten boiler hanged.
The Telegraph’s whispered plea for no inter-
ference at all with the coffin-ship in the future,
because it hasn’t been interfered with suffi-
ciently in the past, is simply the theory that
because the police haven’t managed to wholly
suppress murder and burglary therefore the
police should be abolished, and murder and
burglary should be left to suppress themselves.
Making' a Madman.
A verdict of Nob Guilty in the second trial of
Emery Medor for murdering one Stevens, at
the Eastern Market, Melbourne, may be taken
to express a fairly popular opinion. The ques-
tion as to whether the deceased Stevens was,
or wasn’t, chiefly responsible for the murderer’s
burst of fury had nothing to do with the case.
At any rate, he knew that he and his wife
were chief objects of the goaded man’s sus-
picion ; but with heartless persistence Stevens
continued to take part—probably a leading
part —in the dangerous game of making Medor
mad, by means of practical jokes. Medor,
coming back to his shop after one more visit lo
an adjacent pub., found a bunch of carrots
hanging from the shop-door. Sundry grinning
traders in the Market were waiting to see how
the crank would act under the influence of this
new jest. He took hold of a gun and shot
Mrs. Stevens, the wound, as it happened, not
being serious. Stevens rushed to avenge his
wife, and was promptly slaughtered by the foe.
Another man, well disposed towards Medor,
tried to disarm him and bring him to reason,
and this friendly interloper lost an eye in the
struggle. And then Medor, no longer mad,
quietly went to prison, where he was true to
himself, extenuating nothing, nor feigning to
be in any way changed from the Medor who
had practised as a phrenologist, astrologist, and
general “professor” in the Eastern Market.
The trial of this nob-very penitent sinner for
wilful murder resolved itself into a conflict of
medical evidence. Dr. Shields, the Melb. Gaol
practitioner, and other self-styled specialists
appearing on behalf of the Crown, testified that
Medor was “legally sane” when he killed
Stevens ; medical witnesses for the defence
held the opinion that, he was suffering from
“impulsive mania” at the time, and Judge
Williams asked the jury to consider whether
tlie persistent “baiting” of this “excitable,
irritable man ” might have wrought him up to
such a pitch of rage as to make him unaccount-
able for his actions? The jury found that
Medor actually was, in a reasonable sense of
the term, mad when he wounded Mrs. Stevens
and killed her husband. It now remains for
the Government to say what shall be done with
the murderer, who is normally a harmless, in-
telligent crank. Neither a criminal, nor a
lunatic, is he entitled to go forth and face the
world again as a salutary warning against
anger, hatred, malice and all uncharitableness?
Some Language from Riverina.
The petition of a number of Western Riverina
residents to the Vie. Government, asking for
railway communication with Melbourne, em-
bodies a loud complaint against the whole
accursed system of provincial railway manage-
ment. These people want a bridge at Cobram
to connect with the nearest Vic. railway, but
the Reid Government has flatly refused either
to join wdth Victoria in building it, or to let
Victoria build it at its own expense, lest it
should divert trade to Melbourne. Also, the
N.S.W. Government refuses to connect its
railways hereabouts with the Victorian lines.
It builds plenty of railways to Sydney, and
gives exceedingly cheap fares, and asks the
Riverina people to be grateful. And they are
beastly ungrateful instead. They reply that
they don’t want to waste time travelling 450
miles to Sydney when they can do their busi-
ness in Melbourne, only 180 miles away ; and
that the ruinously cheap fare for the long dis-
tance to Sydney is higher, after all, than the
ordinary fare would be for the short run to
Melbourne. Also, they want to know why,
seeing that railways around Sydney are built
so as to best suit the convenience of the people,
their railways should be built to give them the
largest amount of inconvenience, simply be-
cause Sydney profits thereby. And they ask
disrespectfully how the Parramatta people
would enjoy it if they were given greatly
reduced fares to Brisbane, bur were denied any
railway communication whatsoever with Syd-
ney.
Where Doctors Differ.
Next election of the honorary medical and
surgical staff for the Women’s Hospital, Mel-
bourne, promises to produce a record in the
game of roll-stuffing. From all parts of this
tired continent the hospital committee has
lately been getting hard-cash evidences of a
lively interest in the charitable institution
where unmarried mothers are religiously
ticketed. The great heart of Australia seems
stirred by a desire to do good by stealth, as it
were, for most of the far-off people who send
along twenty shillings or a guinea are so vague
as regards their addresses that they amount to
anonymous subscribers. All they ask is to be
placed on the roll, with the right to vote by
proxy for one or other medical gentleman in
September. In short, certain candidates for
staff appointments, likewise the friends and
admirers of certain candidates, are spending
money to secure their election, and unless the
committee take measures (which, seemingly,
they cannot take yet awhile) to defeat these
enthusiastic schemers the coveted billets might
almost as well be put up to auction. The humor
of the situation doesn’t end here. Among the
medical men who want the billets, but are not
prepared to pay for them, there is a bitter out-
cry against the alleged iniquity of the roll-
stuffing system. These unfortunates contend
that only “genuine” subscribers should have
votes, or, better still, that the election of the
hospital staff should rest with a committee of
“experts,” as though the aforesaid “genuine ”
subs, or “ experts ” could be depended upon to
give professional Merit the preference without
the least regard for private personal claims
upon their votes and interest. The people who
yell the loudest against roll-stuffing candidates
have candidates of their own whom they hope
to get elected on the “merit ” ticket. What is
the gauge of merit among rival medical practi-
tioners, anyway ? The candidates are, all of
them, fully qualified to kill and cure, and the
fact that they are all applying for the privilege
of practising gratis at a hospital puts them all
on the same level of professional energy and
ambition. Some of the least notorious of them
are possibly the brainiest and the most likely
to give satisfaction if they get the chance, and
these may be the chief roll-stuffers for all that
anybody can swear to the contrary. Medical
reputation is very much a matter of luck and
impudence. Reputatiomranks as merit, though
it is not necessarily more meritorious than
cash, which latter benefits the hospital. Then
why not put the billets up to auction?
The Transvaal Syndicates.
Interesting extracts from a letter written by
an English resident in the Transvaal, and re-
cently published in the Sydney press :
The real grievance which is agitating the minds of the
capitalists who rule the subsidised papers is that they are
not at present able to exercise the all-powerful influence
which they did over the diamond-mining industry in
Giiquaiand West. With the amalgamation of the four
mines, Kimberley, Dutoit’s Pass, Bulfontien, and Old De
Beers, the diamond-mining industry practically became a
close monopoly, to the ruination of local trade. Dia-
monds went up from 10s. per carat to 405., but several
thousand miners were thrown out of employment, and
hundreds of tradespeople had to give up business. The
tyrannical influence of the capitalists is painfully appar-
ent even under present conditions. Large groups of
mines are controlled by the directors of consolidated com-
panies, whose sole aim is big dividends. If a workman
should dare to agitate for any alteration in the reguia-
tions-of the companies, he might as well clear out' of the
mining district at once, for his name would lie posted to
all the mines, and his chance of obtaining a job anywhere
would be out of the question.
The habitations which the workpeople have to live in
are of the most wretched description, no such thing as a
fireplace in any of the single men’s quarters, and the
climate of the Transvaal is very different from that of
Australia. The nights are bitterly cold, and the days
warm. In many of the outlying districts as many as four
men are crowded into rooms 12ft. square, and very few
mines are provided with any such thing as bat hrooms f.or
the men to wash themselves.
Such is the rapacity of many of the mining companies
that the men are compelled to board at certain hoarding-
houses belonging to the companies, and the rent of these
houses is regulated by the number of men boarding at-
them.
The one sole aim is dividends. The workmen's claim
to compensation for accidents is provided for by a com-
pulsory insurance, deducted front their wages. The board
ing-houses at which the men have to take their meals are
owned by the companies, and the profits on the transac-
tion divided between the boarding-house-keeper and the
company.
The mining laws of the Republic are the most
liberal in the world, but some of the provisions of the
gold law do not suit the companies. The extent of the
claims in the South African Republio will, I think, oom-
pare favorably with Australia, and is largely in favor of
the companies here, the extent of a quartz claim being
150 ft. along the reef and 400 ft. wide. The claims are not
allowed by law to be enclosed. This provision is very
much disapproved of by the companies, for, although
they ortlv pay 10s. per month claim license, they wish
to treat ‘the land as private property, and deter any-
one from walking over it. Another great grievance
is the Bewaarplaatsen, or undermining rights,
which prohibit the companies from excavating
under towns, gardens, &c., because, as in many
of the coal districts in England where the coal is mined
from under villages, the houses are apt to be wrecked,
and it is considered here a great hardship that the com-
panies mining under such towns should be liable for com-
pensation. in fact, the gold-mining companies desire to
have the gold and the country as well, so'that they may
be the paramount power, rule the roost, and generally
make every other interest subservient to the company
interest ; and they are doing their level best to gain their
ends. The franchise question is simply a peg to hang
their grievances on. In fact, a well-known sporting man
offered the other day to bet a thousand pounds to a jam-
tart that 90 per cent, of the miners would prefer a fire in
their rooms to 40 votes, and it is believed that there is a
great element of truth in the idea.
Poor old Bryan O’Loghlen, the exploded legal
genius of the Patterson Ministry, was moved
to protest in Vic. Assembly, the other day,
against the so-called filching of their security
from the British bondholders by the proposed
handing over of the customs to the Pederal
Govt. The poor old gentleman was terribly
indignant at this act of wholesale robbery, and
declared that “for this reason it was impossible
the (Commonwealth) Bill could pass and the
Qovt. should consider whether there should nob
be another Convention.” The ancient’s painful
concern for the public creditor is a touching
circumstance, and stands in fine contrast with
his exceeding lack of regard for certain private
creditors—those of the Mercantile Bank, to
wit. And, in any case, the British money-
lender is not in need of any sympathy. Thd
customs revenue isn’t one pennyworth of
security to the British money-lender; he hasn’t
a lien on a single copper of it. His only
security is the promise of the Australian people
that they will pay up, and Federation doesn’t
affect that. Still, if the Victorian Tories really
believe the bunkum they preach, they will, of
course, fight their hardest against the reduction
of the income-tax. That also diminishes the
security of the British creditor.

Westralia’s Attorney-General (Penneeather)
is of opinion that “ any person not a lunatic,
and proposing to settle in W.A., w’ould oppose
Federation on the terms laid down in the ConG
monwealth Bill.” This paper’s idea is that any
agricultural settler in W.A., not being a howl-
ing lunatic, would fight for the Commonwealth
Bill to the last gasp. Westralia has on thet
S.W. a strip of fertile land larger than all the
good arable county of either S.A., Victoria,
Tasmania, or N.S.W. It has, on that bit of
country, the most reliable rainfall in Australia.
It has cheaper land than any other province, r
because it is the least thickly populated. And
therefore it has a chance, when Federation
makes the other provinces take off their border-
duties, to become the granary of Australia. If
the present rate of progress'keeps up, W.A., in
two years from now, will be hunting for mar-
kets for its surplus grain. Eastern and Central
Australia, in their periodical droughts, will be
its market—if W.A. federates, and thus gets
inside the Federal tariff-line, instead of being
a lonesome outcast on the wrong side of the
tariff-border.
The Truck system is a great and weighty curse
on Labor in Westralia, and John Forrest’s'
half-hearted promise to deal wdth it comes very
late in the day. On many of the isolated saw-
mills and timber syndicate estates the workmen
—especially the married ones—are almost as
absolute slaves as any nigger of ye olden tyme
on an Arkansas plantation. As a rule, the
workman has no money when a job at
one of these places, so he doesri’b ask too many
questions. And he hardly ever by any chance
gets any money after he goes there. The only
store where he can buy anything is the boss’s
own store—-no other dealer is allowed on the
estate if.the shindykit can help it. If the boss’s
store doesn’t happen to have what the
wants he must go without it; he has no busi-
ness to spend his money on things the boss
hasn’t gob and thereby diminish his power of
paying for the things the boss has got. The
prices are anything the boss likes to fix. They
are generally arranged so as to make it pretty
sure that food, clothes, tobacco, &c., together,
with house rent—the boss supplies the house and
fixes the rent —will absorb all the employe’s.
wages and, perhaps, leave a steadily-growing
debit against him. Therefore the man can’t
leave at awkward times—he has no money to
leave with ; and the growing debt and conse-‘
quent liability to have his supplies stopped
keep him humble and quite amenable to,
reason. Besides, if he does leave lie can
always be hauled before the boss as a J.P. for
vagrancy or something—after a lengthy spell
under the Truck system he can’t well get away
except as a vagrant. The “tommy-shop,”
which the English factory-owner used to run
in the ’3o’s and ’4o’s and ’so’s, didn’t die out.
It moved to Westralia and has remained there
ever since.

lished annual report he sets official tradition at
defiance and castigates the Fatman sweater
with a courage marvellous in a civil servant.
He also confesses his inability to deal with the
Chinese, who are such consummate liars, and are
so much alike in name and appearance that the
Ait Cow fined £5 is never discoverable to serve
his sentence in default. The report deals
trenchantly also with the people who snivel
out “ Charity ” as an excuse for paying.star-
vation wages, and exposes the dodge by which
many dressmaking establishments get back as
“ premium ” the 2s. fid. a week they are com-
pelled to pay apprentices. Dressmaking “ ap-
prentices,” it is pointed out, are, in fact, ' not.
apprentices. They are merely employed to do
the mechanical work of the shop at from
nothing to 2s. 6d.-a week. They are not. in’-
dffnturerl ; and are mostly “ fired ” without any
real knowledge of dressmaking. Mr. Ouo is
indeed a rare bird amongst Government officials,
knowing his subject perfectly, and having
the pluck to make use of his knowledge in the
teeth of the powerful sweating vested in-
terests. One result of his interference is that
