A drama called “Our Native Home,”
which chanced, or happened, or blew in through
the door being left open, or otherwise transpired
at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday, has at least one
feature about which it is possible to speak defi-
nitely. It is the very worst affair which the
Holloway management has produced up to date.
There are very few things which a drama shouldn’t
be that it isn’t, and it appears really proud of
being all these things. In the main it is a par-
ticularly childish essay on that good old stage in-
stitution—the Incriminating Document. The long,
honorable man with the wonderful hat had copied
someone else’s name in an idle moment, and,
though he hadn’t uttered the forgery, or made
money out of it, and there was no evidence to
show who wrote it, it is understood that he is
thereby completely in the power of the other long
man with the tall boots. The latter drives him to
terrible crimes as the price of his silence, though
there is nothing much to he silent about. They
both concoct a charge against a third man,
vet the latter, with a warrant out against
him, wanders about publicly and takes no
notice of the warrant. One wild character in the
story attempts to butcher a lady at a ball. The
ball in general gathers round, but doesn’t inter-
fere, though he is waving a long knife above the
V-shaped bosom of his victim ; only one person
remonstrates with him, and seems to think it
would be better to take the lady into the yard
and kill her there. Nobody even makes a decent
pretence of being surprised; possibly in that
locality it is common to cut the throat of one’s
partner at a ball when she has been used up for
dancing purposes, and put the body in the cloak-
room, and then select another girl. The hero in
the “Native Home” flies to avoid arrest on a
false charge, but when he comes back nobody
thinks of arresting him, so lie is wrecked, and
goes through great anguish in top boots, all to no
purpose. And the latitude which the law gives to
the bad man is something amazing. Even after
he has shot his son in the presence of a witness,
and embezzled A‘50,000, and chased a blind girl
overboard, the curtain goes up again to find him
being tried for nothing worse than abduction—
with his belltopper on the edge of the dock beside
him.
* * * *
_ There are long scenes of pitiable “humor”
in the Lyceum drama, wherein a person concealed
under a table groans at night to the great discom-
fiture of two other funny men; and a spectral
hand will insist on coming out of nowhere and
seizing the policeman’s beer ; and the witnesses,at
the trial can’t be restrained from bursting out of
the witness-box and trying to personally batter
and stoush the defendant in the dock. It is
difficult to decide whether these cheerful parts 'of
the drama or the sad parts are the sadder. The
jokes are old and primitive—they mostly belong
to that very far back period when' man first began
to sleep on his ear and thereby started it on the
downward path to its present crumpled condition.
Also the plot is of the simple order that the small,
inky boy composes on his slate when the master
isn’t looking, and is caned for afterwards. Mr.
Stanford toils in a hard voice air.id a great waste
of villainy, but he is the kind of villain who spends
years of tremendous sagacity in building up
schemes that go to pieces as soon as the fat
sailor threatens to hit him on the “jore. ”
H. O. Willard is a long, weak, unconvincing per-
son with long hair. Miss Wiuton, also
Messrs. Hawthorne, Saunders and Sweeney, and
the big dredge, and Mr. O’Neill, and the 'really
splendid scenery deserve better luck than they
get. The big dredge faces the situation and
dredges manfully, but Mr. Saunders isn’t at his
best. The painful slush of his dialogue most of
the loud and noble slush falls to his share—
probably gets on his nerves.

At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) a few nights of
the “ Belle of New York ” are to fill in the week
till the “ King’s Musketeer ” gets over from Mel-
bourne. Oscar Girard has dropped out of his old
character of Ichabod Bronson, and his part has
been filled for some time by- Albert Whelan. The
Australian Ichabod has given}every satisfaction ;
he is as tall as his predecessor, and as thin, and
the uniform which drove the dogs mad in Cohoes
fits him to a nicety. Boor Girard, who was in
lamentably bad health when he arriyecl here, and
has grown no bettor since then—he suffers from that
terrible disease, laryngeal tuberculosis —was last
heard of in hospital at Adelaide, sit is uncertain
whether he will ever appear on the stage again, and
the proceeds of the matinee which was announced for
yesterday (Wednesday) are intended for his bene-
fit. A man with strength and grit enough to do
the joyous foolery of Ichabod Bronson under sue!)
heavy disadvantages deserves at least six benefits.
* * * *
The “King’s Musketeer,” which, as
obscurely hinted, is due at Her Majesty’s on
Saturday, brings back a lot of old favorites.
Misses Henrietta Watson, Emilie Hughes, and
Nellie Mortyne (the Roman lady who urged
Nero to fry more Christians) are in the cast;
likewise Harry Plimmer, Cecil Ward, Walter
Bentley, Hawtrey (much-beloved as Sapt in the
“Prisoner of Zenda”), George Majeroni, Cyril
Keightley, Lissant, and Atholwood (the noble
Belgian drunk in “ A Royal Divorce ”), are all on
the bill. If all this talent doesn’t make the
“Musketeer” boom, he must be a very flat
musketeer indeed.
* * # *
The humble “tanner” which the writer
offered last week to any person who would tell
him whether he really liked or didn’t like the
“ Physician ” at Sydney Royal has been claimed
by a number of poets, artists, and other irrespon-
sible parties, but in the absence of satisfactory
information he has declined to pay over the
money. On further consideration, he inclines to
think he does like the “Physician.” It is built
out of rather middle-aged materials, but they are
well handled, and the fact that Jones contrives to
give a powerful lesson on the ’orrois of Drink
without presenting the old, crude picture of a
drunkard laying out the family infant with the
gin-bottle is something in his favor. In fact, he
leaves out all the coarse, red, staring horrors of the
regulation Drink drama. There are seven of these :
The coster blackening his missus’s eye on the
Mile-End Road; the blighted home in an attic
with the faithful wife moaning by the cold ashes
and listening for two dissipated hob nails on the
stair: the aged, bloated grandmother calling
for rum, with her hair down, in a cellar;
the once proud father sullenly picking snakes
out of his boots ; the small boy outside the gin-
palace calling, in a cracked voice, for his parent to
come home'; the flattened-out baby aforesaid ; and
the suicide. And Jones judiciously omits them
all. His drunkard is never seen drunk once, and
Lovell’s picture of him is merely a clever study in
nervous wreckage. Brough’s clergyman grows on
the fancy of the audience, too, as time goes on.
He i 3 a pleasant, beaming old man, who tries hard
to keep his eye on the Heavenly home and off the
mundane collection-plate, and his cheerful coun-
tenance rises like a full moon behind the nervous
wreck, and the strong, silent physician, and the
fashion-plate lady, who feels that she is growing
old and wants the physician to marry her, and
give her poison as soon as her face wrinkles and
her back-hair grows thin. The “Physician” is a
mass of able and convincing gloom alleviated by
Brough’s clergyman, and there is just enough
clergyman in it to redeem it from absolute
tragedy.

* * « *
The “Kelly Gang,” at Sydney Opera
House, was responsible for the largest crowd that
ever crammed itself into that ancient playhouse.
Every seat was occupied, and the street was
blocked. Act I. shows a clearing in a gum forest,
and beyond loom up the everlasting hills. The
gang is listening to Ned complaining of how the
police give him no opportunity to make a decent
living at cattle-duffing, when Kate Kelly brings
the information that the force is coming with a
warrant and sortie shooting irons ; also, that Con-
stable Fitzpatrick has “wronged” her. Ned
swears to have ber-lood. Charles Field makes a
regular Dead wood Dick sort of young man of Ned,
and hauls the last two words of every sentence out
of his'throat with both hands. Then the Kellys
depart to secure the last mob of stolen cattle,
and Steve Hart stays behind to hug his Kate.
The police arrive in their own protracted way, and
there is much melodramatic talk from Ned, and
soihe shooting, and when the smoke clears away it
is seen that Sergeant Kennedy is wounded and
Fitzpatrick, the man who is always hugging Kate,
is a prisoner. Fitz gets away, and while the
others chase him snaky-faced Dan shoots the
wounded sergeant dead. Act 2 shows how one
Aaron Sherritt tries to earn an honest £lOOO by
giving information to the police. Constables
Maloney and Murphy and Miss Melpomene Prim,
who appear about here, are just O’Hara, M’Ginnis
and the ancient maiden lady in “ Robbery Under
Arms,” but all the same they are the life of the
show, and but for them the long succession of dreary
butchery is calculated to give a black snake the hor-
rors. Sherritt is dug out of his hut by the Gang, and
there is more ber-lud. As Sherritt, Walter Vin-
cent raves, and begs for life, and dies howling,
with a realistic frenzy that is horrible. Act IV.
finds the Gang cooped up in the hotel at Glen-
rowan, with Moloney and Murphy prisoners ; and
intermittent shooting gives a fine martial tone to
the piece. Kate gets in with some food. A red
glare shows that the police have set fire to the
pub. Hart and Byrne don the constables’ clothes
and escape. Dan is shot amid howls of execra-
tion. Murphy and Moloney escape in sacks, and
find a bottle of beer they think, but whisky they
hope, which turns out to be kerosene. The hotel
is burning, the police are looking on, and a grey-
haired priest stands for twenty minutes and reviles
them for setting fire to a house containing a help-
less cripple, and declares that he is going to save
a child, also that his life is his Master’s—and all
this time the child is burning. Then he rushes in
and brings out the child with his hair nicely curled.
Ned stalks on in armor and tells how he is a fear-
less outlaw and will die as sieh. And then he goes
down smash, and heaves himself up and fires off
cheap moralities, and loud copy-book trivialities,
and dies. The “ Kelly Gang ” is the most groggy
piece of literature the writer has seen for a long
time, and either it was stolen wholesale from
“Robbery Under Arms,” or vice versa , or they
were both stolen horribly from something else.

#* * *
Rickards, the perennial “goer,” starts
shortly on another business expedition through
America and England, thence to Paris, Vienna
and Berlin, engaging all sorts of “ variety ” per-
formers, for near or distant dates, as he moves
along. He expects to get back in four months,
although there are some legal matters to discuss in
London re the breakage of certain engagements.
Marie Loftus (the mother of fair Cissie), Marie
Lloyd, and George Robey have forgotten to eross
the sea as per contracts duly signed and delivered,
and for his own protection in the future Rickards
must, one way or other, deal with the defaulters.
A manager here needs to stand upon his penalty
rights when far-away artists have “slipped him
up,” seeing that he stood to be made a convenience
of—if they had chanced to fall sick and need a nice
long sea voyage at the time fixed for their depar-
ture to Australia. They are certain to come and
put themselves on his salary list if their health is
poor, whereas, in some cases, they don't hesitate to
disappoint him if they are doing well. Maybe the
two Maries will get their contracts reconstructed,
whilst Robey elects to pay the penalty and look
pleasant. He was a bachelor, and a rising young
man in the profession when Rickards shrewdly
snapped him up for an engagement some four
years ago. In the meantime Robey has risen to a
“star” position, also he has married a wife (Ethel
Haydon), and he has no use for the hit of parch-
ment which he signed ere his present circumstances
arose.
** * *
The “ New Discovery ” Concerb came off
at Melb. Town Hall last Saturday with a lot of
carefully prepared enthusiasm. Miss Lili Sharp
and Miss Hansen-Knarhoi were the Bendigonians
of the hour, and two large bodies of barrackers
came to boost them on their way. Also, a third
lady, Miss Julie Layton, from Adelaide—a “con-
tralto, ” possessing an inferior voice and an awful
capacity for prolonging her contributions—got a
boisterous encore from some relentless admirers at
the far end of the building. The writer pauses
here to award Miss Layton the whole beer for ill-
deserved applause. She was the most wearisome
success he has ever met. Miss Sharp and
Miss Hansen - Knarhoi are infant prodigies
whose united ages amount to between 45 and
50 years, or thereabouts. Neither damsel is
much less juvenile than the other on her platform
appearance. Miss H.-K. played the violin, and did
it very well sometimes ; also, she wore a long dress
and bore herself like one who suspects that she is
overrated in the advt. but doesn’t seriously object
to a furore. The furore in her ease was not extra-
vagant, the front-seated gentleman who had hurled
“ Bravo, Bendigo ! ” at Miss Sharp being only
mildly excited over the violinist’s first solo. Evi-
dently the stronger body of barrackers and the
bulk of the audience were resolved to idolise the
new soorano in the preposterous frock. Miss Lili
Sharp's outer garment, which came nearly down
to the ground, spoke eloquently of her vocal pre-
tensions. It was very like the frock of Amy
Castles, only longer, and it didn’t seem out of
keeping with the wearer’s smiles. Miss Sharp has
two smiles—one to come on and go off with, and
another to rest upon whilst she warbles. The
“reception” smile terminates in a droop of the
eyelids and a bashful bite of her nether lip; the
singing smile is an indication of joyful self-
approval, lighting up Miss Sharp’s countenance
without the least regard for the business in
hand. It was there when she sang “ Hear,
Ye, Israel,” “Home, Sweet Home,” and other
things. The audience—or some of it—demanded
double encores from Miss Sharpe, and yet the
same brutal Bulletin which foretold a gigantic
boom for Amy Castles does not predict a credulous
glorification of Miss Sharp. As for her singing—
well, Miss Sharp has a nondescript voice with
some strong, high soprano notes which impress
the hearer when they first come popping out, but
soon lose their effect. The attention wanders
from the vocalist before she has left the platform.
There is positively nothing in Miss Sharp’s voice
or singing to produce excitement among people
who take her on her merits and demerits, and care
nothing about Bendigo in the matter. The most
pleasing feature of this concert on Saturday night
was the genuine, all-round demonstration earned by
Miss Elsie Hall. Her one pianoforte solo made the
rest of the show look poor. Marshall Hall last
week championed the cause of his pale, unassum-
ing namesake in a letter so “nice’’and tactful
that it would be sacrilege to take out the senti-
ments and wrap them in more flippant phrases.
Wrote Marshall Hall: “ There is in our midst a
fully-fledged Australian artist who has gone
through a European training, who has made the
most of it, and whom, however, in running after
‘ new, untried gods, ’ we have somewhat neglected
to honor in a sufficiently marked manner. I
allude to the pianist, Miss Elsie Hall, an artist
whose playing has a feminine charm, grace, and
refinement, such as it is rarely given us to hear.”
An orchestral concert, for the benefit of the win-
some lady, is to happen, under Marshall Hall’s
direction, on Aug. 17, and he appeals to all boom-
sters of doubtful musical “ talent ” to seize this
“ opportunity which will be afforded of recognising
not merely a prospective artist, but one who is
now, and here, able to actually accomplish some-
thing truly ‘admirable.’”
The Castles boom, which started with top-
note predictions of a speedy European fame for
the songstress, threatens to peter out in a very
minor key. At Bendigo Masonic Hall last week,
the local operatic society gave a farewell social to
their priina donna} Miss Castles. The vice-presi-
dent, having duly expressed the society’s sense of
reflected glory in the damsel’s Melbourne and
Sydney profits, “wished her God-speed, pros-
perity, and a safe return koine.” In response,
Miss Castles sang Tosti’s “Good-bye.”
Adelaide is the Cinderella city. Cinque-
valli is not to visit the pious town, and Dante, will
“probably” play for three nights. And they
would be great successes in Adelaide on their
reputations alone. _ Bland Holt has not visited
Adelaide for over six years.
At Poverty Point.
The Melbourne members of Coolsrardie Exhibition
orchestra are back airaiu. distrusted with the ennui and
drowsiness of the city, which was but yesterday such a
swarming hive. The orchestra comprised 18 performers,
with old friend Otto Linden as conductor. Its en<ra''e-
ment was for 12 weeks- two performances daily and
rehearsal every morning. The first week the show did
pretty fair business, but afterwards the performers played
from overture to finish to an average audience of five
somnolent individuals—generally the same five every
day. The veteran Joubert slumbered peacefully at the
door, only rousing up on Saturdays, when a few casual
visitors would drop in. One Saturday, the committee
made a spasmodic effort to liven things a bit, and engaged
the local darkies to hold a corroboree. The niggers howled
and jabbered, and hit kerosene-tins in a praisewoithy
manner, but nobody had enough energy to go in anil
inspect the affair, and the result was a dismal failure.
After that, the management let things diift, and the
whole concern sank into torpor, and settled down dog-
gedly to await the end. As soon as the corpse had been
interred, the members of I he orchestra formed themselves
into a concert co., and gave four concerts >n. Kalgoorlie
with gratifying results.
“Buskin": Re a recent illuminative par. The “local
author,” so dubbed (and ostracised) by Manager William-
son on a memorable occasion, is sometimes at more pains
to annotate bis scrip with stage-directions and correct
“ hi/..” than is (he imported ditto. In both Ihe Sydney
“ Musketeers ” versions pounce-boxes were specially pro-
cured. The blotting-paper incident at Melb. Princess’s
augurs an absence of specification in the “imported”
scrip, as well as managerial absence of mind. Writer
once read a direction in “ The Queen’s Favourite ” (played
iu Australia by Genbvieve Ward and Vernon), “ Puts
letter in envelopewhich sounds all right, only they
didn't use envelopes in Marlborough's day. Even an
author of Sardou’s rank is no exception. In his own
scene-description to first act of “ La Patrie” (lately seen
in Sydney under another name), he directs, “ Mountains
in background.” As the locale is Brussels, this is more
picturesque than accurate, even hills being a rarity there-
abouts. A character iu a Iticbelieu-period costume-play,

One of the calmest and funniest things yet printed is
the Sydney Freeman's Journal statement: “It is not
going too far to say that but for the Freeman s critic
neither Miss Ada Crossley nor Miss Florence Schmidt
would be now in Europe.”
The “ Trelawney ” costuming suggests an analogy be-
tween women’s dress and women’s morals, with the' con-
clusion that the slimmer the trucking the friskier the
wearer both in ideas and conduct. In the beginning of
this century, when women moved as if competing in a
sack-race, certain feminine errors were viewed with
leniency—witness the escapades of Napoleon’s Josephine,
and the fact that Lady Hamilton's name was engraved
with Nelson’s on the silverware presented to them by the
people of London. As the skirts amplified, the ideas
contracted, and it was not till women began to have legs
again that they likewise started to entertain ideas. One
thing is certain : the crinoline period in England was co-
incident with the most awful state of colorless conven-
tional morality that that unfortunate, devil-forsaken
country has ever known.
“0.C.” : Notice that Holloway’s new leading lad v. Miss
Dora YVinton, has the gift of weeping naturally. Conse-
quently, it is all the more pity to see her yield to'the
stage convention which prompts the heroine to go into
black before the innocent husband is executed, as if she
had determined to get her mourning in good time, or
wanted to cheer him up by showing him how she would
look as a widow. Also, why does Miss Gresham—like
many other had stage-women—always repent in a dress
without a neck-hand ? The dress was presumably bought
in better days when she didn’t mean to become poor and
homelpssaud repentant. It had a neck-hand on it then-
all dresses have, originally, unless you order one specially
to repent in. Does the had woman, when site feels re-
pentance coming on, sit down by the wayside, and take
her dress off, and rip awa.v the neck-hand ?

teer":—“Mr. Cecil Ward, as D’Artagnan, surprised

pourtrayal of a brainless aristocrat.” Evidently it was
the ’Erald man himself who thought of the actor as the
rickety earl in “ The Amazons,” or the idiot with crim-
son socks in one of the bygone farces produced tty B. and
8., and couldn’t identify him with any other kind of part.
Yet it was not so long ago that Cecil Ward played the
Little Minister. In the old days he played a large assort-
ment of heroes : —Tom Jones, Joseph Anrlr ws, Ned An-
nesley, the lover of Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith, and various
“leading juvenile” characters of more commonplace
types. He made his Australian dfehut as a gallant colonel
in “ Held by the Enemy." The ’Erald paragraph, for
all its indiscreet display of ignorance, was quite a fair
sample of the praise which pleases an actor and is care-
fully preserved in his Family Bible hook of press-notices.
The late Harry Towie (lately known as W. H. Harrison)
was the original Ralph Raekstraw (“Pinafore”) in
Australia. Under his direction that opera was produced
for the first, time in this hemisphere, in ’7O, at Christ-
church, M.L., by a semi-amateur co. During the per-
formance the comedian Walter Hill was taken ill and
carried home, where in a few minutes he expired—still in
the dress and make-up of Dick Deade.ve. The Little
Buttercup of the cast was the once well-known operatic
artist Signora Venosta.
The corrohboree given at Rockhampton by the Dawson
blacks in honor of the English footballers was a miserable
fiasco. Ground in state of semi-darkness: only a few
fires scattered about, instead of at least a couple of dozen,
and a big crowd in possession of the place allotted for the
advertised “wild weird dance.” The 6000 persons
present (including many from Townsville, Charters
Towers, &c.) were utterly disgusted at the painful
exhibition.

The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney an-
nounces the best half-year that it has reported for a
number of years, the profits for the six months to 30th
June, 1899'being £42,339. The profits for the last few
years have been as follows :
December, 1893 £67,470 December, 1896 .. £40,067
June, 1894 .. .. 25,560 June, 1897 .. .. 40,119
December, 1894 .. 36,599 December, 1597 .. 40,150
June, 1895 .. .. 38,037 June, 1898 .. .. 40,410
December, 1595 .. 39,474 December, 1898 40,864
June, 1896 .. .. 40,303 June, 1899 .. .. 42,339
Dividend at 8 per cent, per annum (£40,000) leaves £2339
to add to previous balance, which makes £19,021 at credit
of profit and loss. The profits are distinctly better than
at any previous date since tbe grievous slump of June,
1894, being equal to £4 4s. 3d. per cent, per annum on
shareholders’ funds (capital £1,000,000, reserve - fund
£1,010,0.10). The Commercial Banking Co. has £10,267,000
of deposits. Its entire liabilities to the public are
£11,440,000. It has £2,400,000 in cash, £1,280,000 in
State securities, and ils total liquid assets (coin in band,
money in transit, Queensland Government notes, State
securities, &c.) are £4,350,900. Altogether, it makes a
fine showing, and is an institution that may well feel
proud and satisfied about itself.
» * * *
The City Bank of Sydney again just shows
enough profit to pay its 4 per cent, dividend (£8,000) and
enough over to save appearances, the declared profits for
the half-year ended 30th June, 1899, being £8,009. There-
fore it is reasonable to assume, according to a statement
made some time ago by one of the directors, that the
balance of the earnings (amount unknown) is still being
applied to gradually write off the amount (also unknown)
by which the assets have depreciated, and that when this
process has been finished (which, at the present rate,
whatever it is, and provided new losses don't arise, will
be in an unspecified period) the visible profits are likely
to be increased to a large, or small, or middle-sized extent.
The declared profits in recent years have been these :
June, 1890 .. .. £8,198 June, 1898 .. .. £8,273
December, 1896 .. 8,071 December, 1893 .. 8,U53
June, 1897 .. .. 8,048 June, 1899 .. .. 8,069
December, 1897 .. 8,395
Said visible profits are equal to £3 4s. 6d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds, and after paying the
usual 4 per cent, dividend (£8000) the balance (£69)
raises the sum at credit of profit and loss to £9099.
During the half-year deposits have decreased
from £1,110,671 to t 1,910,715, which is a rather heavy
decline. So far as Tub Bulletin can trace, this seems to
be the lowest figure the City Bank’s deposit business has
reached for at least a dozen years. The cash reserve has
dropped by £104,000 —from £377,843 to £273,758, but the
City Bank has now £25,000 invested in N.S.W. State
securities, which is a new and altogether commendable
departure; and allowing for this the drop in the cash
reserve is £79,000. As usual, there is very little informa-
tion in the balance-sheet. The policy of keeping dark
about the amount of depreciation to be provided for is
apparently not doing the City Bank much good. Its shares
still sell at such a low figure that investors in the new
issue, at last week’s prices, get £8 13s. per cent, per
annum for their money, and investors in tbe old
issue, £7 12s. 6d.—in other words, they apparently
stand lowest among the shares of the dividend-paying
banks of Australia in public estimation. Therefore the
public appears, rightly or wrongly, to have a very mode-
rate opinion about the City Bank. Perhaps if that insti-
tution wrote down all its values to bedrock, and declared
openly how much the old losses amount to, and how
much the real earnings are, and how much is being set
aside each half-year towards writing off the debit, and
thus took the public into its confidence, it might be
better for it.

The Bank ot Victoria, which earned .£27,637
for the half-year to 39th June, 1899, shows a slight
diminution in profits. Recent net earnings have been :
June, 1394 .. £29,921 June, 1897 .. £17,877
December, 1594 .. 21,087 December, 1897 .. 22,729
June, 1895 .. 19,863 June, 1898 .. 24,380
December, 1895 .. 29,631 December, IS9B .. 27,800
June, 1896 . 15,ut>8 June, 1899 .. 27,637
December, 1896 .. 17,928
The profits are equal to £3 11s. 6d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds (capital, £1,476,806, and re-
serve fund, £70,000). The preference shareholders get
their usual dividend at 5 per cent, per annum (£10,419),
and the remainder, added to previous surplus, leaves
£37,249 to carry forward. The balance would only be
sufficient, at the outside, to give the ordinary share-
holders 3 per cent., and in view of the urgent
necessity for building up a strong reserve the
Bank of Victoria did well to carry it all forward.
The concern has now only £1,056,000 of the bur-
densome per cent, reconstructed deposits on hand, and
it pays off £528,009 in September, so this liability will
soon be shifted, and after that profits may improve a
little. But in spite of the new sour e ot income supplied
by the charge of ss. half-yearly on each depositor’s
account, and the relief gained by the repayment of
£509,000 of 4.) per cent, deposits last March, they cer-
tainly have not improved this half-year, The business,
however, is a very good and solid one ; the cash reserve is
large ; and the earnings are up to the average. The hank
which has a million capital and a million reserve fund
bringing in 3J per cent, can, between them, earn a decent
6 or 7 per cent, dividend on its capital nowadays, hut the
one that has no big reserve toiling to earn money for it
has a poor show.

The Commercial Bank of Australia publishes
(for l he half-year to 39th June, 1899) another of those
mysterious balance-sheets which tell the public practically
nothing that it wants to know. What the public and the
shareholders want to hear about is whether the whole
concern (which is made up of the Commercial Bank, with
£4,099,000 of liabilities to the public, and the Commercial
Bank Special Assets Trust Co., with £3,443,000 of liabili-
ties to the public) has made a profit or a loss. And there
is no real information whatever. The profits of the bank
itself for the half-year are set down at £52,247, which is
the worst return since June, 1897. And if the
Assets Trust has paid its way and earned enough to cover
all outgoings, then the profits are £52,247. And if the
Assets Trust didn't pay its way, then the net profits are
something else. In view of the neglect of the directors to
explain whether the Assets Trust is earning enough to
pay interest on its deposits or not, there is room for a lot
of speculation. For a while a certain amount of informa-
tion was sui plied, but lately the stream of knowledge
has been turned off sharp at the meter.
The profits of the Commercial Bank itself since
the second reconstruction have been :
December, 1896.. £41,036 I June, 1893 .. ..£52,363
June, 1897 .. .. 52,529 December, IS9S.. 52,891
December, 1897.. 63,222 | June, 1899 .. .. 62,247
The profits are equal to £3 7s. Id. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds, as compared with £3 (is. Bd.
for previous half-year. The usual dividend at the rate of
2 per cent, per annum on preference shares absorbs
£21,171 ; £30,000 is transferred to the mysterious Special
Assets Trust —perhaps because there are losses lo that or
some larger or smaller amount to he provided for, or
perhaps out of pure hilarity on the pari of the manage-
ment; and the remaining £1,076, added to previous
balance, leaves £10,248 at crpdit of profit and loss. The
Bulletin presents the profit and loss account thus :
Dr. Or.
Dividend .. £21,171 Profits of Bank £52,247
Losses of Assets >• °f Assets
Trust if any.. Unknown Trust if any Unknown
Balance, if any * Balance, if any ?
£ x £ ?
That is exactly the information which the Commercial
Bank of Australia gives about its affairs. The share-
holder can put down the net loss at £70,000 or £120,000
or or the net- profit at £60,000 or £lOO,OOO or 9d., jus
as Ills liver suggests.
Thk Bulletin lays these six propositions before
the Commercial Bank’s shareholders. (1) If the Assets
Trust is earning enough to pay interest on its deposits,
and there is, consequenily, fair reason to suppose its
assets will sell for enough to pay off its deposits, and con-
sequently there is no reason to believe there will
he any ruinous, or large, or even only medium
loss to he ultimately made good by the shareholders,
it is difficult to understand why this desirable state
of things should he concealed. (2). It is difficult to
understand why the Commercial Bank should transfer
£30,00.) half-yearly out of its profits to the Assets Trust
unless there is a heavy loss to he provided for. Surely it
isn’t done for amusement. (3) At the same time, the
transfer of 130,000 is no proof that the Assets Trust isn’t
losing more than £30,099 half-yearly. The bank transfers
practically all the profits it has after paying the fixed
2 per cent. div. on preference shares. It may he really
necessary to transfer more, but there is very little more
to transfer. At all events, the preference shareholders
are entitled to 4 per cent, dividend if the profits are there ;
the fact that they only get the fixed minimum of 2 per
cent, seems to imply that all the rest (and perhaps
more if it were available) is needed on account of
the Assets Trust. (4) The bank’s profits show a slight
decrease. (5) The fixed minimum dividend to preference
shareholders is 3 per cent for the future (£31,756). There-
fore unless tilings improve there will he only £29,000 per
half year to help the Assets Trust with—the Trust which
seems to need at least £30,000. Also (6) the reason ad-
vanced by the present directorate for giving no informa-
tion about the Assets Trust is that, when it took office, it
stipulated that it should not be held responsible for the
old bank’s had bargains which the Trust took over. But
not being responsible is one thing; not giving informa-
tion about the state of £3,443,000 worth of the assets is
quite another.
Things look well with the Commercial Bank so
far as they can he traced. During the half-year note cir-
culation has increased by £3009 ; deposits by £453,000 ;
and cash and liquid assets have gone up by £574,009 and
now stand at the splendid total of £1,763,854 equal to
43 per cent, of liabilities to the public. But the mys-
terious Assets Trust is always hanging about.

The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. (Mel-
bourne) has done somewhat better this half-year (to 30th
June, 1899). Net profit is set down as £1953, and the
usual 5 per cent, dividend on £60,000 capital consumes
11500 of this. (The other £29,702 receives no dividend).
These figures compare with some previous incomings and
outgoings after this fashion :
Profit. Dividend.
June, 1892 .. £8548 30 percent. = £9OOO
December, 1892 .. 6874 22J „ 6750
June, 1893 .. 4656 15 „ 4500
December, 1833 .. 3909 12J 3750
June, 1894 .. 2287 „ 2250

June, 1895 .. 305 Nil
December, 1895 .. 256 Nil
June, 1896 .. 1702 4 percent * 1200
December, 1896 .. 1779 6 „ 1500
June, 1897 .. 1756 6 „ 1500
December, 1897 .. 1705 6 ~ 1500
June, 1898 .. 1723 6 „ 1500
December, 1898 ..1101 5 „ 1500
June, 1899 .. 1953 5 1500
In the half-year ended 31st December, 189 S, the co.
was struck with £l3OO costs in a disastrous law-suit. So,
in the ordinary course of things it should have shown a
profit of some £2400. Allowing for this extra expendi-
ture the present half-year is really hardly up to the level
of the last. Still, the accounts show a tolerably steady
improvement.
The last £42,000 of the Trustees, Executors and
Agency Co.’s reserve fund disappeared six months ago.
The co., by virtue of the law-suit above-mentioned, was
held responsible for a large sum of its eiiems’ money in-
vested (wrongly, the Courts decided) in bank deposit
receipts, and £29,409 was set aside to make this good.
The other £12,600 went to write down tile co.'s own
disastrous investment of £14,000 in Australian Deposit
and Mortgage Bank shares to their then value of £l4OO.
The A.D. and M. has now begun to climb out of the mud
a little, and these shares (put in the balance-sheet
at £1400) are worth, at time of writing, nearly £3500.
The co. also had a disastrous investment of £20,000
in deposits of the same hank, but it seems to be gradually
selling these and writing-off the loss. There are still
£16,000 worth, put in the balance-sheet at face value,
whereas their present selling price is only about £11,600,
therefore the difference is a mere paper asset of £4,400.
But the deposits are steadily improving in price. Con-
sidering that the shares are put in at £2,000 below their
present price, and that the balance-sheet shows £3,253
accumulated profits, the co. probably has its capital
intact, and a clear surplus of £5OO or so after allowing
for all losses. So the prospects may he summed up
thus : The co’s business appears to he improving; its
2,800 A.D. and M. shares, now valued at £1,409, may
some day he worth £14,090 again ; its £16,000 worth of
deposits are increasing in value ; the widow of the late
managing director, wno receives £5OO a year pension,
isn t immortal ; and if the hoard only keeps the dividend
down low, and set to work to rebuild its vanished
reserves, there is no visible reason why the T. E.
and A. shouldn’t be a large and prosperous concern
again in a few years. Apropos of which, the co.’s
paid-up capital has been lately increased from £60,000 to
£89,702 (a call of £30,099 was made when the big legal
disaster struck it last year), and, if it paid 5 per cent, on
the real capital, it would absorb £2243 instead of £l5OO.
As the profits won’t nearly run to this, the dividend is
paid on £60,000 only. Therefore the shareholders really
get £3 6s. lid. per cent. Last half-year, the paid-up
capital was £73,3i6, hut then also the dividend was paid
on £60,000 only. A 5 per cent, dividend isn’t always
what it seems.

The N.S.W. Mont de Piete (Sydney) shows a
great decline in profits ; the earnings for the half-year, to

last 5j years have been :
June', 1894 .. .. £4,314 I June, 1597 .. .. £4,225
December, 1594 .. 4,470 December, 1897 .. 4,297

December, 1895 .. 4,765 I December, IS9S .. 3,589
June, 1896 .. .. 4,699 June, 1899 .. .. 2,300

The case doesn't admit of ihe old 10 per cent, dividend,
so it is cut down to 7j. Even this absorbs £2,773, which
is £473 more than the earnings, and the balance at
credit of profit and loss is reduced from £750 to
£277. Last half-year also the dividend wasn’t fully
earned. The profits have shown a very steady down-
ward tendency since December, 1895 ; but this time
the co. appears to have struck really hard against
a snag. The equalisation-of-dividend reserve has been

possible losses, and the total reserves have been le-
duced, hv provision for losses, to £15,633. Two years ago
they were as high as £19,500, so the fading away is con-
siderable. Whether there are any further losses to be
provided for doesn’t definitely appear ; the transfer of the
Dividend Equalisation account to contingencies looks as
if the management had doubts on the subject. At all
events, it is"a very had record for this usually careful in-
stitution to have twice in one year paid a dividend that
was not fully earned.

B.H. Water Supply directors reply to B.H.
Municipal Council, seeking to purchase the co.’s works,
&c., that they will recommend shareholders to sell for
£309,099 —which would pail out at just under 32 shillings
a share. The Council clutched the table to save itself
from falling ; though, after all, the price isn’t much of an
advance upon market quotations. The present outlook
is that the Government will he moved by every means the
Council knows to carry through the Yanco Glen project.
And it will get help from some at least of the big mining
co’s, which are not well-disposed towards the Water Co.

* • * *
B. H. Prop., which has not ventured much below
800 ft. so far, proposes to open out straight away at 600 ft.
Block 10’s deep-ground development has given the neces-
sary encouragement. But just at present Block 10 s
deep-ground workings are rather poorer than^middling.
There are no announcements in B.H. Prop.’s
half-yearly report which will surprise shareholders. Net
profits for the period were £107,563; this is making
allowance for depreciation (£34,616), but not for plant
provided. The three things that are best worth attention
are—(l) by briquetting the concentretes and fine ore it is
found that a greater quantity can be smelted, and that
the metal-recovery is better ; (2) 110,797 tons of sulphides
were concentrated at “piactically the same total cost as
the smaller quantity (79,742 tons) during the previous
half-year ” ; and (3), despite better prices for silver (|<l.
per oz.) and for lead (4s. 4d. per ton), it was not possible
to earn the £120,000 which was paid in divs. Of course,
the sulphide-concentration plant is being doubled, and
the briquetting will make a difference ; but, then, the
kaolin and other oxidised ores are to be drawn on more
sparingly. The 2s. Od. per half-year may be continued ;
but a great deal will depend on the metal-market. But if
zinc-treatment is accomplished (and little is said about it
in the report) all calculations will have to, be upon a
different basis altogether.

Sydney D.T. on that disgrace to the Stock Ex-
change, the mysterious bake George mine :—“ In copper,
the ever-growing mystery, Lake George, was dealt in at
from 15s. 3d. to 145." 9d. No explanation can be offered
for the jump in value of these shares, except that the
gloomy record of last month is followed by a rumor that
it is intended to close down at least one of the furnaces.
Then, next day : “ Lake George furnished another sensa-
tion bj" jumping to 17s. The latest report is that arrange-
ments are to be made to treat the ore for zinc. Lake
George has been a conspicuous failure as a gold-mine, as
a silver-mine, and as a copper-mine ; when it fails as a
zinc-mine probably an effort will be made to run it as an
iron-mine, and that failing it may become a sulphur pro-
position. On present showing it has as much chance of
succeeding as the latter as it has as anything else.”

Turon River gold dredging: A new issue of
shares to erect the second dredge. Shares have gone to
over 605., representing the value of £100,060, and this,
for a scheme that has never had a clean-up, is remarkable.
It speaks well for the dredge business in Sydney, if not
on the river, and very badly for the Chinamen who have
been working the stream for the last 20 years. It is also
remarkable that the Sabbath Shellback warns the public
not to anticipate an enormous yield at the start —that it
will only really represent a week’s run ; at the same time,
the S.S. think's the shares worth a lot more money. But
has not the dredge been running oyer six weeks? Alto-
gether, the situation provides an enigma of too, profound
a character for The Bulletin to deal with.

* * * *
The present price of tin is .£135 per ton, and
there are tin-mining men who say it must go to £2OO.
And why not? Its uses are innumerable—and increasing.
Nothing can take its place. It is more perishable than
copper ; does not come into re-use like that metal ; and,
above all, isn't found all over the world like other indus-
trial metals. America has none, although the biggest
consumer. There are many tin-mines in Germany, Hun-
gary, &e., but they are so low-grade that the total yearly
production does not amount to 1000 tons. In Germany
there are tin-mines worked only for scientific purposes,
not paying with the cheapest of labor and the most effec-
tive and economical plant. There are only three big pro-
ducing countries in the world. The Straits Settlements
are, tar and away the biggest. In the Straits there are as
many as 12,000 Chinese and other aliens working a single
mine, mostly stream tin, and for that reason the Straits
shows are being gradually exhausted, a fact that has had
more to do with the increased price of the metal than any
other. Tasmania has the second largest tin-mine in Mt.
Bischoff. Cornwall tin-mines were once copper, lodes
having changed to tin—a geological feature, the writer
believes, unknown in any other country. Tin, in Aus-
tralia, is generally found in widely separated and isolated
places. Herberton, in Bananaland, and the New Eng.
districts of N.S.YV. are the chief, both producing much
less than they did years ago. Under all the circum-
stances, a big boom in tin must come sooner or later,
though at this moment it is easier to’float a cork than a
tin mine ; but miners might do worse than look for tin
where it is known to exist. Almost daily writer hears of
unpayable 2 and 3 p.c. tin lodes, while there are in Corn-
wall payable deep tin-mines working under I p.c. at a
profit.

Copper still about £77— a mighty fine figure for
those who have producing mines. Cabled that, owing to
the high price of the metal, the cost per word on the pro-
posed new Pacific cable is likely to be 3s. 6d. It will take
thousands of tons of copper to lay this cable, and this,
no doubt, is somewhat influencing the market. The
work has been determined on in face of the persistent and
powerful opposition of the Eastern Extension Cable Co.
By the way, The Bulletin observes, in connection with a
recent deputation from that co. to Chamberlain, that over
half-a-column of chairman t*e Marquis of Tweeddale’s
speech was cabled to the Australian dailies, while Cham-
berlain’s forceful reply was dismissed in a few words. The
cabled account of this unimportant deputation, the out-
come of certain vested interests only, occupied altogether
a column—which is more than has been sent on one day
upon any other subject for a long time past. Now, who
paid for that cable ? The allied Newspaper Cable Syndi-
cate of Australia—or was it sent gratuitously on behalf
of one of the greatest of the world's monopolies, the
Eastern Extension Telegraph Co. ? By the way, what is
the betting that wireless telegraphy will become really
available for long distances just about the time the new
cable is laid ?
* • * *
Another offspring of the S. A. boom is arsenic-
mining. When copper-mines were deserted there were
some big bodies of arsenical-pyrites showing. But there
is no hope for the production of arsenic in Australia ex-
cept as a by-product. The Broken Hill mines thought of
it once, but found it wouldn’t pay to purify.
The Bulletin office often has a lot of pre m
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submined. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, bv ossav and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assavs alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not only give a statement of metallic contents—
owners will he advised by an experienced, practical metal-
lurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in bulk,
whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation, smelt-
ing, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should not bo
less than lib. nor more than 101 b.
* • • •
Worturpa (8. A.). —The telluride boom has been
very short-lived, and it is the sacred duty of a number of
Adelaideans to kick themselves in sheer shame and dis-
gust. One man aptly describes the place as famous or
infamous for telluride of nearly every metal but gold.
The Wilgena (8. A.) boom was also a horrible
frost. How humiliating, after the glittering yarn about
the dying man’s advice to his son, to have the report of a
cold-blooded Inspector of Mines: “ Formation, decomposed
granite and lime-tried numerous prospects; no trolfl ;
country only slightly mineralised. Small specks may
have been found—but appearances very unfavorable,
nothing worth pegging.” All sorts of schemes have
withered away, or gone into liquidation. The whole
thing a fiasco, and heavy insurance premiums will be de-
manded on the lives of men capable of trying to start
another S.A. mining-boom.
Waihi (M.L.) shares are now at <£B. With a
quarterly div. of 25., this means exactly 5 percent. As
every pick-stroke brings the property nearer the end of
its tether, this is a poor per-ceutage for an “ investment”
mining stock.
Tas. mining figures for year ended .Tune 30 :
Gold, £300.151 ; silver, £215.211 ; blister copper, £468,877;
tin, £178,438 ; coal, £34,602 ; all other minerals, £38,400.
Total, £1,235,679. Value of increase for year, £157,812.
Divs. paid, £282,700; miners employed, 6053 ; wages paid
(estimated at £2 ss. per week per man), £708,188. Differ-
ence between total value of output and divs. and wages
paid, £244,791.
Mt. Bischoff, Tas., tin-mine has paid npnrly a
million and a-half in divs. to date, and produced between
59,009 and 60,000 tons of tin. Has got mining and milling
costs down to a minimum. Mining, including mainten-
ance and new development work, 2s. 4Jd. per ton. Crush-
ing and dressing, just under Is. per ton. All costs, ss.
4£d. per ton. Divs. last six months, £36,000, and many
ahead while tin keeps at anything like its price.
• • • *
North Mt. Lyell : What is the next share-
boom to be founded on? N. Lyell has been boomed on
rich streaks of ore. Boomed on fairly-large deposits
without anything very defined or high-grade ore. Boomed
on ils railway, boomed on tbe purchase of a steamer, and
then on the recent sale of ditto. Boomed on sending
rich ore away ; now boomed because this costly procedure
is to cease ; the latest cause of boom is the rumor of
future treatment at Mt. Lyell reduction works. What it
is going to do with its unerected, but well-boomed, smelt-
ing plant isn't just now mentioned. Writer would sug-
gest—selling to Mt, Lyell and having another little boom
in consequence. The parent mine may have made mis-
takes such as are inseparable from the establishment of
any gigantic concern, but the N.M.L. management seems
to do nothing but follow one blunder with another.
Rocky River Mine, Tas., has received the
results of some complex ore sent to England, giving 14
per cent, nickel, 4 per cent, copper, 2 per cent, cobalt ;
also some molybdenite. It has started to boom on the
strength of these things. The It.lt. is one of many mines
of which the public has grown tired, by reason of the lies
told about them, and if this one has anything like the ore
it is represented to have, it should long have been paying
divs. instead of making calls. Writer happens to know
that it was unfavorably reported on by Holroyd, the
discoverer of the Kalgoorlie tellurides, but the manage-
ment has never published Holroyd’s report.
• * « •
The Tas. Smelting Co. isn’t likely to go un-
employed. The agreement with the Reids guarantees
the delivery of 30,000 tons, assay stipulated, in three
years. In 1899, 5000 tons; in 1900, 10,000 tons ; 1901,
15,000 tons. Several small shows, encouraged by im-
proved smelting facilities, are recommencing to burrow
for ore, but the cry is for moie tramways.
* * *
The Wilfley concentrator has only been a short
time before the mining public, but has already demon-
strated its superiority, over 1200 being sold within the
past two years. This machine has a capacity of from
25 to 30 tons per day, and will save more sulphuretts,
float gold, amalgam or quicksilver, and make a cleaner
and better separation than any belt concentrator made.
It can be easily and rapidly adjusted while in motion, and
requires no expert attention. The Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Coy., South Melb, have.been appointed sole manufac-
turing agents, and are now engaged constructing machines
for the colonies. The price of the machine is £ll5.
* * * *
Garden Gully’s (Bendigo) formation at 1400 ft.
has in 12 months brought in something like £BO,OOO profit,
of which £77,927 has gone in divs.—the rest has been
spent on machinery. This is equal to £2 6s. Cd. per share.
Production has eased off, but there is much prospecting
and good hopes of other bonanzas.
* * • *
Venture Corporation, after its many dismal
failures in Westralia, directs its attention to Rhodesia,
S.A., and is asking the public to buy “ The Old Dog,”
“ Jessop,” ami “Jackson” claims, which, the directors
say, “ make up a very nice proposition.” What has be-
come of all their ventures in W.A. ? They had one in the
90-mile which was reported to have 200,000 tons of 2oz.
ore in sight; also, what has become of their experts
One of these was lately said to have started a vineyard
near Albury.
* * • *
Vic. mine-inspectorships entice more practised
hands than the corresponding N.S.W. billets—probably
because of the miserable pay of Vic. managers. Lately,
when applications were invited for three vacancies, a
score of almost the best-known goldfields men applied.
One at least has already been chosen —R. Youlden, of the
Long Tunnel South, formerly of the New Chum and
Victoria. While grafting underground, Youlden stuck to
the classes at the Bendigo School of Mines, and as a result
has got this far out of the ruck already.

Before the site of the new Sydney city
railway-station is decided, the people should think
hard over this problem. Bringing the railway
along the east side of George-street to King-street
means the removal of a very decent block of build-
ings to make room for the station. Taking it
along the west side to the neighborhood of the
Rocks mostly means the clearing away of slums
which sooner or later will have to be cleared away
at great expense, anyhow. And a railway station
at or near Miller’s Point leaves things all ready for
a further extension to North Shore, which must
come some day. To extend the line from upper
King-street to North Shore makes no such provi-
sion, unless the railway bridge is to be in the road
of every vessel going into Circular Quay.
** u * . *
, Sydney Telegraph prints pictures of the
rotten plates stripped from local steamers
(harbor and ocean-going) since new blood was
poured into the stagnant Marine Board. The
plates were originally three-eighths of an inch
thick. They were allowed to rust down to
one-sixteenth of an inch before they were taken
off—one-sixth of their original thickness— and
weighed onty as many dwts. as they originally
weighed ozs. And they were so brittle that
pieces could be broken off by hand. How many
years they took to reach this stage is uncertain ;
but they have repeatedly passed the Marine Board
coat of paint; and sailors and passengers re-
peatedly went to sea (or down the harbor) with
one-sixteenth of an inch of perished iron separat-
ing them from eternity ! All these lovely reve-
lations are the direct result of the Atacama ease,
about which N.S.W. ship-owners have not yet
ceased cursing The Bulletin.
Melb. Argus and Agb are competing for
popular favor on very “commercial principles.”
Argos is booming the En t o>olopced ia Brittanica
in the Amurkan style—time-payment biz., and is
publishing on Wednesdays and Saturdays stuff
that it wouldn’t have touched with a long pole 10
years ago. And the Age is serving-out “ penny-
dreadful” of an aggravated type. Altogether, a
complete falling away from journalistic grace.
• • * •
The Age does its best to “queer” the
Argos by pointing out that the Encyclopedia
Buttannica is a quarber-of-a-century behind the
time in all its political information, and leaves
Britain in the hands of Mr. Disraeli, and France
under the presidency of Marshal MacMahon. But,
then, this is quite as much up-to-date as the
Argos usually considers it advisable for the
average person to be.

Justice as she is in Australia :
At Redruth (S.A.) police-court, one Bennett was sen-
tenced to two months’ imprisonment for throwing boiling
water over his wife, while she was holding a child in her
arms, because she refused to give him money for drink.
Two months for partially boiling a wife ! And the
annexation of 20s. worth of property is often good
for 12 months’gaol in civilised Australia. In fact,
9d. worth of baking-powder has been known to
pan out for three years. Two months ! It seems
a pity some judicious, person doesn’t empty a pint
of boiling water over their washups on the bench,
so that they can form some idea of the kind of
case they are dealing with.
* • * *
An applicant for old-age pension in Maori-
land was asked by the magistrate how he earned
his living. “Just doing odd jobs; but sometimes I
don’t get one for six months, and then I have to
go to the bank.” Magistrate : “ Ah, you’ve
money in the bank. Post-office, I suppose. How
much have you?” Applicant: “I didn’t mean
that kind of bank* It’s the pippi (shellfish) bank
Igo to. I get there at low water and withdraw
something.”
One casual joy which has
gone out of the Cornstalk’s
existence is the loss of
Bernard Ingleby’s letters
from Wragge’s tent on
Mount Kosciusko. In
the early days of
Wragge’s windy experi-
ment the N.S. Waler
got some value for the
money which Premier
Reid spent in subsi-
dising the high and
unstable habitation.
There were interest-
ing columns in the
daily papers telling
how Bernard Ingle-
by struggled down
to Jindahyne for pro-
visions; how he strug-
gled up again and
was lost in the snow
and had to camp out;
how the tent blew
away and Bernard
Ingleby ran after it;
how the tent ran
faster than Ingleby;
how the thermome-
ter blew after In-
gleby and struck
him a foul blow
from behind ; bow
cold it was when the
thermometer went
down to so many
degrees below some-
thing, and how it
wasn’t so cold when
the thermometer
rose to so many
degrees above some-
thing ; and how the observers
suffered from cold feet and ingrowing toe-nail
while going out in the early morning to look ati
the barograph, or the cenotaph, or the hexameter,
or something. All that news has died away now.
Ingleby’s successor doesn’t run after the tent in a
literary spirit, nor does he publish his cold feet in
the papers. So far as the public is aware, Kos-
ciusko has never produced any scientific results,
and now it lias even ceased to furnish interesting
reading matter.

The Melbourne bridegroom who disap-
peared on the morning of what should have been
his wedding day and was found a month
later in the Narra, was, apparently, an-
other victim to the devastating influ-
ence of furniture. He had lost his
billet, but decided to go on with
the wedding just the same, though
his own family was unaware of
the fact. But at the last
moment he failed to raise
the needful furniture,
and he couldn’t take
his bride to an empty
house and ask her to
sit down gently on
the floor, and seem-
ingly he didn’t care to
tell her that he was
unable to offer her a
chair. So he solved
the furniture pro-
blem in the Yarra.
Sitting accommo-
dation can be ob-
tained in the Yarra
without paying
any deposit, and
the after instal-
ments are easy.

♦** - *
At one of Mrs. Escaped Nun Slattery’s
Sydney lectures to “women only,” some girls in
the gallery spotted down below two persons whom
they took to be men in women’s clothes. They
communicated their suspicions, and squalls arose
of “ Put out the men ! ” but Mrs. Slattery ap-
peared and assured the ladies that if there were
any men in the hall they wouldn’t hurt, and
should be let alone. Then there was quietness for
the time, but at the close the suspected persons
were mobbed as they were making their way out.
They took it all so coolly, however, that The
Bulletin’s informant remained doubtful of their
sex until she saw the diabolical smile with which
they disappeared into outer darkness.

• • * *
The commission appointed to in-
quire into the great Rakaia (M.L.) rail-
way smash has decided that it arose
through the driver “maintaining too
high a rate of speed.” But when a driver
has to keep time, and the rails are slip-
pery with rain so that his train won’t
pull up easily, and the station is at the
foot of an incline so that the train (not
being fitted with modern brakes) slides
helplessly down the wet rails, and there
is a train in the road which shouldn’t be
there, and the uncivilised management
hasn’t put up a semaphore to let the
driver know in good time that the track
isn’t clear, and the official who is sup-
posed to crawl through the rain and wave
a miserable glimmer of warning by hand
only appears with his glimmer when it is
too late —what can be expected ? The
commission has reported that all M.L.
trains should be fitted with the Westing-
house brake. Which is a tolerably
straight admission that it was the want
of that brake which caused the trouble.
One tiling seems reasonably certain. The
driver—being only a driver, and not a
prophet—couldn’t tell in advance where
he might find an unexpected obstacle.
And if he approached every station at a
crawl to make sure of taking no risks, he would
shortly have been sacked for habitual procrastina-
tion of his train.

Poetically-named Leonora (W.A.), accord-
ing to N. Coolgardte Times, is a place where
things happen. Something happened there the
other day when a policeman after vain appeals for
help to the people around, arrested a one-legged
drunkard all by his own self. Then he rested
from his labors, after which he swooped down
anew and took up a spectator named Harset for
not assisting him in doing the mighty deed.
Harset, thus arrested for his crime in being where
he was, was chained up all night outside the
Police Court and afterwards sentenced to a
month’s jail. He was doing his month comfort-
LOVING and LEAVING
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Mary complained tliatl wasn’t as faithful
And hardly as warm as in seasons of yore,
And said, in a voice that ivas plaintive and wraith-
ful,
That she feared it was true that I loved her no
more.
I hissed her, and said to her, “ Dear, it is not;
Though I leave you a little, I love you a lot."
But when T sat down to a quiet revisal
Of the love that had gallantly lasted so long,
Then I knew that old Time icas beginning reprisal,
And that Mary was right and her lover was
wrong;
And I murmured obscurely: “ Things ripe and they
rot;
I must leave you a little to love you a lot. ”

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, t,he recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
•of crystalline carbide of calciums Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia —are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. Thiseo, have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36. Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

S.M. Herald’s “ Miscellaneous ” advt.-
eolumn on a recent date contained 19 notices,
every one of which came from a palmist or
futurist. As clairvoyancy and fortune-telling are
illegal, and very properly so, we shall no doubt
soon see the respectable Herald, if it continues to
print such advts. at all, classifying them under
the heading “Impostures.”

S.M. Herald, having taken to publishing
serials, will now be known as the Family Herald.
♦ * * *
Mrs. Tates, ex-Mayor of Onehunga (M.L.),
recently got even with Dr. Erson, present Mayor,
her chief opponent. Some time back the medico
declared with great emphasis, “ I’ll never again
contest the Mayoralty with a woman.” Next
time, accordingly, he stood out of the contest,
and let a nominee of his fight Mrs. Yates. Last
election he waited till the morning of the last day
before entering his nomination. At 11.55 a.m.
the doctor and his friends retired to have a drink
over his unopposed return. At 11.57 Mrs. Yates
turned up and planked down her nomination.
Dr. Erson was, accordingly, let in for another
contest with a woman.

The Yic. Queen's Fund, founded by dear
Lady Loch in ’B7 when Her Gracious scored her
first jubilee and the early Boomsters were lavish
with offerings of other people’s money, seems
just the sort of Charity that might have been ex-
pected. The capital is .£13,184, there are 48 names
on the committee, and the distribution of cash to
75 “ poor and distressed ” persons (most of whom
were possibly distant cousins of said committee)
amounted to £374 last year. Has the Vic.
Labor-party in the ’Ouse ever turned an enquiring
eye towards that precious Queen’s Fund ?

The mysterious father of a certain sensa-
tional infant has been identified by the public as
every prominent man in the capital, from the
Governor to the coroner who presided over the
inquiry into the affair. A candidate at a recent
election was neatly spoofed over the same infant.
One of his personal friends, Soflgoods, chagrined
him by taking a seat on his opponent’s committee,
and on their first subsequent meeting the candi-
date immediately demanded, “ I thought you
were a friend of mine?” “So am I,” replied
Softgoods. “Then,” asked the candidate, “ why
did you take this position against me?” Soft-
goods assumed a confidential air, led the candidate
to a quiet corner, and whispered, “ Well, you
know, you could hardly expect me to take you up
after that Baby O’Callaghan affair.”

A few years ago an apprentice on a mer-
chantman, calling at a S. A. outport, was smitten
by the charms of a rustic Sweet Seventeen. He
kept his passion to himself, though, until his ship
had sailed ; and then (he was only an apprentice,
and not a seasoned mariner) he wrote and told all.
The chilling answer was that marriage couldn’t be
thought.of till he had his master’s certificate and
a ship. The youngster accepted the condition,
set to work, gained the certificate, got his ship in
record time, and sent for the lady. The two then
.made several voyages together, but a coming
domestic event led to the captain taking one
voyage alone. It was his last—he was Capt. P.
H. Jones, who went down with his ship, the City
of York, off Rottnest. At Fremantle a cable
awaited him that a child had been safely born.

N.S.W. country police court. Ah Ling
charged with using a kerosene-tin for rubbish
instead of the pan prescribed by the municipal
by-laws. The beak: “I really cannot see any
offence in this case, but the ironmonger has to
live, and Ah Ling must buy a pan from him ; and
I will inflict a nominal penalty of Is., because, if
I dismiss the case, the solicitor .won't get his 10s.
6d., and the rubbish man and the nuisance man
won’t get their ss. each as witnesses.” Next, a
person is charged with heaving half-an-ounce or so
of rubbish in another man’s yard (his tenant).
Defendant; “ I never got a notice about this
matter, your Worship ; and, in any case, accord-
ing to the by-laws the occupier is responsible.
The Mag.: “Well, here also there is no offence, but
the solicitor must have his 10s. 6d. and the wit-
nesses their ss. each ; so I fine you 2s. 6d. And
you will notice that, by making the fine Is. Gd.
more, I have avoided classing you with the China-
man 1 ”

The irony of circumstances : At a recent
Sydney R.C. church-bazaar a Hebrew tailor won—
of all things in the world—a pig ! Imagine the
unfortunate Israelite’s feelings at being obliged to
give away his prize !
N.S.W. Col. Secretary Brunker meditates
for next session a Bill compelling persons to con-
tribute towards the support of their relatives who
are inmates of the asylums for the infirm and
destitute. As the law now stands, no one is
liable for the support of any relative, except wife
and children. How far the Col. Secretary intends
to extend this limit is not yet announced; any-
how, he will open a very pretty question. Un-
doubtedly there are many cases in the various
institutions of more or less aged parents aban-
doned by affluent but unnatural children to
the tender mercies of the asylums, but there
are also cases in which the unnatural parents
enter to spite the children, or because they
prefer the life, or because, through drink,
&c., they cannot be kept decently outside. (In
one asylum at present is a once-prominent bank
manager, of good family, who cannot be kept out
by his family). The proposed legislation will no
doubt lessen the strain on the Govt, institutions,
principally through the dread people will have of
their cases going before the Court. If the authori-
ties were now able to publish a list of cases where
State hospitality is grossly abused by well-to-do
persons the evil would much diminish.

At Sydney Benevolent Asylum every
Wednesdayis distributed with much parade a small
quantity of food to several hundred poor families,
and the issuing of these miserable doles keeps five
men employed all day ; whereas, if money were
piven instead of food, one man could pay it all out
m half a day. “ But they would drink the
money,” says the local philanthropist. Some
might, but what is to prevent them from selling
the dole for a shilling or two?

* * * *
The recent Adelaide Mayor’s ball was
remarkable for the fact that, for the first time
since the hospital dispute began, four years ago,
Govt, doctors and membersof theoutside profession
were at a civic dance together. The late Mayor
was chairman of the Hospital Board and the mal-
contents refused to accept his invitations.
Had Adelaide City Coroner Whittle not suffi-
ciently recovered from his recent illness to resume
duty, the long-standing hospital dispute would
have come near settlement: Ramsay Smith, the
bete noir of the outside doctors would have got the
appointment. Dr. Napier, the other imported
medico, is not nearly so much objected to.

N.S.W. Judge Cohen, in his recent judg-
ment on the National Guard case—wherein G.
Republican Dibbs made an expensive error in
accepting a corporal’s verbal resignation, instead
of getting a written one —called attention to the
N.S.W. Volunteer Regulation
“That the Queen's Regulations and customs of the
Imperial military service will be taken generally as a
guide in all matters not specially dealt with in the Volun-
teer Act and these regulations.”
This is constantly forgotten in N.S.W. military
procedure. The ‘officers of the staff are grossly
ignorant, and act under the advice of non-com-
missioned and warrant-officers, whose experience
as sergeants and corporals of British regiments
does not qualify them to give opinions on ques-
tions of military law.

On August 11, exactly three-quarters of a
century will have elapsed since the first N.S.W.
Leg. Council assembled. It consisted of Gov.
Darling, Col. Stewart (Lieut.-Gov.), Chief-Justice
Forbes, Archdeacon Scott, and Colonial-Secretary
Macleay. The non-official members were: John
Macarthur, Robt. Campbell, sen., and Charles
Throsbv, whose old home is now the country-resi-
dence of the Governor (Throsby Park).

Golden-haired Elsie Hubbard, of Mel-
bourne, has all the makings of a Jeanne d’Arc, a
Mrs. Gordon-Baillie, or a Sarah Siddons—circum-
stance will shape her career anon. Elsie is an
adventuress of eight years’ growth, and she was
lately sent back home from Adelaide, whither she
had shipped on her little own as a steamer stowa-
way. On two previous occasions the restless in-
fant was found wandering to nowhere in particu-
lar for the sole purpose of being.her own mistress,
and when the inevitable policeman captured her,
she gave great accounts of her father’s rank and
station, although the “ old man,” as a matter of
fact, is merely a “respectable laborer. ’’ Evi-
dently a child with a future is Elsie Hubbard.
Make a note of the name, for Elsie may one day
tell a thrilling story with a young man in it

The poet’s dream of perfection in the
feminine form divine has been realised at last.
Nature working in many mysterious ways has
wrought out, by hereditary and by the mental pro-
gress which directs physical culture, a new woman,
who can afford to laugh at the figures of her
mediaeval grandmothers as depicted in the family
picture-gallery. And the enchanting outlines of
to-day are best defined by P.D. corsets. P.D. :
Poet’s Dream.*
“The Light of Asia,” which, as is well known,
was one of the principal books of its year, was an un-
doubted success, and without doubt so is the new light of
Australasia, the “ Albatross Lamp Wick.” “It requires
no trimming,” emits no odour, uses less oil, prevents
smoky chimneys, and is far safer than the ordinary wick.*
