A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 25, 1899.
My Deak Myee,—
This fevered indecision as to whether Lord
Beauchamp will turn out to be the correct sort of
Gov. he ought not to be judging by the look of
him ; or whether the wall-papers and the Old
Masters will shut him off from the large varnished
and unvarnished crowd, waiting breathlessly to
see what he is going to do next, only gets fiercer
as the days go on. Nobody can make up her mind
(which might almost as well have been written his
mihd, as ail the Society johnnies are wondering
wearily if it can be true that H.E. is paying more
attention to the othah-classes-don’t-you-know than
to them) whether Govt. House is the same dear,
sweet, homely place it used to he under other
regimes. Cards for balls and At Homes up there
have not been issued yet. In fact, the only
whisper in’'connection with invitations says, that
the lists have been reduced by a tremendous
vulgar fraction; which merely intensifies tho
awful agony. The only people who benefit from
all this trouble are the poor, dear Hampdens,
whose sudden popularity trebles every day that
those invitations don’t come.
A Gov. should always consider this. He can’t
do anything well, without doing it not too wisely
for the Gov. that comes after him. You mightn’t
think so just at present, but Lord Beauchamp's
successor will have the saddest time. No wall-
papers ! No dimple ! No soulful views on
the cultivation of long hair and daffodils!
Nothing that this aesthetic city of ours has been
used to all its life and can’t possibly do without!
Even those who refer to-day to our H.E.’s pride
must acknowledge that it’s a case of pride going
before destruction when they think of the H.E.
that will follow this one.
Anyway, invitations press in to Govt. House.
These are issued in all sorts of strange shapes.
One over-anxious ball-committee woman rang
up lately, and demanded to speak to Mr.
Corkran. “Mr. Corkran is out,” replied an
etherial Oxford tone. She shouted hurriedly
that she had wanted to ask Lord Beauchamp
something. “Lord Beauchamp is speaking,”
was the reply. “Oh-h!—Er —Oh-h ! Well, we-
we-we only wanted to know if he’d come to a ball,
your Excellency, and lend your presence, if he
would be so very kind; it’s in aid of a charity,
your Excellency, if Lord Beauchamp hasn’t an
engagement for that night.” There the handles
dropped from her fingers. Perhaps her way of
putting it was too puzzling. Anyway, that ball
was unavoidably impossible.
But at a Poultry Show Lora Beauchamp is a
glorious success. He gazes into the eyes of every
prize fowl, strokes the dogs, goes the round from
duc'ks to magpies with a fearfully interested air, and
no suspicion of scurry. N.S.W. Poultry, Pigeon,
Canary and Dog Society had its exhibition vice-
regally opened last Thursday. His Ex. fancied
the dachshunds and bought an eight-days-old
puppy from Mr. Isitt. Of course, you know that
these are the only dogs received into Good-Society
just now. For my part, I can’t see -why a
dachshund should put all the other dogs in the
shade from a fashionable point of view. Is it
simple vandalism ? Or are they too ugly for
words ?
FoWls, too, are getting up fashions of their own.
Every stylish hen tries to be buff-colored. Even
fowls that used to look so chic in (mre white have
taken madly to the new tint. It makes you feel
quite sorry for the Spanishes and other faces that
were born black. As for the cats, they were hot
let in to the show on opening day. It seems that
dogs get too excited at the sound of “meows,”
and on their behalf the felines'were kept out till the
last day. One mastiff lost his head altogether
over a cat last year. To hear the “ meow, ” and
not be able to chase it, was too much for him.
He broke out of his cage and 'dashed into the
feline quarters —and I draw a curtain over the
blood-curdling sequel. Some valuable cats were
shown this year. Mrs. W. Keep exhibited a
Persian lady worth .£lOO, and a Maltese worth
£5O. There was no entry for the “ heaviest cat of
any variety.” Times don’t rise to that yet.
Can anybody tell me, were there ever six women
banded together in the cause of charity who did
not tear each other to tatters before they had got
their poor people into new garments ? The charity
of the'future will have to be a committee with its
nails cut righ,t off, all its teeth blunted, and the
sharp edge razed off its tongue, that will devote
itself to saving Women Who Go On Committees.
Even,the Fancy Fair, that includes all the vir-
tuous people in this city, has had its little breeze.
One hard-working stallholder suddenly took
the usual mysterious umbrage. She had dressed
over a hundred dolls, too, and the weary commit-
tee tiredly remarked that they wouldn’t have
minded so much if she had left the dolls behind.
Then there were things said to left and to right.
And the charge was made point-blank that the
committee had picked out the stalls it liked best.
What an awful-charge ! But things like that are
dark blue insults at committee-meetings.
There was one stall-holder who said to another
with crushing satire, “Yes, I knew you were tell-
ing the truth when you said you didn’t like the
stall, but you’d take it because nobody else would.
I-suppose you didn’t know that that was the stall
I had set my mind on? ” The other looked at her
ferociously. “I plead innocent to telling the
truth,’’she said, grandly.
And speaking of strange things women do,
how’s this for queer? “Dear Sir.—l am ijiviwj a
private entertainment next Sunday, and I shall be
obliyed if you will kindly forbid any mention of it
to be made in your paper .” "That came into all the
newspaper offices in town last week. Among, the
rest, The Bulletin got its copy. But what has
the poor Bulletin done? It ndver made any
allusions to birth-stains. The note wound-up
with : “ As l cannot imayine that the privatedoinys
of nip family can be of any interest to the ycneral
public,” etc. By a strange coincidence, that is
what nobody else can imagine either.
And while on the subject of childish things,
there's nothing later than the “ Babes’ Book Tea.”
All the Sydney infants, in and out of arms, are
rolling up to these in their perambulators with
book-fever burning in their little eyes. Have you
heard of the record child ? She was 10, and
elected to carry an apple in her hand. “ What
are you, Thithie ?” lisped Gwennie. “I’m ‘The
Origin of Thin,” lisped Thithie.
I have just heard of a new and interesting in-
terprovincial engagement. Miss Ethel Marian
Clarke Marcus For-the-Term-of-His-Natural-
Life Clarke’s youngest daughter—is going to marry
Mr. E. E. Ridge, of Greyfells, Manly, shortly.
And a very “Society” engagement is that of
Mr. Mahaffy, Tho Art of Conversation’s son, to
Miss Enid Boyd, who is staying at the Australia
for the present.
A concert to raise the wind for the Seamen’s
Mission drew a fairly-well-paying house to the
Town Hall last Thursday. That great White hall
takes a lot of filling. An audience can leave
much blank space between the seats that hold
its opera-cloaks, and still be a paying con-
cern. A good round sum was netted by
Thursday’s concert. A big and gaudy V.R.
party attended. Lords Beauchamp and Bras-
sey, with their respective aides in white revers and
brass buttons, the Chief Justicess and her daugh-
ters, the Mayoral trio—Mayor, Mayoress and Miss
Harris (which never separates, and on the whole,
looks about the best-satisfied family party in Syd-
ney), also the Primate, and the cunningest little
curate that ever stepped out of Mrs. Oliphant’s
muffin-teas, filled up the frontal arm-chairs. Rev.
Harry Morgan, whose Oxford clays haven’t spoiled
him for the Mariners’ Church work in Lower
George-street, worked up the concert in con-
junction with Mr. Trelawney and Mr. Aengen-
heyster, secretary and manager respectively.
As far as the programme went, it might have
gone not quite so far with advantage. There were
rather too many lady singers, for one thing. Miss
Rooney’s sudden rush into popularity showed no
signs of ebbing; she was warmly encored. In
looks, she is rather like Madam Alva ; slight and
dark, with a sweet expression. Her contralto
voice is easy to understand. It pleases you at
once, and settles down inside you, and you don’t
want to ask any questions about it. It isn’t the
sort of voice to set up a heated discussion in the
papers, but it is plaintive and pretty always.
Hetty Holroyd, in pale pink, was listened to with
much interest, after a recent D.T. correspondence
re herself and Amv Castles. I can see now that
Miss Holroyd hasn’t the peculiar, effortless, gush-
ing way of singing that characterised Amy Castles,
who got at her notes without seeming to climb
ladders to arrive there. But for sweetness, and a
sort of erystalised purity, Miss Holroyd leads by
a length. Miss Ruby Irene Marks was the third
vocalist, and the first in the order of frocking.
She was in a pale pink silk, with a lot of chiffon
about her heels, and also got in her little
encore. Miss Millie Wynn was the fourth ; Miss
Ethel Godfrey the fifth. But the one substantial
music that you could hold on to, and abide by
without any sacrifice to your aesthetic soul, was
Herr Vollmar’s well-conducted ’cello ; for Law-
rence Campbell was too ill to appear, and the lady
who filled his place forgot her words, and could
not be heard beyond the middle of the house. The
rest of the concert is more or less a blank.
At Resell ill, on Saturday, everyone was asking
about certain overcoats. The technical name is,
I am told, “ It’s-raining-in-England.” Which is
all very well when it is rain' g, md well away in
Enr/land , but on a fine way, and at our
own Robe hill, what’s the whichness of the
why of these overwhelming garments ? They
are made in light-tan, and come down to
the heels, where they flap at every step. But
that’s nothing. It’s the strange similarity between
all the men who wear them that strikes one. And
also the —well, the peculiarity of these similar-
looking men. They all have round faces, round
eyes, round mouths, round noses. Their little
heads are sunk into big felthats down to the
ears. All their boots are declamatory tan. And
they hook their sticks into their outside pockets ;
or—and this is the only point at which there is
any “or”—hang them on to a black ribbon slung
across the waistcoat. Where does it come from—
this weird, new race? I am wondering if it is the
New Man come to run away with the unappro-
priated New Woman.
The long, moulded frocks are as mystifying to
men as the apple dumplings were to King George.
In town, the other day, a woman of lost, crossed the
road in an eelskin costume of palest tan, which is
even a more magnifying shade than white. It
fitted her without a pleat, a button, a hook or an
eye—as far as.a mere masculine mind could judge.
Two men stood petrified at the vision. “ How on
earth did she get into it? ” said one. “ It doesn’t
matter how she got into it,” said the other, “but,
in the name of Heaven, how is she going to get
out of it?” Just, so ! The woman who can excite
that wonder is not dressing herself in vain.
Melbourne fashions v. Sydney again. A Mel-
bourne tailor-made skirt sent over to a Sydney
maiden was so lop-sided and baggy that she took
it to a Sydney tailor for inspection. He said,
“The cut is ail wrong.” She wrote off to the
Melbourne tailor to tell him that. He replied,
“We might state that the expert you got
to examine the skirt is incorrect in his statement
that it is made on a model of two seasons ago. The
model is one of this season’s and that the most
popular in Melbourne. In fact, the newness of the
shape may be the explanation of your expert’s want
of knowledge of what tins season’s shapes really
are.”
The New Woman went into Adams’s Marble*
Bar the other day. She walked up to the bar,
ordered her .drink, and.drank it off coolly. Then
she turned to inspect the place, ala visitor. She
was as cool as a cucumber ; walked round, stared
slowly at rtl 1 the nudities on canvas, then wentout
with ladylike nonchalance. Several men vowed
they mightily admired her pluck. Possibly a new
species of feminine pluck comes as a relief to jaded
man.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Tho marriage last week, at Toorak, of the
daughter of Mr. Jenkins, clerk of Yic. Leg. Coun-
cil, was a semi-political function. All the Leg.
Councillors who were in town were present;
guests could hardly move about for the bulging
waistcoats that blocked up the doorways and
corridors. Jenkins pure is personally popular, so
his friends rolled up in force. His daughter, who
has married a Bombay man, is tall, fairish and
brainy—has almost as many accomplishments as
inches. Her pa reckons he has a good show for
the clerkship of the Federal Senate.
“No joke, ’tis said, mapping out the future for
a local prodigy. You can’t pluck and transplant
a budding. Genius without ‘by your leave’ to a
possibly thorny parent-stem. ‘ How sharper than
a serpent’s tooth it is to have a thankless child.’
And a thankless Pa is just as much of a nuisance
to a gifted juvenile and the latter’s well-wishers.
“If the era of musical ‘discoveries,’ Castles-
costumes, and unsophisticated back hair is to last
much .longer, Melbourne’s only Beano Scherek
will die of suppressed laughter one fine Saturday.
He must have gone very near to it last Saturday.
In the afternoon, in trotting ‘little Miss Celia
Dam pier ’ across the Town Hall platform, he had
to travel on his toes to make himself look as tall
as the cheeild, who showed about twelve inches of
maidenly leg below the hem of her smock frock.
Now, we didn’t need the still, small voice of
Celia's violin to tell us she is more than thirteen
next birthday. In the evening the volatile Polish
gentleman was called upon to keep a straight
face all the time Lili Sharp had him in
tow. A pianoforte acoompanimentist seems very
much in tow, at ballad concerts especially
concerts where Native Talent is making a fashion-
able debut. Herr Benno played his part with
easy gravity for some time, but what with the
weird innocence of Miss S.’s dramatic soprano
doll-dress, and the recalls, and the vegetable
tributes, and the artlessness of her selecting a
bunch of violets—a simple little bunch of violets
to carry in her hand —it was no wonder that poor
Benno’s sense of humor got the better of him at
last. When his young friend was about to fire the
second barrel of her double encore at the audi-
ence, and he sat down to the piano to give them
warning of ‘ Home, Sweet Home,’ the musician
was observed to make a most horrible grimace, to
prevent himself from tumbling off the music-stool,
no doubt.
“Madame Marchesi—whose opinion in this
matter goes for a good deal more than Melba’s
enthusiasm—is said to predict a splendid success
for Regina Nagel. The nervous girl must have
been marvellously ‘ brought on ’ after she went to
live with Madame, and had her tonsils removed.
The new Regina, says her instructress, is a truly
French artiste. As for her voice—the voice that
sang ‘Ben Belt ’ behind the scene in ‘Trilby’—
there never was any doubt about its natural rich-
ness and sympathy.
“The 14,000 crowd at Amy Castles’ Exhibition
concert has been eclipsed by the crowd which
rolled up to welcome R.C. Archbishop Carr on
his return to Australia after 10 months’ absence.
From 20,000 to 2,'), 000 people sat or stood
about the great barn-like building, and for two
and a half mortal hours did the portly prelate
stand with his right hand outstretched while
thousands of men and women passed to kiss his
ring. It was a real feat of endurance for a man
far past middle age to stand in the one position
for so long, and afterwards to use his voice to the
utmost in half-an-hour’s speech.
“ Dr. Carr has the royal gift of memory for
faces. Amongst the thousands who passed in
front of him to pay him homageat his reception at
the Exhibition, lie let few pass whom lie had pre-
viously known without a few kindly words. But
no doubt his wonderful memory is a powerful
factor in his popularity, united as it is with so
much tact and graciousness.

Mother Church.

There’s a lot of human nature even in learned
women who hang out brass-plates.

“Another nice kettle-of-fish through want of
Ma’s consent. A Melb. convent-girl chose the
man—one from the grazing country due West—
and asked her old black-shawled teachers to pray
for light. He was a holy High Churchman. Ma
remained obdurate ; the good young man clung to

were made one at St. Peter’s Church (Heaven
forgive them !), and her cousins and aunts stood

“The clothes in ‘The King’s Musketeer’ im-
prove on acquaintance. Cecil Ward doesn’t
darken eyebrows, Ac., enough to live up to the
red velvet and blue satin of his uniform. His
hair’s mousey and mammy-cut, too. But his
comrades-in-arms are gorgeous swaggerers, espe-
cially in their golden-brown plush cloaks. Hen-
rietta Watson looks one of the snappiest sinners
on record, in her Court-gown of white and black
satin, with huge collar of silver gauze, silver
circlets in her raven hair, and a mass of white
plumes worn to one side.
“ Rose Musgrove, as the young Inm/cent, strikes
a cheap modern note among the grandiose dress-
symphonies. The redingote of her grey cashmere
shows a lining-skirt underneath, just faced with a
slice of grey. She’s still sharp-edged in her
methods, though pretty in the pale-pink polonaise
of her last scene. Queen Emelie Hughes, as
usual, draws down her lips, in tragic moments,
as if she had taken on board a mouthful of
scalding porridge. And King H. Hill, in
prune satin slashed with pink, and ivory frilled
with yellow, looks so waspish, despite his fine
wear, that you’d like to take a bat and knock his
supercilious white-stockinged legs from under him.
“ A very funny incident occurred at Prof. Mar-
shall Hall’s fourth lecture on ‘ The Essential in
Art.’ To illustrate the passages of music referred
to, a piano has been put on the platform. The
Prof, came, to a part when he desired to illustrate
the soulfulness that could be put into a chord.
‘ Take, for instance, ’ he said, ‘ the deep yearning,
the ardent, passionate longing, Wagner has ex-
pressed in the opening chord of “Tristram and
Ysolde.” ’ He struck the chord. The instrument
gave out a bundle of metallic discords like the
noise made by a cat walking over the slack strings
of a banjo. ‘ Good Heavens ! ’ gasped Marshall
Hall. He opened the top of the piano and fished
out a heap of old newspapers, dusty music,
and—a packet of discarded luncheon-sand-
wiches. ‘This comes of having a hired piano,’
he said. Then lie tried to illustrate a chord from
the Sonata Pathetique, but it sounded like drop-
ping pennies into a Salvation Army tambourine.
Tho Prof.’s hair all but stood on end. He had to
give up the illustrations, and filled the gap by a
discourse of how a man feels when in love, and
how he falls in love at different periods of life.
“Janette Michie, the unmarried daughter of
the late distinguished Q.G., and his nurse until he
died, is a girl of character. Before her father’s
illness took up all her time; she distinguished her-
self by lecturing on economic subjects at various
working-men’s clubs in Melbourne, advocating
views which were very shocking to her wealthy
friends. Not long since, noticing that the big
family, mansion at St. Kilda wanted re-painting,
she determined to do it herself. So she hired
ladders, bought quantities of'paint, and “did up”
the place from roof to ground. Few people know
Ruskin as. Miss Michie does ; thei house-painting
was in the spirit of the professor, who. turned his
Oxford art-students on to making, a road, so that
they might learn something about useful work.
“ A question of interest to parsons with at-
tractive sisters-in-law; is just now being dis-
cussed in Melb. diocese. A clergyman holding a
living in a remote country town has announced
his engagement, to that old bone of contention, his
deceased wife’s sister. By the law of the land
such a marriage is legal. The laws of the Church,
which laws all Anglican clergy swear to observe,
forbid it.

Laziness Breeds Evil.
True as this is in general life, it is equally true
that a sluggish and inactive liver is the cause of
innumerable evils and complaints, such as jaun-
dice, indigestion, furred tongue, sour stomach,
flatulency, bitter oily taste in the mouth, frequent
headaches, pain in the right side or under shoulder-
blades, costiveness, general depression, a sleepy,
tired feeling after every meal, restlessness, if not
total inability to secure natural sleep at night, &c.
If any of these symptoms are present, it is evi-
dent the liver is not acting properly, and Warner’s
Safe Cure should at once be employed, being the
best tested remedy for liver disease in Australia.
A few doses will prove this statement.
Dr. Robert Shaw, L.D.S., M. 8., D.A., Rock-
hampton, says : “ I have much pleasure in stating
that I prescribed Warner’s Safe Cure very fre-
quently whilst acting as medical officer on the
Cairns-Herbarton railway contract, and obtained
more satisfactory results from it than any formulae
of my own prescribing.”*

strict rule in those matters. I never had any
dealings in land, or other property, except as

community. My despatches were my sole title-

It will be news to most people that Hori
Grey was once mentioned as a candidate for the
crown of Greece, but took the matter as a joke.
“In the Far South,” he said, “ there was nobody
to lead, whereas Greece had men sufficient to
mould her destinies.”

“Goodness Gracious!” where diggers would say
—well, something else.

“ H.” : Michie, as a barrister, was apt to
be overrated. He was deficient in concentration,
never a safe man at the Bar, if pitted against a
Fellows, or even a Dawson —lawyers who gave
their whole intellects to Business. The lawyer
who is such a fine journalist, caustic politician,
brilliant causeur, profound classic, and what not,
as Michie, is bound to suffer. His compeers roar
over his wit, but reflect that they are the safer
men. So do clients, and attorneys. Therefore,
Michie’s position at the Melb. Bar never satisfied
him. Fellows would mop up twice the coin.
Aspinall shockingly bungled some cases. I remem-
ber his apologising, in a newspapor letter, express-
ing his grief, because he thought he had potted a
client.
THE APPLE OF DISCORD.
MR. ALFRED DEAKIN : “ There's a nice treat for those dear little boys!”
Coincidence, or what? At exactly the
time Bravo’s Melb. Cup was being started, owner
“ Billy ” Jones, his wife, and some friends were
in a Paris theatre, and the very first character to
come on the stage wore a costume of black and
yellow. “Don’t you see?’’said Billy excitedly.
“See what?” “My colors; my colors!” he
whispered desperately, “ and Bravo hasn’t a 50-to-
-1 chance.” But Bravo won all right, and Jones
nearly pulled the hotel to pieces when the cable
arrived in the a.m. But, after all, coincidences
chiefly come to those who are looking for them.
Mrs. John Doyle, dead at Glen Innes, was
the mother of the first white child born in that
district. Glen Innes was then a place of but three
or four bark huts. The Doyles arrived in Sydney
in 1844, and went North to station life. John,
who is still alive, formed Bookahramguy Station,
some of the aborigines on which were included in
the historic white man’s revenge poisoning case.
Arsenic was put in the blacks’ waterholes. Seven-
teen dead gins and picaninnies were found at one
hole. Arrests were made and attempts at con-
viction repeated, but definite proof was not forth-
coming and the crime has remained unavenged.
“E.D.” writes :
Dear Bulletin. —In answer to somebody: Thatcher,
the Bendigonian topical warbler, hung out at Heffernan’s
Hotel from ’54 to '56, where he was associated with
Madame Carandini, Mrs. Sylvester, Miss Urie, and other
singers dear to the hearts of the diggers of the fifties.
The heroine of the Bullock Greek incident (which I told
in The Bulletin years ago) was Mrs. Sylvester. Thatcher
left Bendigo for Maoriland shortly after the dates men-
tioned above, and his mining friends in Australia never
heard of him again. One of his books of songs is to be
seen in Melb. Public Library, amongst the pamphlets in
the department devoted to Australian material.—E.D.
Another of those German patriots who
found it better to leave the Fatherland suddenly
and proceed to the ends of the earth by a circuit-
ous route after the Revolution has just died in
S.A. H. B. Holzerland, standing 6ft. 3in., was
the most imposing figure of the strange little
band of immigrants. Holzerland was an archi-
tect by profession, but, like most of the others, he
got hold of a bit of good country and gave it his
attention. Botanist Schomburgk, for a genera-
tion lord of the Adelaide Botanical Gardens, was
me of the party ; another was Dr. Muecke,
ather of the Adelaide merchant and B. H. Prop,
lirector.
Irreverent Australian papers frequently
alluded to Gov. M‘Gregor as “Governor Bill.”
It is interesting to find that there was Papuan
precedent for the custom. Danimkohler, an
Austrian scientist, lately travelling in New
Guinea, says the Tugeri tribe always spoke of
the Tate administrator as “Governor William.”
Danimkohler further tells how the natives mis-
took him for a missionary, the chief reques ing
him to say prayers and read the Scriptures. The
traveller hadn’t any Bible, so he used to read to
the nigs, stanzas out of his pocket Byron. The
Papuans were hugely edified, and usually departed
exorcised by “ Don Juan ” into a holy calm.
Premier Kingston, who is engaged just
now in fighting S.A. Leg. Council, has military
blood in his veins. His grandfather, Colonel
Cameron, from whom he takes his second Chris-
tian name, served with distinction in the Penin-
sula, during which campaign he made a runaway
marriage with Ludivinia da Silva, daughter of a
Portuguese nobleman. Her daughter married
Premier Kingston’s father. And Kingston says
he is ready to shed all his Cameron blood in
securing adult suffrage for S.A. Council!
Vic. Speaker Mason’s ear was first tickled
with mention of place, power, and pay in 1871.
In this way : His bosom chum, Tom Healy, lived
next door to him in Carlton, just when Mason
had first got into the Assembly, and C. G. Duffy
was going to form his Ministry. One morning
Mason got a note from Duffy, asking him to call
on him at Parliament House at noon. Mason
ran and showed the note to Healy. “By George !
old man, you’re fixed up,” says Healy. At 11.45
F.C.M. hailed a cab and drove to Parliament
House. Was shown in to Duffy, who talked of
all things in the heavens above and- the earth
beneath and the waters that are under the earth,
except office, which was the only thing Mason
had a bit of interest in just then. “ I—l’m going
home to dinner, Mr. -Mr. Mason,” said Duffy, in
his cracked, hesitating voice—“ will you walk
towards the station with me?” Of course he
would. And so the pair trotted together to
Prince’s Bridge, and Duffy was going to bid
Mason “Good day,” when Francis Conway gently
mentioned the morning’s letter. But Duffy knew
nothing about it. Mason produced it. “ It's a
forgery—-a forgery, Mr. Mason,” cried Duffy,
hastening to catch his train. Healy had written
it for a lark.
A good way to secure posthumous fame
seems to be to hook your name on to a law-con-
solidation. Justinian did it successfully, and
saved himself from the degradation of going down
the centuries as the mere husband of Theodora,
the bear-keeper’s daughter. Napoleon did it—and
his Code is not the least part of the good and evjl
work he did. Alfred Stephen, in N.S.W. (crimi-
nal law), and Higinbotham, in Victoria, also
managed to do it. Query : Whose name will be
attached to the present valuable consolidation of
N.S.W. statutes, which has given us the Consoli-
dation of Laws regarding Conveyancing and Pro-
perty, Bills of Sale, Evidence, Wills, Probate and
Administration, Weights and Measures, Bank-
ruptcy, Municipalities, Registration of Deeds,
Quarantine, Employers’ Liability, &c. ? R. E.
O’Connor, Q.C., began it when Minister for Jus-
tice, though his Consolidation Commission failed,
being too unwieldy. Barrister Heydon is. now
superintending the work, and will probably be
identified with it in the future. The thing was
started in N.S.W. bv a suggestion from a member
of the first N.S. W. Lab >r-party, who sent a report
of a lecture he had delivered at Leigh House on
the subject, with an urgent request to R. E.
O'Connor to take it up. From Leigh House to
Heydon—the way is long !
N.S.W. Leg. Council presented a Siraon-
etti-carved bust to its President, the urbane
Lackey, the other day. As a President, Lackey
is almost perfection. As Chairman of Committees
in the Lower House his suavity and self-restraint
were similarly marked. He is in his 69th year,
is a Parramatta native, and was made a J.P. by
Gov. Fitzroy 47 years ago. He is the seventh
President in the 40 years’ life of the Council.
Rev. George Robinson, 8.A., the priest
who has been acting as entrepreneur to Amy
Castles, is a born musician. He is the son of a
Dublin architect and was a Presbyterian in the
old country but went over to Rome. His head-
quarters are the S. Melb. R.C. Church, where good
music has always had a show. It was there that
Giammona’s Mass was first performed. Giammona
died mad some years ago at Cook’s River Asylum,
Sydney.
N.S.W. Justice Stephen, now in his 71st
year, has been practically fifty-four years in the
courts. He left school at the age of 17 to become
associate to his father,. the late N.S.W. Chief
Justice. He passed from there to the Bar at 21,
and at 59 ascended the Bench. It is almost for-
gotten that, while at the Bar, he cultivated a
somewhat amende political ambition and was
returned for Mudgee. But politics tired him,
and after two years of the worry he let go.
Marconi, the inventor of wireless tele-
graphy, is half an Irishman, and spends some of
his time on the ould sod. Also, he’s entitled to
be endued with complete Hibernianism in virtue
of his being related to John Jameson, of whisky-
fame. O’Marconi’s mother was born in County
Wexford, and married her Italian singing-master
or something. Another well-known Irish-Italian
name was that of Bianconi, who so long contracted
for the carriage of all H.M.’s mails in Ireland.

WHERE THE SOBER MEN HANG OUT.
SWAGGY : “ Any show for a job here? ”

“R.1.P.”: Councry doctors mostly have
some experience of “ tight places. ” Knew one
who was sent for to see a young man (epileptic)
who had taken a “ bad turn.” Ten miles out in
the bush he arrived at the two-roomed hut, and
found the patient in bed—a stalwart young fellow
6ffc. 3in. high, and 15st. weight. The medico
squeezed into the narrow space between bed and
partition, and had begun to ask the usual ques-
tions, when the patient suddenly jumped out of
bed, took his stand in the doorway, and remarked :
“ The time has arrived.” “What time?” said
the astonished doctor. “ The time for me to offer
up a sacrifice for lost souls ! ” was the answer.
“ Indeed,” said the doctor, feeling none too com-
fortable, “and what are you going to sacrifice,
Bill?” “ You,’’ said Bill, glowering at him
wildly. “ Pooh ! nonsense, man,” said the
wily doctor. “ You haven’t got a knife. Better
go and get one! ” Bill went, and the doctor
hurried out to the old father and told him that
Bill wanted him in the kitchen—thinking that, if
there was to be any sacrificing, it might as well be
kept in the family. Two days later Bill was
admitted to Kew as a dangerous religious maniao.

** “ G.M.” : Re query (8., 29/4/’99) as to
how long native bear can live without water.
Many bushmen say that it does not drink water- —
in the ordinary sense—obtaining sufficient mois-
ture in its diet Jof young leaves. Kept a half-
grown one for months, but never saw it drink,
though it would eat freely from the hand, answer
to its name, and sedately follow me about. Let
it free in the timber at last, but for weeks it kept
on coming back. Can anybody say in what parts of
N.S.W. the blades used the name “koala” as
spelt in Australian Reading Books? Was reared
where blacks were numerous, and where most
settlers had a fair knowledge of native names, but
always heard the name pronounced “ kob-la.” Has
Coolah, on the Talbragar, any connection here-
with ?
* * * *
How long, under the most favorable condi-
tions, can a dog live without food or water ? A
Wentworth (N.S.W.) butoher locked up his re-
frigerating chamber for the winter. Shortly after-
wards a cattle-dog was missed, and it was con-
cluded he had been poisoned. Exactly five weeks
afterwards the chamber was opened for the in-
spection of a visitor, and the dog was found, alive
but unable to walk.

“ H.P.S.” : I live in a country town, and
sometimes combine dentistry with other painful
methods of earning a crust. Last week a man
came in, face like a full moon. Wanted a tooth
out. Pulled it. “How much ?” he asked. “Half-
a-crown,” I answered. Picked up greasy old
slouch hat, hunted in lining, produced seven 2d.
stamps—handed them over. “ That ain’t enough,”
he said, his tongue in the spot lately occupied by
the molar. “ Not half enough ! But next time I
come in I’ll bring you a young jackass ! I reckon
that’ll more’n fix it! ”
• » * *
“ C.J.” : On a station up Ooonabarabran
way, I shared with another hand a hut containing
two bunks, raised about 18in. from ground. Was
awakened one night by' a hissing Tsound, and,
striking a match, discovered two big black snakes
on the floor. Roared out to old “Tim,” and ex-
citedly asked what was to be done. “ Done? ” he
growled. “Go to sleep, you fool — that's what's to
be done. They come here ’most every night, and
go away when they’ve had a rummage-arunnd
after mice.” The snakes did go awayafter a time,
but I told the boss next morning that, unless my
sleeping-quarters were shifted, I didn’t think my
constitution was equal to the strain of work on his
station.

‘ ‘ Ellerdale ”: Re jibbing horses.
.Recently saw horse jib with a Botany
(Sydney) Chow who simply backed
the moke down an incline into a
shallow waterhole. On coming
out horse went on all right.'
Heard that same Chow buys
jibbing horses cheap and cures
them by this simple process.

“ Jon ” : In the bee-line,
Bill Howell isn’t in it with the
Australian blackfellow. When
latter wants to locate a bee-
tree, he catches a bee and
affixes to it a small feather,
which retards its flight so that
he can follow it home. [Device
not peculiar to the Australian
aboriginal.]

A Maoriland Cornish school-committee-
man thus delivered himself lately :
I don’t think our last teacher did right by we at all. I
was on the committee when her came, and us told she
her would get seventy a year. Well, there was more
children came than us’d a-thought for, and the Board
kept a-sendin’ of she bigger cheques till it a-came up to
£l3O. They didn’t a-send the cheques to we, and her
never a-give we any of it back, though her had agreed to
teach for seventy.
Query : How much would “her” have got if the
Board had sent the cheques to “ we.”

“ Bopill ” : Re bush cruelty. Once wit-
nessed destruction of a foal not considered worth
keeping. In full view of the mare, foal was bashed
on head with a nulla-nulla, and the poor mother’s
solicitude was most pitiable. She went rapidly
round and round the foal for a couple of minutes,
smelling it, whinnying gently, and pawing the
ground wildly. Finally she gave an actual shriek,
galloped straight for the paddock-fence, cleared it
and disappeared in the scrub. Afterwards found
she had jumped several other fences; and although
reared on the place, she was not seen for three
years, when she was found 20 miles away.

“Treb”: W.A. black-gins carry a stick
about sft. long and l|in. thick, called a “ wan-
nah,” with which they dig for the “bardies”
(grubs), which feed on roots of shrubs. On these
delicacies, which arelin. to 3in. long and yellow in
color, the natives subsist when other “ grub ” is
unobtainable. With much difficulty I was in-
duced to taste one, and became very fond of them.
They should be roasted on hot ashes till about to
burst, and then eaten hot, Taste like yolk of
fried # „ ,
“Hiram Abiff” : Re production of fish.
There are many “old Indians” in Sydney who
have eaten “chilwa,” a small fish caught imme-
diately after a shower. A place perfectly dry
before will teem with them 24 hours after rain,
and the niggers fish for them with a piece of
dough on a bent pin. Mystery where they come
from. I have even found them on the flat mud
roof of a bungalow. This was in Jallundhur, in
’67.

• * * *
“Ishmael” demonstrates that virtue is
sometimes its own (and only) reward :
Was riding home from the township after 11 p.m. It
was my birthday, but I hadn’t celebrated it very exten-
sively, and was laying plans for future conduct—turn
over a new leaf, and so on—when I came to a dam pro-
vided with pumps and troughs for watering the 10,000
sheep running in'the paddock. Got off to give horse a
drink, and found troughs empty. Crowds of thirsty
sheep were pressing around so I started pumping, and, as
they drank up the water nearly as fast as it flowed, I had
to keep on for half-an-hour before my horse could get a
proper drink. Then looked at my watch, 12.30 a.m. ; and
the sheep as numerous as ever around the troughs. Could
not well go away and leave them gasping, especially after
my newly-formed good resolutions ; so, with a dogged
determination to leave the troughs full, I toiled
on, and when the thing was done it was 4 a.m. Got
to the station dead-tired and overslept myself, so
that it was 9 o’clock when I ran the horses into the yard
A HUMBLE SUGGESTION.
DRIVER : “ Well, I’ve tried ther whip, an’ fire, an’
ther ‘ jingles.' What ’ave I ter do now ter git
’im ter go?”
TRAVELLER (apologetically): “Supposin’ per took
off ’alf the load!”
Manager tackled me at once for being so late, and said he
supposed I’d come home drunk. Now, while running the
horses in, I had come to the conclusion that a good action
loses half its virtue by being made known ; and further,
from my knowledge of my employer in particular, and
Q. station managers generally, I knew that if he had his
mind made up to sack me, telling him about the four
hours’ pumping would not save my job—in fact, he would
only think me a fool for it, and “get on to’’the men
whose duty the pumping was. So I merely said that I
had had but two drinks, and I thought I could take that
amount without getting drunk. “ Come to the office for
your money!” was the answer. And later, that day, I
tightened the girths and steered for the setting sun,
wondering what idiot originated the notion that “Virtue
is its own reward.”
* * * *
Another variety of bush tragedy. A few
days ago a man was driving his waggon down a
mountain road in S. Queensland after heavy rain.
The waggon slipped to the side and ran against a
half-fallen tree overhanging the road. There the
vehicle stuck, with the man pinned between it
and the tree-trunk. Remained there 24 hours in
the cold and rain. Found at last; tree cut with
a crosscut, but the poor fellow died in agony.

“0. M‘A.” : In answer to “J.H.” re
blackfellows’ axes (8., 27/5/’99). The stone is
serpentine, and occurs, in a shattered state,
near granite country. May be found at Garan-
gula, Brindabella, and Carabungla (near Goul-
burn). The blacks used these axes mostly for
cutting nicks in trees as toe-rests when climbing
after ’possum, and also for getting the animal out
when found.
“ Tayan ” on same :
Stone similar to that of which these axes are made is
abundant at a place known as Jones’ Hole, in Rylstone
district. In that locality are long stretches of freestone
rock lying at an angle of 45°, grooved as evenly as if by
machinery, where the axes were evidently ground to an
edge. Once had two almost-perfect specimens, picked up
in same neighborhood. Thirty years ago was informed
by old aborigines of Rylstone district that before rum
was obtainable from the white man, they traded stone
axes with Castlereagh blacks for some root that produced
intoxication, meeting where Mudgee now stands to con-
duct the barter. I have a piece of vellow-box 14in. by
9in. and ljs£in. thick, tapered to handle at one end, which
was found fn a cave on the Upper Colo or Capertee River.
It seems to have been used for carrying fire by the abori-
gines, and was doubtless fashioned with a stone axe.

“H.S.N.” : Since the partial break-up of
the drought in Narrabri district there has been
much disputing over the ownership of stray sheep.
With reference to one woolly the other day I
heard a claimant call attention to his fire-brand.
“ Fire-brand, be banged,” said another—“ that’s a
dog-bite.” “Let him go,” said a third ; “and
when I see how he walks I’ll tell you whether he
is mine or not.”

“A.H.” : M.L. law forbids supply of
intoxicants to Maori women. At one pub.,
during the melting of the grass-seeding
cheques, writer saw the ivahines go to back
bar and buy a dozen of lemonade, while the
tangatas were buying a gallon of beer in front.
Squatted down together in passage and got
noisy on “ shandygaff.”
* * * *
In a small N.S.W. township the inhabi-
tants go shares in a letter-box into which the
coach driver dumps the daily mail, and the
addressees sort the correspondence out for
themselves. One resident proudly subscribed
to a Sydney daily, and was much concerned
to find that its arrival became gradually more
erratic, until at last the paper came no more ;
whereupon the subscriber indignantly wrote
down and withdrew his subscription. His
language can be imagined when a few days
later he found in the box a memo, addressed
to himself—“ Sammy, where the —— is the
Daily Tinpot?”

THE CURIOUS MAN FROM WARRNAMBOOL.
“ I’m not—although I look— a fool,”
The curious man from Warrnambool
Said to a man that came to town —
That oame to join the polished school
That hangs about the station-gates
With winning ways and wily mates,
On watch for men from Warrnambool.
They met the man from Chillagoe
And told him he was in the know.
They put him up to mag and mag
Unto the man from Warrnambool,
Until they got him to a pub
And drank and played—then played the “ rub.”
He lost. (The man from Chillagoe.)
Ab first, of course, the Western fool
Won every shilling in the poor ;
And then the man from Chillagoe
Commenced to win (the spieler’s rule),
Till late the landlord joined the men
And drank and played and drank ; and then
Chucked out the man from Warrnambool.
And now the “ guns”—“ Ha-ha ! Ho-ho 1”
Proceed to count the goodly show ;
Then all at once grow sickly pale,
And rush the man from Chillagoe.
“ By Gawd ! we’re had by country cranks—
The notes is them Provincial Banks !”
And then they “ went ” for Chillagoe . . .
And Chillie “got,” and whacked the pool
With brother Bill from Warrnambool.

WHITE LABEL LAGER
WATSON’S WHISKY.
He was a full-fledged wool-
classer fresh from some technical col-
lege or other, and he used to drop
into the township store and prate
about wool until it was resolved to
test his knowledge. He was handed
a sample of “ clothing wool ” (it was
clean kapok) and asked for his opinion
thereon. After careful examination,
the “expert” pronounced the stuff
to be the very best sample of scoured
clothing wool, and thereupon sat
him down and wrote an elaborate
statement of its value in London,
with an addendum to the effect that
it gave him great pleasure to know
that wool could be so well scoured
locally.

“ Scotty’’ : Re “ F.P.’s ” stric-
tures on M‘Cooey. A 17£ft. skin
does not necessarily prove a
snake. Also, as a 12ft. skin, pro-
perly prepared, is worth about half-a-
sov., what price “F.P.’s” chickens?
A 12-footer would only gorge two at
most —worth, say, Is. each. I did once
see a carpet-snake that extended over
a panel and a-half of fencing. Killed
him, and when I went back next day
he was gone. No tracks, no gorged
kites, but no snake. That was on
Merungle. I give my big-snake
locality.

Dear Bulletin.— Re deafness
of white cats (“C.F.,”8. 8/7/"99).
The family Thomas pure white,
with green eyes—can hear, 250yds.
away, the rattle of the meat-safe, and
is on hand in a few seconds. Know ocher white
cats with usual hearing. Tennyson, therefore, was
wrong in making Sir Gawain (Holy Grail) say, “ I
swear I will be deafer than the blue-eyed cat.”—
Jimmy W. [Tennyson wasn’t wrong. There may
be exceptions, but it is matter of common obser-
vation that white cats with pale-bluish eyes are
stone-deaf.]
* * * *
A big N.S.W. squatter gave a ball, the
guests whereat were all somewhat swagger, with
the exception of a much-respected old boss-cockie
and his wife whom it would have been impolite to
leave uninvited. After much objection, the cookie
had yielded to his wife’s supplication that he
should wear black clothes, and dug out an old suit
which had for years been nourishing moths in the
bottom of a gin-case. Violently cantering round
in an item which the programme described as a
waltz, and not a hurdle - race, a moth - eaten
trousers-seam gave way, thereby revealing
much hairy limb to the shocked assembly._ Mrs.
Boss-Oockie seized the torn one, hurried him into
an ante-room, scrambled him along a passage,
round a corner, and into a distant recess. Arming
herself with needle and cotton, she ordered him to
“take ’em off.” As soon as he had obeyed, the
giggling of several girls was heard approaching.
No time to be lost. Where to hide him ? Ah, a
door close by. She opened it, thrust him out of
sight, and locked him in. Immediately there be-
gan a terrific knocking, and the old man’s voice
was heard in agonising shrieks, “ Maria! Open
the door, quick ! I’m in the ball-room 1 ”

“V.”: The sun has nothing to do with
the “ lean ”of trees. The weight of limbs deter-
mines it in high trees, and stunted trees on plains
lean away from the windiest compass-point, fre-
quently also having roots exposed on that side.
There are also “ Yes-No ” trees with straight
stretches between the leans.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
O. Spardo: Very nice, but then so many hum-
bugs are walking round the world, professedly for wagers,
that no rational person believes in any of them
W.P.S. : An old dodge long practised by newly-
rich bounders desiring to enter what is called
“ Society.” A variation of it is to issue invi-
tations to people who aren’t at all likely to
patronise your pretentious shivoo, and then supply the
papers with the statement: “ Among those invited were
So-and-So.” Of course, they, poor things, couldn’t help
being invited, and they didn’t attend, or even accept the
invitation; but the ends of the bounding host are
served by having his name coupled with theirs

department most wants is power to destroy the
diseased orchards which already exist in N.S.W

The Edina-Excelsior collision in Port
Phillip Bay draws attention to the want of life-
saving appliances on harbor excursion boats. As
is the ease in Sydney harbor, the sinking of both
boats after a collision would mean a death-list like
the foundering of an Atlantic liner.
Why shouldn't N.Q. folk go solid for
Federation ? For the last score of years the whole
province has been ruled by gangs of politicians
scornfully and truly dubbed “Queen-street
ministries for years the dog-in-the-manger
policy has caused trade to drift past Moreton Bay.
Goods are shipped as cheaply from Sydney as
from Brisbane and to the majority of N.Q. people
Sydney is a bigger factor in their commercial lives
than the selfish city up the tortuous Brisbane
River.
A scoffer, to The Bulletin :
“ General ” Booth is a delightful humorist. “ Get the
devil out of Australia,” says he, “ and you will solve the
emigration problem, for people will flock to the land
where there is no devil.” Ungrateful General! If there
were no devil, there would be no work for the Army ; no
coppers thrown on the flag of an evening ; no self-denial
weeks, with their glorious harvest of do lars flowing inti)
headquarters. It is also very doubtful whether people
would really flock to that alleged happy land where the
cloven hoof of Satan had never left tracks, for the afore-
said devil is the creator of most of the pleasant little
variations that ruffle the monotony of a hum-drum exist-
ence. Furthermore, it is notable that most of the nations
that have cut a figure in history have had a big “spice of
the devil ” in them, and individuals without the aforesaid
streak of hell-broth are rarely worth much to anybody.
(For witness whereof may be quoted Olive, Napoleon, St.
Peter, St. Augustine, and numberless others.) Besides,
facts are dead against Booth’s sweeping assertion. As
soon as a man makes enough to be able to go whither he
pleases, in place of cutting off to some virtuous isle in the
ocean, he invariably makes for London, Paris, New York,
Monaco, Vienna, or some other delightful location, where,
by all accounts, the devil is phenomenally active. No,
no, General! No disease—no physician ! No devil—no
brass band—no drum— no offerings—no General!
Jones and Shakespeare.
“ Aletes ” holds forth to The B deletin' :
Apparently the Sydney public can’t or won’t take the
Brouifh Co. seriously. It has become accustomed since
the B~ and B. days to roll up regularly for a good laugh
at anything 1 in the B. line, and resents a change. A play
like “The Physician ” knocks it endways for an act or
two, after which it recovers sufficiently to laugh at any-
thing affording decent excuse and a good deal that
doesn’t. Yet Brough has been educating that same pub-
lic up to an improved taste by a gradual yet continual
process. (Incidentally, the p. has been educating itself
up to R.B. It never really knew its own Robert till re-
centl.v). Note the sequence in the B. repertoire ; pure
farce, farce-comedy, social comedy-broad, ditto, refined
and pathetic, histori-comedy, tragi-comedy, psychologic
drama, pure tragedy (once only). In his mind’s eye the
writer sees, looming ahead, a vision of the co. in a grand
Shakespearean revival—say, “ Macbeth.” One is almost
tempted to opine that Brough could manage the hairy
chieftain too. But could the public be trusted to keep a
straight face ?
To return to “The Physician.” The frequency of
Shakespeare quotations justify one in dragging their
originator before a tired world. For one is set thinking
all the time how he would have handled the twofold
theme that Jones has attempted. The modern dramatist
has achieved much, and the pla}' is strong—in patches.
But one goes away unsatisfied and puzzled to know why.
Where lies the defect? The writer has thought it over,
with ensuing result: The first act is weak, dramatically.
The central figure of the play is the Physician. It is im-
perative that the audience should be in sympathetic
rapport with this character at least, and from the outset.
Yet, when the curtain falls on the first act, what percent-
age can even dimly understand the mental fog that
envelops and stifles the man’s nature? That stagnant
morass into which some temperaments drift almost help-
lessly, when the soul loathes its own cynicism yet cannot
displace it, when life loses even its bitterness (that, at
least, is a tonic) and becomes “flat, stale, and unprofit-
able ? ” How Shakespeare would have made us feel such
a character! And what few yet convincing strokes.
Almost impossible, perhaps, in his day, and yet, after all,
it is his own Hamlet in the atmosphere of the imminent
twentieth century. How would he make us feel it?
Possibly by some powerful soliloquy ; that revelation of
self to self when the brain turns and focusses its search-
light on the soul that moves it. But modern dramatists,
it seems, do not write soliloquies—supreme test of dra-
matic art. Perhaps they dare not.
Later on in the play our sympathies are engaged and
held, but we never recover from that preliminary mis-
understanding, or rather non-understanding. With one
exception full justice is done to the merits of the drama,
and nature is more to blame than art in that exception.
So the drama falls on its own. Belief without regret is
the feeling when the final curtain falls ; one doesn’t want
to go again. And somehow one is driven to the con-
clusion that the reason for that feeling, and for this wail,
is simply—that Jones isn’t Shakespeare.

The destruction of 43 horses carried by the
German s.s. Augsburg shows the folly of carrying
horses on deck. Under the old system of carry-
ing them between decks, and slinging them in
bad weather, the risk is much less.
DESIGN FOR A MENU CARD.
“A dry season out back,"
When Speaker B. S. Bird (Tas.) was de-
posed in favor of N. J. Brown, Henry Dobson
said he voted for “ Nick ” Brown because he was
a school-fellow. Later on Dobson helped to make
Bird leader of Opposition. Dobson didn’t think
Bird was good enough for Speaker, but he thought
he was good enough to follow as a political leader.
Also, the whole Opposition deposed Bird for
alleged partiality when the stone-wall was put up
against the G. W. Railway, but next year they all
submitted to him as a leader who personified poli-
tical honesty and integrity.
Barrister Drake, Q. M.L.A., thus gets
home on the anti-lawyerite in Progress :
When we are asked to remember that Mr. Edmund
Barton, who piloted the Constitution through three
sessions of the Convention, and who has spent his means
and energies in splendid,and happily successful, advocacy
of the measure through the neighboring colony, is a
lawyer, let us also remember that Hon. J. Want is
another member of the same profession. Bet the labors
of the two be contrasted. Mr. Want said he believed
Federation would ruin Sydney, and clung toth Attorney-
Generalship until political decency required him to re-
linquish it, and then contented himself with sending two
lazy, languid telegrams telling the people of N.S. Wales
to save themselves. With a specimen of the Federalist
lawyer and one of the anti-billite persuasion before ns,
we have no hesitation in predicting the position they will
relatively occupy in the hearts of the public when the
present agitation calms down and the history of the
Federation movement is written.


A general meeting of members of Vic. Democratic Federal Union was held
last evening at the Temperance Hall. Mr. H. B. Higgins, M.L.A., occupied
the chair, and there was a large attendance of members.
The chairman addressed the meeting at some length. He said that all
parties had declared the Federal Bill democratic, but he thought they protested
too much If those present wanted to shout with the bigger crowd on polling-
day they had better say “ Yes " to the Bill, but he did not think a man present
would bring himself to say that. (Cries of “ No,” and cheers.) He would
cast his humble negative on principle.

Hidden Tide ” (Is. 9d. post free from Bulletin

—“Margaret”—will appear in next Bulletin
Xmas number.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Estimated that a special free train to carry
Sydney morning papers to Newcastle and Mait-
land would entail a loss to the State of between
Jill,ooo and £13,000 per year.
Queensland, like N.S. W., boasts of one big
anti-Federalist—Nelson. He fills the place in
Queensland that bungler MacLaurin didin N.S.W.
Apart from Nelson the Little Queenslanders pin
their faith to Barlow. Barlow and Nelson were the
two great financial experts who certified to the
solvency of the Q.N. Bank.
As most of the big N.S.W. landowners
have done no good for themselves and very little
for others, isn’t it high time the smaller fry had a
show ? Think of it ! the great bulk of the interior
almost uninhabited while scores of would-be
settlers have balloted from 12 to 20 times and not
yet got hold of a block of land.
Already thei’e is discontent in S. A. Opposi-
tion ranks with newly-elected leader Solomon.
Like his predecessors, Downer, Glynn and Copley,
Solomon cannot “ solidify ’’ the Fat Man ranks.
Kingston says the enemy will only dislodge him
when those composing its forces are formed into a
committee —all of whom must be leaders !
How these curses do come home to roost !
Westralia, demanding a bribe before she enters
the union, obviously follows the lead of N.S.W.
under Reid in his Yes-No days. And it was
surely the irony of fate which caused the same
Y.-N. Reid to be deputed to send the message of
remonstrance to the Westralian Yes-No-Yes.
North Queensland intends to go strong for
Federation. Government and Opposition are at
one in the North on that matter. Treas. Philp,
of Townsville, invited Labor-member Givens to
lecture on Federation in Cairns, the other day,
and Givens cheerfully acceded. And it is in N.Q.
that Democratic feeling is ever most vigorous and
vital.
Yic. Govt, gives certificates to voters at
the referendum to-day (Thursday) as mementoes.
A rather foolish idea, as anti-Billites must partici-
pate, and the document can have no significance
to them. Also, it is to contain photos, of the
Premiers, and will cost a bit. But, then, politi-
cians take kindly to any scheme for the dis-
semination of their printed pictures.
London County Council is buying up
-£4,000,000 worth of property in order to widen
the Strand, open up new streets, and turn
some awful slums into good thoroughfares. Con-
sequently the rates in Marylebone are to be raised
for one year from the present 6s. Id. in the £ to
9s. 3d. ; and after that, they are to stand at 6s. 7d.
And in Sydney, the City of Mean Streets, they
are only Is. 4d. in the £, and a proposal to raise
them ' to Is. 6d., merely to prevent the deficit
growing any larger, was scouted with horror.
ON THE ROAD TO HEAVEN
FIRST TRAVELLER : “ Why is this such a lonely
road?"
SECOND DITTO: “Because there's not a damned
soul on it!"
Reid’s hostility to Federation euchred
Parkes out of the N.S.W. Freetrade leadership
and consequent Premiership ; now his advocacy
of it enables him to retain the Premiership
against Barton and Lyne.
The British Government’s proposal to re-
establish the conscription so far as the militia is
concerned, and make every person between 18 and
35 liable to carry a gun on the more or less gory
battle-field, ends the boast that Britain is the one
European country which is sufficiently defended
by a purely voluntary army. It is 70 years or
more since Britain had a conscription.
Gravely cabled to Australia that “ Mrs.
Schreiner, mother of Mr. Schreiner, Premier of
Cape Colony, and of Olive Schreiner, the well-
known writer, has expressed unbounded admira-
tion for Mr. Cecil Rhodes, and the Imperial
policy supported by him.” There is a most un-
accountable omission to mention the good old
lady’s opinion about flannel next the skin.
If Victoria re-introduces the thin end of
the wedge of denominational education, it will
have to face the old problem : what is to be done
with R.C. teachers. These are among the most
useful of the whole body; and when the State
forces them to say prayers and sing hymns which
they conscientiously disbelieve in, it injures them
without doing any good to their pupils.
Cabled that U.S. Admiral Dewey, late
commander in the Philippines, ridicules America’s
compliance with the disarmament proposals of the
Peace Conference. If Dewey is “ inspired,” this
is a nasty slap. If not, then a New World phase
of military discipline has eventuated. For this
offence (mouthing semi-political utterances) a
European officer of regulars would be cashiered.
South Australia is to vote as one district
for both Federal Houses at the coming election.
She will send in six Senators and seven Repre-
sentatives. The best-known men will win, and so
Kingston, Holder, Playford, Baker, Downer,
Symon, Glynn, and Howe are sure to be among
the chosen. Cockburn has some hope of being
made Federal Agent-General, but not much chance
of getting that billet, for which so many shelved
men in the bigger States will be grabbing.
The topsy-turvydom of politics ! Last
N.S.W. general election, fought on Federation,
the Federal party sent Thomas Walker, ex-Par-
liamentary word-painter, to fight W. J. Ferguson
in Sturt. Poor Walker never had a chance, of
course, and afterwards was helped over to the
West. Now Ferguson has joined the Federal
side and is being gibbeted by his party ; while
Walker, chaired by Vosper and supported by ex-
Vic. veteran C. E. Jones, is pointing out in Perth
what a defective Bill this is, and warning Westra-
lia not to join in—just yet!
Dishonesty has ever marked the religious
crowd in politics, since before the time that the
Israelites borrowed those jewels of silver and
gold from the Egyptians and then quietly
“skipped by the light of the moon.” There
never has been, in Vic. politics, a better example
of this deceitfulness than the move now being
made for the introduction into Vic. State
School curriculum of what are mildly called
a selected prayer, hymn, and passages of
Scripture. Publicly, that is all the
clerical people say they want. Pri-
vately, however, they don’t disguise
the fact that they intend to make
that the thin end of a wedge which
will eventually burst up the secu-
larism of the present education
system. The first piece of auda-
cious deceit is that all the
speakers parsons, parsonic
politicians, and presbytery-
ridden laymen—base the whole
of their arguments for the in-
troduction of this hymn,
prayer, and these passages of
Scripture on purely secular
grounds. They talk glibly about
the necessity of Bible - reading
in order to give the children the
requisite knowledge to understand the
“finest passages in our great English litera-
ture and the most beautiful pictures hung in oui
art galleries.”
The S.M. Herald’s demand to have a
tram put on at 2.30 a.m. for the conveyance
of Sydney newspapers to Ne wcastle and the North
should provide N.S.W. Govt, with a ripe oppor-
tunity to consider the whole question of newspaper
carriage both by train and post. Due considera-
tion must show that there exists not a trace of
reason for treating newspapers in a different way
to any other kind of merchandise.
The election-fight for Encounter Bay be-
tween King O’Malley and ex-Mayor Tucker comes
off to-day (July 29). O’Malley, who supports
Household Suffrage and all the rest of the Govt,
policy, says the district has to choose between
their King and their Tucker —their loyalty and
their stomach. A delicate allusion to the meats
and drinks so lavishly distributed by the friends
of the unseated candidate (Tucker) in April last.
Wickham (N.S.W.) Municipal Council
has struck an idea of considerable tonnage. It is
heavily in debt and is staying there with great
energy. And it wants a new law passed whereby,
if any council levies a special rate for the purpose
of reducing its debt, that good old milch cow, the
Treasury, will grant a -£ for £ endowment—in
other words, that it will pay off half the debt. This
would be a really great idea. If a council levied
an extra rate, and paved the town with the pro-
ceeds, it would pay all the bill itself; but if it
borrowed the money, and then levied a rate to
pay off the debt, Govt, would stand half the
racket. Wickham, by-the-bye, has. a rate of
Is. 3d. in the £. The idea of putting it up to
Is. 6d., and tackling the problem that way, hasn’t
occurred to Wickham.
A Northern correspondent :
Previous successes embolden for another attempt.
The Federation vote on Sept. 2nd in Q. will he a very
close thing and its success or failure depends mainly on
Central Q. Shrewdly estimated that the Brisbane and
Ipswich bundles will show about 8000 majority for the
lit tle Q’landers. The North, roughly speaking, will about
balance this with a big vote for union. 'Central Q.
appears evenly divided and the result is a huge note of
interrogation. In favor of the national cause there is
every newspaper of weight that circulates throughout
Queensland. For the leper and the maintenance of
Japanese immigration and the old Boodle Government
are mainly Brisbane Worker and the Tki.korahi. Fancy
the humor of it —Brisbane Tei.boraph and Worker in
common cause ! If th" workers and voters in Central and
N.W. Q'land take their opinion ready made from the
apostate Labor organ it will go hard with the Bill ; if
they think for themselves, up goes the hat for Australian
union.
During the “ marine scandal ” debate in
M.L. Parliament the hyphenated Jones taunted
Labor-member Hutcheson with having been “ a
handy man on the Hinemoa. ” Which was not
the sort of thing you would expect from a Radi-
cal Minister. If Seddon falls, it will be through
his lieutenants, and Hyphenated Jones is the
worst of them.
Two years ago there were
21,800 names on the Westralian
Electoral rolls; now there are
43,000. And as the increase has
been confined to a few districts,
the need of a redistribution of
Parliamentary seats is most
urgent. The most striking
anomaly is the equal repre-
sentation of Ashburton, with
42 electors, and East Ooolgardie,
with 5674. Seven electorates
in the N.W. —the country of
the “ indentured ” blacks—
have 744 electors among them ;
the four Coolgardie districts
have 14,118. The goldfields
are, of course, the worst suffer-
ers ; Perth and Freeman tie,
where there has been a con-
siderable addition to the rolls,
have eight members for 14,561
electors —twice as many, that is, as
the goldfields have, though the two
have almost precisely the same number of elec-
tors.
Ex-Attorney-General Lewis, in suggesting
that the present Tas. Parliament should simply
refuse. to expire until Federal matters are fixed
up satisfactorily, and should tell the constituents
who elected it for three years in 1897 to go out and
throw apples at themselves, evidently hopes to
avert a row in the Land of Sleep. He argues that,
as Federation will mean a reduction in the num-
ber of members in the State Parliament, the
necessary re-organisation will be much simplified
if there are no newly-fledged members to placate.
The Lewis idea seems to be that newly-elected
members would prove more tenacious of their
seats than the old hands. But as Tasmania
hardly ever elects any new members, that looks a
thin excuse.
“BABIES ON OUR BLOCK.”
“Is it you who have got the new baby in this
street?”
"No, mum—me mother!"
A. J. Thynne has pointedly reminded a
Brisbane audience that the second clause of MTI-
- National-party programme (1888) ran:
“The federation of the Australian colonies into a
united dominion, with provision for a system Of
national defence.” Clause 4of same programme
read : “ The fostering and protection of Australian
industries.” Note the word Australian, not
Queensland.
The new one-rail railway, successfully tried
in Belgium some time ago, and on which specially
constructed trains raised a speed of 100 miles an
hour, has got so far beyond mere experiment that
a railway on this principle is going to be laid down
between Liverpool and Manchester. This is
something for heavily-burdened,Australia to think
over. Australia owes about £120,000,000 borrowed
for railways and telegraphs (in addition to many
other millions for other purposes) and it is quite
content to keep on owing it because it is repre-
sented by “ permanent assets.” But they are not
permanent. Some day wireless telegraphy will
make one of these items only a bad debt. And by
the time Australia owes -£300,000,000 or so for rail-
ways they will probably be superseded by some
newer and faster means of locomotion, and that
will be a useless and unproductive burden also.

ora rn :

THREE MONTHS.
MR. KITEFLYER ( to Isaacs, to whom he has just
given P.N. and who is looking for blotting-
pad): “ Oh, don't worry — it's got vlenty time
to dry.”
Strange that no anti-Federalist thought of
a more plausible yarn than Dibbs’s anent Vic.
money being used to bribe N.S. W. to give a
“Yes” vote. The British bondholder ought to
have been the person named as pouring gold into
the hats of N.S.W. electors. The argument
Dibbs might have used, but didn’t: “ A Federa-
tion offers the best security for interest-payments,
also for principal. Everyone remembers Sydney
Smith’s wail about being slipped up by a one-
horse American province, and the British bond-
holder remembers it better than anybody else,”
&c., &c. This paper turns pale at the thought of
what G. Republican Dibbs might have said, but
didn’t.
The programme of the new N.S.W. ses-
sion reads like a gathering of old friends, long
separated. The Bill for amalgamating the two
Savings Banks, promised as far back as 1894, is
there. The promise to make the new institution
a State Bank of Note Issue isn’t revived, but
something is said about combining with it a State
Land Bank. The Early Closing Bill and the
Navigation Bill are alluded to. There is to be a
measure for amending and extending Local
Government —which has been a good old stand-by
for the past 25 years, though nothing has come of
it so far. The system of old-age pensions is. to be
“ preceded ’’ by a measure providing for compen-
sation to sufferers by mining accidents, but when
the real Old Age Pensions Bill may be expected
isn’t even hinted at. It is merely to be “pre-
ceded ’’ by the other thing—just as the Apostle
Peter preceded Peter Jackson, or the Creation
preceded John Norton.
Federation matters are mixed over in
Westralia. There is a majority of at least three-
fourths in favor of Australian unity. The gold-
fields are solid on the question and the working
men of Perth and Fremantle are largely in favor
of the Commonwealth Bill, so the Groper party is
certain to be rebuffed when the country gets a
chance of speaking. But the Federationists are
badly in want of a leader. At first it was thought
that Forrest would run straight, but he has ratted
and may be counted one of the mdst dangerous
opponents of the cause. Vosper started off with
a great burst of enthusiasm, but found himself in
such uncongenial company that he decided to
leave the Federal League and play a lone hand.
He admits that Federation is the manifest destiny
of Australia, and that is something to go on with,
as the man said when he got 21 years’ gaol. Mean-
while the men who are in the front ranks of
Federation in the West are only second-rate
politicians and parsons. A leader of commanding
ability would win an early and decisive victory.

E. J. Brady’s book of ballads and verses,
mostly from The Bulletin, is now ready ; and
will be sent post-free from this office on receipt of
price, ss.
A magistrate at Orange has dismissed a
charge of assault on a woman-federalist by an anti-
federalist man. They attended a public meeting
when the cause was debated, and when the woman
applauded the man didn’t like it, and vice versa ,
so the pair “got in holts.” The magistrate, a
small local Solomon, recommends that, at future
public meetings in Orange, women should be kept
on one side the hall and men on the other—as is
the case in some churches.
Marshall Hall, discussing “ The Essential
in Art ” at Melbourne Athenaeum, treated
his audience to some casual remarks upon Love,
beautiful Love, which makes the world go round.
“ At about the age of 40 a specially dangerous
form of it is often met with,” said the sapphically-
poetical prof. Why not have quoted Pinero, who
has several times harped upon the subject of
middle-aged passion. “ I know what you are
going to tell me,” says Aubrey Tanqueray to
Cayley Drummle—“that a man at 40 is either a
stoic or a satyr. lam neither.” What a valuable
proverb for men of the world to paste inside
their hats! One should be forearmed against
Nature—at 40.
Mrs. Sarah Wilson, of the Blue Mountain
Hotel, Lawson, N.S.W., has just had her annual
hotel-license renewed for the 62nd time.
Amongst the guests at N.S.W. Governor
Beauchamp’s recent evening party—“ Mr. John
Norton!” “ High, low, and the Jack'" —though
hardly on “all-fours” with John’s previous
attitude.
One of the brothers Longworth, members
of the lucky Great Cobar tribute party, the other
day bought Woollahra House, lately the home of
Mr. W. Cooper, for something under £12,L00.
The Longvvorths (who have an income between
them of well over <£50,000 a year) are investing
heavily in Sydney bricks-and-mortar. One of. the
gilded pair is reported to have already bought
about 150 houses.
The case of the Lithgow (N.S.W.) miners
is another proof of the necessity for a mini-
mum wage-law. Imagine risking your life
and working hard for 30s. per fortnight. Every
now and again there is a strike somewhere
about Newcastle district, and hundreds of women
and children suffer semi-starvation and misery
because some bowelless corporation tries to under-
sell its rivals by reducing its price a few pence per
ton.

Archbishop Carr’s sorrowful insinuation
that the R.C. Church in Victoria would derive
little benefit from James Crotty’s will, over and
above the <£lo,ooo bequest to St. Patrick's Cathe-
dral, has served to bring out the facts of the case.
To begin with, <£13,000 has been already absorbed
by the Church —the extra <£3ooo going to Crotty’s
own particular parish church at Kew (Melb.).
Furthermore, one of the parish priests gets a £3OO
annuity under the will. Thu residue of Crotty’s
estate, after payment of all debts, legacies, and
probate duty, amounts to at least <£65,000, and
the annuities chargeable against the income from
said residue (including the extra <£soo a year
which Archbishop Carr has conceded to Widow
Crotty) leave a balance of more than <£lloo a year
to be received by the Archbishop for the benefit
of the Church in general. Said “ balance ” income
will increase as each annuitant drops out, but
when the last annuitant dies the whole of the
residue capital is to be divided between two R.C.
cT i ties. This ealati ity to the Church may not
i open for 30 or 40 years, and, seeing that Mrs.
Crotty is one of the youngest annuitants, she will
enjoy the Church’s best prayers for her health.
Sydney Telegraph, mean and snarling to
the last, got up last Tuesday under the heading of
“ A Hoeussed Referendum ” to allege that the
N.S.W. vote of 20th June wasn’t a genuine one
after all. The old tales about the “ graveyard
vote ” and personation and the other stock
properties were all dug up afresh, and the con-
clusion is a demand for another poll which will be
“above suspicion.” Probably the Telegraph,
on this occasion, is nearer to the truth than usual.
If there was anything crooked about the poll of
20th June the Telegraph’s party should
know as much about it as anybody. A party
which disseminated so much false literature
probably wouldn’t mind disseminating false ballot
papers also. Likewise, some members of the party
got so low down as to forge tickets for the Barton-
Reid meeting at Sydney Town Hall (an offence
which ought to be good for six months
“ hard ’’), and the rest of the party, including the
Telegraph, hasn’t risen up yet: to denounce the
act; a crowd which resorts to fraud of this
kind probably wouldn’t stop at much. So, if the
Telegraph says that the vote of June 20 was
fraudulent, the only possible, answer is that it
would be very surprising if it wasn’t—considering
the sort of people who were about at the time.
The fact that these people were about can be
proved by their letters to the Telegraph.

• • Bicycle Don’ts for 1899. • •
DON’T buy an auction or sales wheel.
DON’T buy a wheel assembled from parts which are bought at a few
shillings a set.
DON’T buy a wheel because a friend recommended it to you ; the friend
gets a commission.
DON’T buy a Cycle from a factory that does not manufacture Bicycles
exclusively.
DON’T buy a Cycle from a factory new to the business. Unless you’re
paid ; let some one else do the testing.
DON’T buy a Cycle from a dealer who sells a new make every few
months (they’ve no reputation to lose).
DON’T pay a higher price than £l5 for a cycle with outside joints and cup
and cone bearings unless you don’t believe in value for money.
DON’T buy a wheel without a direct factory guarantee. You want
some security for your money.
DON’T buy a Cycle until you have had a “RED BIRD SPECIAL”
shown and explained to you.
The Gooicl Bicycle Co. Limited.
