SPORTING NOTIONS.
■Fifteen thousand people attended Brisbane
Exhibition "Ground last Saturday and saw the British
footballers win the second test-match against Australia
by 11 points to nil. Winners showed vast improvement
on their previous displays. 'Pace and scrutn work of ihe
forwards was of a high order, and the backs rose to the
occasion splendidly. The “down-under” reps, had it all
their own way in first half, and kept the oval in enemy’s
territory the whole time The Australians rallied won-
derfully during last quarter of an hour, and the crowd,
excited by the persistent onslaughts upon the English
goal and the latter’s .stubborn defence, roared itself
hoarse. Each individual player was besought to save the
day, and each responded, but the Englishmen were too
good. No side ever fought harder for victory, and no
side ever deserved it more. This was recognised by a
perfect storm of cheers from the lusty lungs of thousands.
Britisher Nicholls stood out conspicuously, his play and
kicking being perfect. The visitors’ N.Q. trip has done
them a lot of good, their form in the match under notice
being far ahead of that previously shown. It will be
well-tested in Sydney to-day, for the N.S.W. team is a
splendid one and could hardly be improved on, vyhile the
last Australian Fifteen hardly passed muster with good
judges.
* * * *
Rev. Mr. Mnllineux certainly has good cause for
grumbling if it be true that some of the Australian
players were mean enough to call on an opposing player
by name to pass the ball. The ethics of the football field
have never been on a par with those of cricket, but such
downright “ pointing ” as above reduces football form to
the level of professional pedestrianism.
* * • *
During the recent visit to Rockhampton o. Die
English footballers they were invited to p>lo spoits.
Gray, of the team, attended in full Highland costume,
much to the astonishment of the natives. On the
ground he got investing British capital at an under-and-
over table and having gone down suddenly for numerous
sovs. remonstrated with owner of joint for eronk
play when the rev. skipper came along and told the
bonnie laddie that he ought to be ashamed of himself.
Small boy in the crowd : “ That’s right, Daddy, take him
home and finish dressing him.”
* * * •
No better Rugby has been seen in Sydney this
season than that shown in Saturday’s club matches.
Randwick just scraped home 3 points ahead of Sydney,
after an open, even game, the feature of which was a re-
markable run by ex-Britisher Swann for Randwick.
Wallaroo, with all the luck, beat the sturdy Buccaneers
by 9 points to 4 after a hard game, and will battle out the
premiership with Randwick.
» * • *
Australia had all the worst of the match against
Dr. Grace’s eleven. The Englishmen started off with 431
and the Australians, after scoring 301, followed-on with
the inevitable “draw,” losing 3 wickets for 113. A
noticeable feature of the match was Iredale’s innings of
115—the first time during the tour he has shown anything
like his home form. On a form r occasion Iredale started
to make his mark towards the end of the tour, so perhaps
history is to repeat itself.
* * * *
“ Mary Ann ” Noble’s fine effort in the recent
test-match puts him well ahead in the Australian XI.
batting averages, his record being : 31 innings, 6 not outs,
1202 runs—average, 48'08. Hill and Worrall still run
second and third, with 48 and 40 respectively, but Darling
has run up fourth with 3274 for 34 innings, 3 times not
out; and Trumper, Laver, Gregory and Trumble follow
in that order. Trumble still heads the bowling with 88
wickets, at an average of 15 runs each ; Billowel second
and Noble third. In test-matches only, Hill leads with
the bat, with the remarkably fine record of 301 runs for
five completed innings—average, 60'2. The consistent
Noble is second with 48, the same as in all matches;
Trumble and Trumper follow with 44 each, whilst Billowel
brings up the rear in both batting and bowling, with
averages of 3 and 37 respectively.
* • * •
The damage to Lilley’s hand in the Midland
Counties match was bad luck for the Australians, after
all. It can be taken for granted that the “England”
wicket-keeper got instructions to nurse his hand, likewise
he would he advised to look not upon the beer when it is
foaming—if he is a beer consumer, like most oth>-r pros.
Anyway, Lilley came up to the test-match in first-rate
batting form —partly, it may be, through his enforced
rest.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin’. — Every cricket scribe here-
abouts has harped on the fact that Australia would have
won the first test if somebody hadn’t missed Ranji in
the field before he got set. The “draw ” was very “bad
luck ” for the Australians, considering that said Ranji
ought to have been caught, we were told. Will the same
scribes refer again and again to the “ lets off ” of Worrall,
Trumper, and Darling in the fourth test-match? They
“ought” to have been out when they had scored 1,0,
and 3 respectively, yet their aggregate score was 155.
In reports of the same innings, where these mistakes
cost England 151 runs, we find it stated that “the score
was quickly taken to 255, when a serious mishap be'fel
the A ustralians, Noble being held by Hearne off a ball
hit back to him.” Surely it is time that partisans of
either side ceased to talk of luck in the matter of catches 1
Each side in turn makes as many runs as it can against
the other side’s at tack, and the fielding is part of the
attack. When a batsman is missed in the field the attack
is at fault, and any runs which he may happen to make
afterwards are fairly credited to his side and debited to
the other. Who can say how much “good luck” is
included in the composition of a long, “ faultless
innings?” Circumstances conspire to give the batsman
confidence ; the balls that beat him don't hit his wicket;
he gets more than an ordinary share of badly-pit.ched
ones ; finally, he is dismissed by the one fatal ball which
might have settled him for a “duck ” if it had come along
three hours earlier.— General Average.
* * » *
“Jonah” Ranji has done it again for the All
Englanders. He’s a shadow on their path, an omen of
non-success, an un-Hinglish institution, a man to he
avoided. Ranji has now p'ayed ten times for A.E. versus
Australia, and nine times out of the ten they haven’t, won.
Also two famous veteran representative players, one on
either s'de, have lost their reason since they came into
contact with the brown gentleman. He's as disastrous
as the Curse of Deeming.
• * * •
When the first English suggestion of Blanrl or
Bradley as a “representative” fast bowler was cabled
over the names looked ridiculous on last season's form.
It now appears Bland captured all ten wickets against
Kent, and this despite the fact that he was taken off in the
middle of his success, Ranjitdnhji and others having a
turn with the ball meanwhile. Perhaps Bland will be
substituted for Bradley in the final test, which follows
the Australia v. Kent match.
« * * ' *
Still harping on the subject of drawn games.
Grace’s eleven, at the Crystal Palace grounds, won the
toss, and scored 358 runs the first day. Next day they
added 73 to their score, and the Australians followed
with 189 for four wickets. Total number of runs for the
second day—262. Do the Australians play bold cricket,
or do they play against Time as long as they are in any
danger of getting beaten ?
• * • *
Before Trumper started on the English tour,
Australians thought that, allowing the youngster did
nothing phenomenal in the big matches, he would he
sure to repeat his big club scores in the matches against
the weak counties. Strange to say, with one exception,
his greatest successes have been in the big matches. The
exception was his 104 against Gloucester.
Dear Bulletin. — Now that three out of four
test-matches have ended in smoke, and poor old W. G.
Grace has been blamed by Australian partisans for “ de-
liberately playing for a draw” in the first game of the
series, it is time to have another look at the English
system of a three days' limit, criticising it from the
maker’s point of view. The second of these drawn test-
matches would have been easily settled within three days
hut for the weather. Australia scored 172, then England
(battim--one man short) scored 220; Australia made 224
in the second innings, and England got 20 odd runs, leav-
ing some 150 to be made on the third day. The wicket
was unplayable next day and the match had to be aban-
doned It was drawn in favor of England, judging by the
figures up to that point. Unsatisfactory for Maclaren
and co. to lose the chance of winning on a decent wicket,
hut would it have been satisfactory to postpone the play
until thesoddened wicket was fit for the Englishmen to
get beaten on ? The draw, under those ciros., was the
more sportsmanlike ending. The first test-match pro-
duced a draw as the result of Australian tactics. Darling
won the toss, the wicket was good, and his side made
238 runs for eight wickets on the first day. Next day
they increased the total to 252, and the Englishmen went
in with no prospect of winning 'unless they' played a fast
game. They tried to force the runs, and they got out for
11)3. Then the Australians went in again, masters of the
situation, and declared their innings closed (230 for eight-
wickets) at a time which left the Englishmen a choice
between batting recklessly to win or playing carefully for
a draw. Grace, who was captain on that occasion, seems
to have instructed the team to go slow —not as slow as
the Australians had gone on the first day, but slow enough
to defeat Darling’s scheme. As a matter of fact, though
the Englishman (thanks to Ranji) gained their end with-
out- making the game absurdly tedious, they scored 155
for 7 wickets, in the three hours (or less) granted them
by Darling. 825 runs were scored during the match, the
Englishmen, morally beaten on their merits, were not
actually beaten inasmuch as there wasn’t time for them
to lose three more wickets. The Australians had been
too slow over their part of the job. Was the “ draw”
very unsatisfactory to anybody who wants lively cricket
for his money? In the fourth test (also drawn) the
Englishmen won the toss and scored 372 runs on the first
day, with about half-an-hour to spare. Next day the
Australians had the wicket to themselves with a “ follow
on.” There was an interval between the innings, as on
the previous day, and they made 337 runs altogether -35
less than the Englishmen had made in exactly the same
time. Next day the Australians played a contemptibly'
tricky game. They st.on-walled the bowling—the bowl-
ing which had lost its enthusiasm—whilst the crowd
whistled “ Dead March in Saul.” When Darling's side
had scraped another 204 runs together, and 7 wickets
were down in the second innings, he declared it
closed, offering the Englishmen the temptation
to play wildly. There was about an hour and
a quarter (one supposes) left for play, and the English-
men had 165 runs to get. “ Maclaren determined
to make as good an effort as possible, and therefore in-
structed the batsmen to force runs,” says the cable. They
couldn’t forc» more than 94 runs in the time, so the game
was drawn. This third day’s play, even with the assist-
ance of the Englishmen’s spurt, produced only 298 runs.
Unsatisfactory that they couldn’t triumph over Darling’s
facties, yet not unsatisfactory that the “draw” in this
case should serve to bring the system of slow batting into
further unpopularity with the crowd that pays the piper.
Were two good bowling and fielding teams opposed, on
even the best of English wickets, the game could be won
and lost, m 9 cases out of 19, if both sides played a
dashing game with the bat. When rain brings the game
to a standstill, it is fairest for both sides, taking one
case with another, that, a “draw” should result-. And
inasmuch as England is a pretty wet place, the three
days’ limit for matches is a satisfactory rule. If the Aus-
tralians don’t like it let them arrange for victory to rest
with the side that scores the bigger first innings when
the match can’t be played out. In the four test games
this season the first innings result has been a reliable test
of the teams’ relative merits every time. Each team
deserved to win the two games in which it held the ad-
vantage at “half-time.” —Britisher.
* * * *
Melbourne Nickey Peach and Essler (“the
Kaffir), supplied a great baltle at Golden Gate A.C.
(Sydnev) Saturday night. Match said to be for “feather
championship,” but as much haggling occurred in the
ring on the question of weight (Peach was charged with
exceeding limit and looked it, and club undertook to pay
“the Kaffir” 15 in consequence), championship could not
be involved. 800 people paid increased charges, and
filled every inch of the hall. Pace fast and furious, and
thumps savage and severe from “time.” Each brought
tiger-like ferocity to bear in his work, though no mean
advantage was taken. The crowd screeched with excite-
ment-worked itself up to snapping tension. “The
Kaffir” was very open—a straight left would have jabbed
all go out of him, as he’d stop it every time with face or
stomach as the case might be ; but Peach would swing,
and rarely sent in a straight left, though when he did
it brought disaster to the ink-hued target. On the other
hand, had the “ Kaffir” known how to stop and counter
well the money would have been his quickly, for every
time Peach shot out the left he followed quickly with the
right. There was no championship form about either’s
fighting. Once they came together with a great crash,
and the blood welled from a deep gash over Peach s left
eyebrow ever afterwards. Bv-and-bye the optic began
to close through swelling, but one of the seconds (ugh !)
sucked it. Frequently each roughed his opponent to the
boards or ropes, but no fair knock-down occurred till
round 14, when Peach, after landing a smashing left
swing on the throat, shaking Ess er from head to heel,
followed up with another full of vim that settled the
business, though the “Kaffir,” grit to the core, tried to
stem the tide.
* * * •
“Old Timer”: The Peach-“ Kaffir ” battle at
Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney) on Sat. night last was hot from
the jump, and both lads game as they make ’em. Peach was
much too strong and heavy for the black, but the latter
long held his own and looked like winning once or twice.
Spectators were greatly prejudiced against the colored
man, for no other reason ti an that he ivas colored, as a
better-behaved lad never put up hands. As showing the
peculiar ideas of rules and how to conduct a fight some
referees have, I may mention that in the 9th round, and
while the black was getting worsted, his second, wily
old Jim Barron, sought permission to tie the Kaffir’s
shoe-lace, which had got loose. Referee allowed it, and
Jim didn’t hurry over the job ; the spell proved a god-
send. Have often seen referee at G.G.A.O. permit a man
to stand or sit in his corner while a loose glove-string was
being adjusted during progress of a round. Have also
been much amused at same official’s notion of what con-
stitutes a clinch.
* * * *
The Conlon-Goggin fight at Wyalong (N.S.W.),
20th inst., drew a bigger crowd than had ever been seen
there. Miners ready to plank their last dollar on local
idol, Jack Goggin, found few takers, though odds of 6 to 4
and 2tolon in some instances were offered. The Syd-
neyite’s backers didn’t face the music beyond the £lOO
stake-money. Conlon owned the fight from the word
“ go,” but Goggin, weak at the knees, nearly fluked him
in round two—sent a smashing swing home on the point,
and dazed the recipient who went star-gazing, and got
up silly, but managed to elude further trouble till
“corners.” Next 3min. saw Conlon fresh again and
bashing away vigorously, knocking his man from ner-
pendieular to horizontal as fast as he stood up. Ulti-
mately referee interposed and gave Conlon the fight.
Had Conlon’s backers been game they might have won a
pile, for the miners proposed emptying-out on their
favorite, over whose defeat, ’tis said, some actually shed
tears. Conlon fights Mick Dunn for N.S.W. middle-
weight championship early next month, and winner will
probahly meet Tim Murphy (Melb.) who'll give either a
hot time.
* * • ’ f
Seven-footer Pete Felix, who claims to be the
champion pug of Australia to-da.v, was recently chal-
lenged by the hurricane 101st. Tim Murphy. Murphy was
forced in desperation to throw out a deli. to the darkey,
as there was nobody else willing to take him on. Felix,
however, calmly regards the comparatively-diminutive
Murphy with an air of contempt from his lofty altitude,
and demands that he shall have a “ reputation ” first.
Surely the reputation of fighting everv man who comes
along in his province ought to be enough !
* * * •
Geo. Atkinson, who tried to fight “Tot”
Iliggins in Melb. on Saturday night, is a statue-like ten-
stoner—as handsome a vourig fellow as ever got a black
eye. He is strong, plucky, and willing, but will never
make a pug. ; despite the frequent hits he landed on bis
opponent, the latter had the fight won from the shake-
hands. Atkinson wastes much strength in unnecessary
feinting and dodging, fights flat-footed, and has no get-
away. In the llt.h round, a left swing got home, and
Atkinson didn't open his eyes until a full 2min. after
being counted-out.
* * * *
A recent Bulletin par referred to Harry
Sallars, the veteran black pugilist, as if he were still on
deck ; whereas the old man passed out at Melbourne
Benevolent Asylum nearly 13 years ago.

A promising three-year-old seen at Rosehill on
Saturday was Meribah, who calmly helped himself to the
Maid- n Race. He i 3 bv Realm from Miriam, and is thus
a half-brother to Sam Hordern’s grand little mare Mirella,
and is much like her—only bigger.
Young “ Bill ” Kelso snared a profitable double
at Rosehill with Amourette and the ancient Robinson
Crusoe gelding—The Captain. For the Australian Han-
dicap the old roan nag Surprise was made favorite on the
strength of a record-gallop, whilst pots of coin went on
Djin Djin and Whakawatea ; but. the black mare, starting
at I6’s, couldn’t go slow enough, and got home before the
pony Djin Djin, with Virginius, another outsider, third.
Both of the old stasrers, Whakawatea and Surprise, got
lost in the crush. The Captain won liosehill Handicap
with his head in the air.
* * * •
For Sydney, Saturday’s hurdle-race at Rosehill
was a first-class event, four nags being well backed, and
the first three finishing within a few lengths of each
other. The sturdy little black mare, Ladylike, repealed
her performance on the same track of a few weeks back,
and easily downed the bottled-up Bertie with short-
tailed old Honeydew 11. a good third with 12st. up.
“Bill ” Kelso’s Murrabrine was, for some unknow n reason,
made favorite, but didn’t run well enough to give him a
chance of winning even a Sydney hurdle-race. All the
same, he probably will win one.
* » * *
“Ware Hawk”: See by dailies that Ikey has
again got hold of the large ear of the gullible turf writer
and through him is booming certain horses into promin-
ence for Caulfield and Melb. Cups by announcing that
they have already been largely supported. This business
should be sufficiently transparent for the bulk of punters,
especially after previous experiences. It’s not Ikey’s
game to put the public on to a legitimately-backed thing
—-he wants to lay something that isn’t in demand by the
right people. The reported backing of Iramoo for the
recent G.N. Hurdles, and Forest for Caulfield Cup are
cases in point already. Big lumps were said to have gone
on the latter—who is going to India within a day or two,
if he is not already on his way there. Lancaster also,
said to have been heavily invested upon for Caulfield and
Melb. Cups, started favorite and ran behind eight or nine
others in Caulfield 2-year-old race last Saturday.
* » * *
Corowa (N.S.W.) racing club is kicking vigor-
ously, and getting other country clubs into line,
against the A.J.C.’s proposed new regulations. Through
getting nothing in return and receiving absolutely no
consideration whatever, they won’t pay the guinea to be
demanded yearly' for registration. The rule that, when
the prize for a hurdle race does not exceed £101), £TO
shall be paid for a winning mount, is treated as the richest
of jokes in light of the fact that more often than not the
prize for a country hurdle-race is under tlO; but writer
has ascertained that an attempt will be made to have the
operation of this regulation restricted to the metropolitan
area—the puerility' of the thing having become apparent
to one of the committee. Corowa club is arranging a
meeting of delegates from all country race clubs to con-
sider matters mentioned, as well as several reforms—non-
registration of meetings providing less than £5O a-day
added money', an expert inspection of all registered
comses, appointment of stipendiary stewards to report
upon and inquire into all matters appertaining to
racing, &c.
* * * *
Cabled that Australasian champion-swimmer
Fred Lane swam second to record breaker Jarvis in Eng.
880yds. championship, at Brighton (Eng.). Winner’s
time, 12min. 48 3-ssec.; Lane’s, 13.2 ; latter led by a foot
at 500, but stopped several times afterwards. The despe-
rate struggle in first half probably' hroke Lane up, but
he’ll do better as he gets down to the changed conditions,
and may be relied upon to show up well at distances
under 500yds.
* » * *
Mr. Mark Foy’s new Donnelly boat, the
Southern Cross, built on En. lish lines with the object of
meeting the Maid of Kent in England, sailed a trial last
Saturday in Port Jackson with the typical Port Jackson
22-footer Keriki. The wind was light, and Sydney boat-
ing men were not altogether displeased when the Keriki
fairly ran away from the British model. If the Keriki re-
peats the performance in a breeze, it is to be hoped Mr.
Foy will change his mind,and send an Australian model to
England to compete for English honors. Mr. Foy may have
made a mistake when he accepted the defeat of the old-
time ill-sailed Irex as indicative of the inferiority of the
Australian 22-footer to the English 24-ft. scow.
* * * *
We are requested to inform Bulletin readers
that the management of the Sydney Safe Deposit (in Ash-
street, back of Paling’s, Sydney) have decided, with a
view to popularising the use of the safes, to offer a special
discount of 25 per cent, to persons paying rent for two
years in advance. At present the annual rents in advance
are from two to six guineas, and the new system will
have the effect of reducing the rents to from to 4J
guineas. It has also been decided to lease safes at from
15s. per quarter and 255. per half year, a further innova-
tion.*

The Massey-Harris Company, Limited, of
Toronto, Canada, are evidently not feeling the pinch of
hard times, if we are to judge hy the extensions they are
making to their already very large warehouses at Broad-
way, where they have located their N.S.W. Branch.
Their present warehouses are filled to (heir utmost,
capacity with every description of implements for both
tillage and harvesting purposes, but they have found it
necessary recently to lease a plot of land covering
20(1 x 100 feet, immediately behind their present ware-
houses, and have built large bulk warehouses, black-
smith ruirl machine shop's, and other buildings necessary
for their growing trade. Tli y advertise themselves as
the largest manufacturers of implements under the
British 'flag, and if all their branch houses in different
parts of the world carry the stock and do the business
credited to the N.S.W. Branch, we can quite understand
their proud boast. They believe in the old truism that
“ you cannot sell.goods unless you have them in stock,”
and a visit to their warehouses would convince the most
sceptical that they not only preach but act.

The present position of Messrs. Wise
and See in re their sponsorship of John
Norton on his election to N.S.W. Assembly
and on the eve of his venting utterly abor-
tive charges of corruption against the Pre-
mier, recalls that of an individual sued for
affiliation at Balmain police court some years ago.
He said that before his illegitimate child was born
he felt ready to maintain it, but that the little
beggar when it came to light turned out to be so
darned ugly that he didn’t feel justified in spend-
ing any money on it.
Tommy Walker, opposing the Constitution
Bill in Perth: “Because a dog’s kennel was
offerer! to them they were not bound to crawl into
it. They -wanted Federation that would be wor-
thy of a great people.” Yet this is the i homas
Walker who sought and begged for a Federal
nomination at last N.S.W. general election ; the
man who, beinsf offered the most outside chance
of a seat as a Federal M.P., hustled all he knew
to crawl into it. It is also the Thomas Walker
who proclaimed this as the most democratic Con-
stitution ever offered, and who fought an election
on that basis. Perth Morning Herald, which
reports Walker at five-column length, is invited
also to report his speeches on the same provisions
as Federal candidate for bturt (N.S.W.).

Missing Friends.
THOMAS FLETCHER.
TNFORMATION is wanted about this person, who, if
I living, is entitled to a sum of money under the will
of his father, who died in England upwards of twenty
years ago. .
In or about the year 1830, Thomas Fletcher was living
in the neighborhood of Mount Cornish, Aramac, whence
lie communicated with his relatives in England, and in
the year 1886 he was heard of at Croydon.
Any information as to the present whereabouts of the
said Thomas Fletcher if living, or particulars of his death
if dead, will he thankfully received, and a suitable reward
given on behalf of his relatives by
MARSLAND, JARVIS & CO., Solicitors,
Gill-street,
Charters Towers, Queensland.

The following resolutions were carried unanimously : .
“That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated
among the Proprietors.”
“That C. J. COHEN, Esq., be re-elected a Director of the Bank.”
“That the Honorable K. T. HUMPHERY, M.L.C., and J. DE V. LAMB, Esq., be appointed Auditors for
the ensuing year.” , „ . ,
‘That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager, and Officers of
the Bank for their very satisfactor\ r management of the affairs of the institution.
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chairman, Sir Edward Knox, said :
“The Balance Sheet which the Directors to-day have the pleasure to present shows, when contrasted with that
and in Deposits £332,000, and is represented on the other side of the account by an addition of £492,000 to Liquid
As ets(Coin. Cash Balances, Government Securities, &c.), and by £154.000 extension of Advances. What I have said
clearly shows that, the Bank has not only maintained its business, but has made steady ptogress during the past year.
‘The general prospects of the Colony appear to me more encouraging than at anytime during the past four
years. The enhanced prices of wool must be cheering to our Pastoralists, who during the recent seasons of drought
have suffered greatly by the decrease in their flocks ; I refer particularly to the Graziers in the central and western
divisions of the Colon vi and it is to be hoped that the Government, in dealing with the 1 eases-am oil n ting to many
millions of acres—that fall due next year, will take as broad and as liberal a view as possible, and give the present
leaseholders a large portion of the areas they occupy on easy terms and for a fixed tenure.
“ The copious coastal rains of last month must greatly assist our Farmers both north and south of. Sydney, and l
am informed by our Country Inspectors that the lighter rains that have extended over the wheat-growing districts
in the west and south-west have done much good, and give promise of an abundant harvest.”

boys charged. Constable Maloney (loq.) : “The

Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*
“ H.J.” : It was at Sydney Labor Bureau,
in the bitter, dreary days when Govt, was feeding
some thousands of families in return for sand-
shifting at Centennial Park. A haggard, timid-
faced woman, with an infant in her arms, crept
into the office, and, in a husky voice, inquired,
“Can yer give me anything to eat, and a tin o
milk for the babby ? I’ve none for it." We sent
her in to the Boss. There was a bad report
against her, for having obtained two supplies of
rations in one week (Heaven knows, it was little
enough), and the Boss looked at her severely,
sniffed once or twice,:and then propounded a dif-
ficult problem : “ Did you have anything to eat
yesterday ? ” “Yes,” said the woman, “ a loaf o’
dry bread.” “How did you get that?” asked
the Boss. “ Got it on trust from the baker.” “If
you got one yesterday, can’t you get another
to-day ? I can do nothing for you in the
face of this report. The Benevolent might !
Show her out! ” As she was going out she
hvlf-turned, and then shut her teeth and walked
away. Ten minutes later, the struggling, cursing
crowd of men at the ticket-window, suddenly
divided to the cry of, “ Mike, let the leddy parss.”
And the same woman put her pinched face against
the window and asked for a ticket for the Park.
“ You can’t shift sand ! ” said the clerk. “ God
help me, I must,” said the woman. The clerk
thought hard. “ I can’t give YOU a ticket,” he
said, looking over the men suggestively. A man
whose face was covered with,tufts of red, shaggy
hair stuck his face against the window and
whined “ You never gave me mine, mister.” The
clerk didn't contradict him ; he hastily put the
useful, non-committal name of “J. Smith” on a
ticket and passed it out. There was a faint
cheer, the woman vanished, and the crowd closed
round the window, and the work went on. Next
day we heard that the woman had presented her-
self at the Park, tendered her ticket, and de-
manded a shovel. The clerk in charge hesitated.
“ You can’t refuse : I’ve got the ticket—me hus-
band’s ticket, an’ he’s sick in bed,” said the woman
excitedly, hugging her infant furiously. The
clerk gave way, the woman seized a shovel,
marched off, put her infant down, and took her
place beside a barrow. The ganger’s whistle
blew, the woman stuck her shovel into the dust
bravely and was starting on her weary job of shift-
ing sand—sand—sand, when the hatless clerk
dashed up : “ Woman, give me that ticket.” And
he snatched the ticket and marked it 11 hours
completed. “Go an’ get your rations ; and God
curse the ill-luck that sends a woman to a job like
this ! ” And the woman smiled faintly, gathered
up her baby, and staggered off towards Oxford-
street.

TO MAKE a Selection of Records from the
Catalogue requires a considerable amount of
thought, and, for the purpose of assisting would-
be purchasers, I am willing at all times to give the
benefit of my experience. Some purchasers have
a tendency to buy only those records they like
themselves; but, whereas one of the principal
objects of buying a
GRAMOPHONE
is to entertain one’s friends, it is essential to have
a variety of performers represented. Paris Cham-
bers on the Cornet gives some excellent represen-
tations of his great ability, and Arthur Pryor on
the Trombone is unique. The clear and sharp in-
terpretation of popular airs on the Xylophone by
Chas. P. Lowe stand out as unrivalled productions
of mechanical art. Shut your eyes and listen ;
then you will imagine the performer is present in
person. Set the instrument going in a distant
room of a house, start a popular song, and all
within a quarter-of-a-mile will flock to hear who
the new man or woman is that has come into the
locality.
Do not wait any longer; write to the under-
signed for full particulars and prices of the
GRAMOPHONE and RECORDS.

How It Is.
In the streets of the town where I live I some-
times meet a poor fejlow who is so badly off that
his appeal for a penny or two is hardly to be re-
sisted. He has lost both his legs above the knees
and punts himself along the pavement with his
hands, like a loaded barge in shallow' water.
Thank Mercy, one doesn’t often see human hulks
like him. Where there is a single instance of a
man having lost both legs or both arms there are
a dozen where only one limb of the pair is missing.
And where there is a single case of the latter sort
there are a hundred cases of people who are lame,
or more or less disabled, by disease or minor in-
juries which are scarcely noticeable, yet in the
long run very serious to those so afflicted.
Consequently, when we sum up both classes, we
perceive that it isn’t the total wrecks and the in-
curables that are most expensive to society, but
the prodigious host which must work, and dues
work, yet always under difficulties and against
hindrances. Men and women regularly em-
ployed, but who are continually breaking down in
a small way, thus losing fragments of time and
fractions of wages, are of the kind I mean. The
amount of income lost in this way in one year in
England is immense. And so far as the cause of
all this is disease, and not accident or born bodily
imperfection, it is almost always preventible and
generally curable. Look at this, for example, and
take heart.
“ In the spring of this year (1897),” the writer
says, “my health began to fail me. My appetite
was poor, and after meals I had pain and weight
at the chest. I could not sleep owing to the pain,
and I got weaker every day. I had so much pain
that I dared not eat, and rapidly lost flesh.
“ I was in agony night and day, and often sat
by the fire at night as I could not rest in bed. I
had a deal of muscular pain, particularly in the
arms. I gradually got worse and worse, and in
two months lost two score pounds weight.
“ I saw a doctor who gave me medicines and in-
jected morphia to ease the pain; but I was no
better for it. Then I met with a friend who told
me of the great benefit he had derived from the use
of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s Syrup. 1 got
a bottle of it from Mr. S. Richardson, chemist,
Bridgman-street, and in a week I could cat well
and food no longer distressed me. Therefore, I
kept on with the medicine, and soon was strong
and well. I am now in the best of health, and
recommend this remedy to all I meet. You are at
liberty to publish this letter as you like.”—(Signed)
William Bridge, Grocer and Baker, 65, Bridg-
man-street, Bolton, October sth, 1897.
Here we have an illustration of the proposition
with which this article sets out. From Mr.
Bridge’s account of his own case we see that he
lost a considerable time from his business. How
much that represents in money he does not say ;
nor is it important to the argument. For two
months or more he lost from his business practi-
cally all he was worth to it; and what that situa-
tion would have signified, had it been indefinitely
continued, any intelligent person can imagine.
Men frequently become stricken with poverty as
with illness in that way. However well any
business may be managed in an emergency by
others, it is not to be supposed that it gets on as
prosperously as when the proprietor is himself at
the helm. And he cannot be there while lie is
suffering agonies from disease. This is true even
if we make no calculation of the direct expenses
created by illness, nor of the suffering experienced
the latter not computable in terms of money.
Now, please remark how quickly Mr. Bridge
was cured of his ailment—bad as it seemed and
really was. Dating from the time he began using
Mother Seigel’s Syrup, he says “in a week I
could eat well, and the food no longer distressed
me.” His trouble was of the digestion only (acute
dyspepsia), for which this preparation long ago
proved itself a specific. Had he known of and
employed it when the attack began he would have
lost no time, felt no pain.
The lesson of the case is this : —As indigestion
is a common complaint, and dangerous also when
neglected, the remedy should be at hand for imme-
diate use when needed. The more valuable the
treasure the more strict should be the guard over
it. And health is a jewel compared with which
rubies are as the glass beads of savages.

CURES COUGH
A REGULAR MARTYR
TO
Biliousness
BUT NEVER
Troubles Him
NOW.
The Case of
THOMAS STEWART, Esq-
(From the. "Penrith Times.”)
BY OUR SPECIAL REPORTER.
“Unquestionably Clements Tonic alone
pulled me through. I couldn’t get an atom
of good out of the numerous other medicines
I tried, but this remedy did the business for
me in double quick time.” These were the
words of Mr. Thomas Stewart, baker,
Windsor-road, Rooty Hill, near Sydney, to
a reporter who was detailed to have a little
chat with him anent his sometime sickness.
“ Yes,” said that gentleman, offering the
newspaper man a chair, and taking a seat
himself. “ Once upon a time I frequently
had many strange attacks. I was attacked
with a giddy sickness. My food disagreed
with me, and I had to drop erring for a
time altogether almost. I lost all my
strength and energy. My work became a
burden; I seemed to be always tired.
Violent headaches came on. There was a
heavy feeling of oppression and great swel-
ling at the pit of the stomach after eating.
I passed terrible nights, sleep being almost
out of the question, and my mouth and
tongue were covered with a bitterish
substance in the mornings.”
“ You were bilious, apparently ?”
“Yes ; I used to be a regular martyr to
biliousness. I was never safe from fear
that I was going to be prostrated by a
bilious attack. I tried all sorts of medicines
to ward off the attacks when I felt them
coming on, and I took many cleverly
advertised peas, pills, pearls, and pellets.
They were guaranteed to cure everything
from measles to paralysis, but, with the
exception of Clements Tonic, all these
medicines were utterly useless in any stage
of my trouble.”
“ To return to your last attack 1”
“Oh ; yes,’’replied Mr. Stewart. “Well,
I had further distressing symptoms. A
severe bleeding at the nose set in. This
was most difficult to stop, and, as you may
expect, it was very weakening. The giddi-
ness became worse, the headaches more
unbearable, and I was suffei’ing from want
of proper rest and nourishment, for I could
eat nothing worth speaking of when it was
put before me; so I got a bottle of Clements
Tonic.”
“ How did you come to get that ?”
“ Well, you see, news was brought to me
of a worse case than mine being cured, and,
as the source of my information was trust-
worthy, as I said before, I sent for a
bottle.”
“ How did it act ?”
“ Just like a true pick-me-up. It started
to strengthen my disordered stomach and
digestive organs from the very first, and 1
could soon take my food without any sense
of oppression or swelling afterwards, and,
more than that, I could enjoy what I did
eat. I suffered no more from headaches,
and, after a few days, I slept as soundly as 1
always do when lam well. I can tell you
I was pleased to get rid of that giddiness.
To cut it short, Clements Tonic soon put
me as right as I am now, and have been ever
since, and I could again go about my work
with energy. Biliousness never troubles
me now, for if I feel a sign of it coming on,
I make haste to take a few doses o!
Clements Tonic, and that wards it off. 1
could tell you of more than one case where
the greatest benefit has been derived from
that remedy, and I know one hotel in
particular where it is regularly served over
the bar at 6d. for a tiny glass full, and the
proprietor of the hotel says it is far and
away the most effective pick-me-up to be
obtained for love or money.”
“ But I came to get your opinion ?”
“ Well, I think it is the only genuine
remedy going. Anyhow, it cured me ;it
did what none of the other things could do,
and I must speak as I find. The proof of
the pudding is in the eating, you know!”
“ What about printing this—you don’t
object ?”
“ You can do just as you think fit about
that. I’ve not the least objection as long
as you only print what I’ve said, for I
know that to be the pure truth.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Thomas Stewart, of Windsor-road. Rooty Hill,
near Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that' I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of three
folios, and consecutively numbered from one to
three, and that it contains and is a true and faith-

A Strange
Nervous Complaint.
A Complete Cure
after a Tour among the
Leading Lights
of the Medical Profession
without benefit.
The Case of
MRS. ANNE PRITCHARD.
(From the “Rochdale Advocate”)
Although midsummer, it was delightfully
fine and cool when our reporter arrived at
the pretty little town of Hurstville, which
Sydney claims as a suburb. Not far from
the railway station the newspaper man
found a large and particularly well kept
orchard, surrounding a solidly built stone
house, which overlooks wide stretches of
undulating bush, and, finally, the blue
waters of the Pacific itself. On enquiry it
turned out to be the very house he was
looking for, and a few minutes later saw
him comfortably ensconced on the verandah
enjoying the cooling breeze, and chatting
affably the while with Mrs. Pritchard, the
proprietress of this charming demesne.
Gradually the conversation veered round to
Mrs. Pritchard’s late illness and her
wonderful restoration to perfect health.
Our reporter, who had heard of the subject
from many neighbours, was all attention,
and missed nothing of the lady’s narrative.
“ I was attacked with a strange nervous
complaint,” said she. “ I didn’t know what
was the matter, and even the doctors were
entirely mystified. The cause of my com-
plaint was unknown to me then, and it is a
puzzle to me even to this day ; not that I
care now what ailed me, for I am completely
cured now, though not by the many clever
Sydney doctors who attended me, but by a
far simpler and cheaper process.”
“Yes,” said our reporter, deeply
interested. “ What was that ?”
“ Clements Tonic—and, indeed, I wanted
something that had good curative properties
I can assure you, for my disease was a weird
one. It heralded its arrival by a gradual
physical change, which is quite indescrib-
able. It was as though the light and hope
was gone out of my life. My nervous
vitality began to wane away, and my
physical strength started on the same down
grade. A severe and heavy depression of
spirits settled upon me like a nightmare. I
was no longer able to attend to household
matters. In fact, I felt I didn’t care what
became of me or my house. My interest in
everything was gone, and all my thoughts
were taken up with one desire, which was
to find out a way to get back my lost health
and spirits. My appetite was gone. Trifling
matters excited and frightened me, and the
most dreadful feelings of apprehension
frequently held sway over me. I fell away
most seriously in weight, and it was then
that I commenced my tour among the
doctors— not ordinary practitioners, but the
leading lights of the medical world. But
these gentlemen, with all their skill and
scientific knowledge, could make no pro-
gress towards the cure of my distressing
nervous malady. Some of them vaguely
hinted at general nervous weakness, others
hazarded a guess that my disease was one
of the heart. I took all their prescriptions,
but my condition was not ameliorated. I
then, in a sort of desperation, called on
several herbalists.”
“Ah !” said our reporter, somewhat
startled.
“Yes,” said Mrs. Pritchard, I know
now I made a mistake. There is no good
to be got that way, nor from their so-called
remedies, as I found out to my cost. At
last I almost resigned myself to my fate. I
thought there was no remedy for my disease.
I was entirely wrong. Clements Tonic was
pressed upon my notice, and its restorative
power made itself quickly felt. My nerves
daily gathered strength and tone through
the use of that remedy, and my spirits
improved wonderfully. That dreadful low-
spirited feeling was soon gone. Finally,
Clements Tonic put me in perfect health. I
had been ill for years, but it eradicated all
disease from my system in a very few weeks.
My nerves are as strong as can be desned,
and I haye not enjoyed such perfect health
and strength since I was a girl. All this is
owing to the use of Clements Tonic.
“Thank you,” said the reporter, putting
up his note book, ‘‘this will make interest-
ing matter for our paper. May we publish
it?”
“Yes.”

How ridiculous the fuss made by the dailies re
the interprovincial University rowing contests—simply
because the competitors are mostly scions of the Naicest.
The University crews have never been anything but
tenth-raters, and in Vic. their record at regattas is almost
a blank sheet. Melb. University crew has on no occasion
been equal to the average maiden eight of the year—
possibly through the silly practice of picking big, clumsy
men, and following other obsolete ideas still alive “ at
’Ome.”
* * * *
For seven years the farce of running N.S.W.
“cross-country championship” over sticks on an en-
closed track has been witnessed, but a recent infusion of
new blood wrought a change, and this season’s event
is contested as it should be—across country. Many of
the harriers relish not the change—they prefer previous
conditions because the going is a lot easier and their
best girls can get a better and more protracted view of
the pipe-stem underpins and pancake-like chests so plen-
tiful and prominent on such occasions.
* * * *
The device “ Copyright ” now accompanies the
cabled cricket scores posted in front of the offices of the
“ great ” Melb. dailies. The inferred threat is under-
stood to be levelled at sundry country sheets, whose
Melb. correspondents have been wiring the results taken
from the boards. But it is very doubtful whether there is
any copyright in news so published. Somebody with
nothing to lose should try a test case.
» « » *
“ G.S.” : An old man won a .£lO prize in a
Perth sweep ; invested another 55., and won another £lO,
having previously “ done-in” a £3O win. Then he came
to Perth, and met a “ gun,” who touched him for his
money and the winning ticket. The swindler was
arrested after cashing the ticket, but before he had spent
a cent of the old fellow’s coin. Yet they say there’s no
such thing as luck !
• * * •
One of the stock arguments of Ikey and his
friends in the press is that the tote only returns to the
public their own money, less commission ; and that
backers must therefore lose in the long run. Quite cor-
rect in its way. In all speculation the majority loses,
but there is a minority which wins. The idea evidently
intended to be convei'ed is that the bookmaker is a man
of means who risks his capital equally with the punter.
But how many of our leading layers started with a capi-
tal ? This writer has been a regular race-goer—to his
sorrow —for many years, and has witnessed the rise of
most of the leading Vic. bookmakers. To select a few of
the big “ cash” men. One was originally a corporation
laborer, two started in small tobacconist’s shops, one
attended to the river traffic on a saltwater bridge (open-
ing and shutting same), one was a “ tick-tacker,” one
sold papers and race-cards in the streets, and one taught
a Sunday school in Collingwood ! The smaller men have
started on even lower rungs. Not one in ten commenced
with more than a bag, a brass-plate, a fiver, and a cast-
iron cheek. Yet. punters keep on walking home after the
races—those who win may squeeze ten in a“ bob” cab—
and see the books travelling home in hansoms or natty
private turn-outs. Racing may be a funny game, but
those who follow it seem to have no sense of humor.
The ad venturous spirit of the present Australian
Ring. An effort was made some little time back to
secure £lO,OOO at 100 to 3 about Fleet Admiral (who has
not started for two years) for next Melbourne Cup. By
great exertions £7OOO was got at that price, but the
layers then refused to go on at a longer price than 100 to
4. Yet there is a legend that First Water was once
backed for a Melb. Cup to win £lOO,OOO, and then, started
at 100 to 4 !
* * * •
Apropos the Ikey Mo strike over the “Prosper”
affair. Sydney Tatt. bookies had a somewhat similar
experience some years back, but they got out of their
trouble by discovering that the rung-in one was just
under the necessary age when he joined and had been
disqualified.
* * * *
At recent Charters Towers cycle sports Bil-
martin (specially engaged for £3O) headed a team in a
pursuit-race. The prizes were £lO to the winning team,
and £6 to the undefeated rider covering the greatest dis-
tance before the bell rang. Martin started from scratch,
and took the £6 ; leaving the four opponents £2 10s.
each, and his three assistants nil. Moral : £6 is more
than £2 10s.
• * * *
Be the proposed oiroling of Australia on bikes
by the brothers White, “H.S.” writes: “Even if they
get through with the pretty tall contract of circling the
oivilised portion of the continent, they will still leave
about 1600 miles of the Far North and N. W. untraversed
by any man on wheels. The brothers left Sydney last
Monday for Brisbane —thence to go on to Townsville;
then inland to Charters Towers; then across country to
Normanton and Burketown. Thence they go to Port
Darwin, and this portion of the journey null probably he
very severe on their tyres. From Darwin on to Hall s
Creek, and down the Fitzroy to Derby on Kings
Sound; and then on to Perth, and round by the
Great Australian Bight to Adelaide, and on to Mel-
bourne, their terminus. One of the brothers, who has
previously cycled the Bight, says much of the way
is splendid going for wheels, and it must be remem-
bered that the telegraph-line now spans the waste.
The pair are taking no blankets —only waterproof sheet-
ing; and their food will consist chiefly of damper, beef
and potatoes, with —by my advice—rice. I also advised
them to get a little rough chart made of any specially
troublesome passes or crossings. They will ride the 251 b.
‘ Dayton ’ road machine, with the new ‘ Hussey’ spherical
hubs, 3-point bearings, and specially-constructed Dunlop
tyres. They hope to eat their Christmas dinner in Mel-
bourne. To actually cycle round Australia would be a
very dangerous and arduous task, especially in the cross-
ing of the great rivers, one of which (the Alligator) is
never less than three miles wide for a distance of ten
miles. Then there is the terrible sandstone on edge (that
even tears shoes off horses and severely cuts their legs),
and the risk of attack by natives. But, even as it is, the
proposed undertaking is a very plucky one, and the
brothers may well be wished good luck.”
• • * •
A pathetio sight at Sydney race-meetings is
poor old “ Tom ” Ivory, the good, straight sport and
owner of champions in bygone days. He is quite blind,
but the ruling passion is still strong, and he is satisfied to
be led about in the ring to listen to the betting and
describe to the younger generation what a good horse
Sweetmeat was. The old fellow enjoys himself thoroughly,
and his delight when “Jack” Mayo gathered in the
Hawkesbury Handicap with Carberra, t’other day, was
boundless.
* • * *
“S.”: Be the oft-heard complaint that we
have nowadays few “ stayers ” amongst our racing stock.
Looking over the events at a recent Hawkesbury Club
meeting, I find that the only flat-race exceeding f-mile
was the Winter Handicap, lj-mile and 100yds. This is
not the sort of thing to encourage breeding for stamina.
Six-furlong courses and light weights are responsible for
the present prevailing stamp of horse—good for a short,
fast flutter, and then done. It will be a good day for the
quality of our thoroughbred stock that sees weights
raised and distances increased. Every meeting of half-a-
dozen events should have at least half of them at over 6
furlongs, say from 1 mile to Is. This, though not all
that could be desired, would be a step in the right direc-
tion. Over 30 years ago writer’s horse, Marmion (4 years),
won in a canter the Casterton (Vic.) 3 miles handicap
with 9st. 61b. up, and then won by half-a-distance the
following event, in half-mile heats over post-and-rail
fences, carrying list. 101 b. (the total distance making six
miles straight off the reel), and finished fairly fresh. In
the same district, Necromancer, full-brother to this
horse’s dam, ran four consecutive 2-mile heats, carrying
10.1, to win the Town Plate. And, about same time, the
chestnut gelding Mac, then close upon 20 years old,
carried 10.1 to a win in Adelaide Town Plate of 2 miles.
* * * *
The new S.A. Governor lately visited a big
football-match for the first time, and word therefore
went round in the dressing-room that, out of respect for
his Ex., play must not be rough. For about half-an-hour
the game was most nancified, but trouble began
through an irascible “ back,” rusty for want of a fight,
throwing one of the enemy a double somersault almost
into the Governor’s box. This started the crowd, and
from that out the Australian national adjective was
hurled round, and a section of the barrackers surrounded
the umpire and offered to cremate him. H.E. says he is
“profoundly impressed ’’ with the Australian game.
Mail details show that Californian giant Jeffries
was far too good for Australian Fitzsimmons, who
weakened perceptibly from round to round while the big
fellow’s strength and youth told more and more in his
favor as the battle wore on. Receipts totalled 86,270
dols.; half went to the club and the balance to the
fighters, Fitzsimmons getting 25,881 dols. and Jeffries
17,254. Fitz, in view of possibilities, shrewdly' insisted
that, win or lose, he, being the “draw card,” should
receive 60 per cent, of their portion and Jeffries, rather
than risk letting the chance slip, acquiesced. Apparently
the world’s new champion is a fighter who will fight,
irrespective of color, creed or class, as directly after
licking the Australian he arranged a match with Sailor
Sharkey, winner to take the whole of the purse. “ Then,”
said Jeffries, “I’ll be ready to meet anyone else.” Re-
freshing this, when placed alongside the two and three
year-intervals between fights demanded by his immediate
predecessors on the top rung—Sullivan, Corbett and Fitz-
simmons.
• » • *
What a freak in build Fitzsimmons is may be
judged from the fact that he measures 44in. round the
chest, to giant Jeffries’ 43J4- Round biceps and calf,
Fitz. is only the size of a well-developed boy—l2J4in.
biceps and 13J4 oalf, as against Jeffries’ 16in. and 17)4
respectively.
• • * *
From accounts to hand of the big fight it looks
as if Fitzsimmons made the mistake of most modern
fighters —trying from the start for a right-hand knock-
out. Jeffries, on the contrary, seems to have used a
good long left which must have shaken up Fitz. con-
siderably ere he discovered he was playing the wrong
game and changed his tactics. Fitz. got home just such
another straight left on the “ mark ” as that which set tied
Corbett, but it failed to do the trick with young and
lusty Jeffries, who soon wore off the effects of the blow.
Jeffries fairly beat Fitz. with his straight left blows,
whilst Fitz. was throwing his strength away in futile
attempts to cross with the right; about the only fair
right-hander which Jeffries landed being the final smash
which knocked the Australian senseless.
* • * •
“Pat”: The ascendancy of “Ould” Erin’s
sons in most branches of sport is remarkable. Latest
batch of British “passed” records embrace these extra-
ordinary authenticated achievements by dwellers in the
Green Isle. That famous “lepper,” the Irish school-
master Newburn, has to his credit 24ft. 3£in., accom-
plished in the Ireland v. Scotland competitions. P.
Leahy has his name before the prodigious high jump of
6ft. 4Jin., and T. F. Kelly can point to a hammer throw
of 151 ft. llin. Roseingrave, who held the world’s broad-
jump record (put up in Sydney) until a year or so since,
hails from the Ould Sod.
* * * *
Recent performances by world’s record-holders,
Kranzlein (Amer.) and Irish schoolmaster Newburn, point
to the probability ere long of a 25ft. broad-jump. Wonder
what manner of men were the ancient Greek judges who
passed a 55ft. record? Recent Pan-Britannie at Athens
saw a champion Greek athlete clear 18ft. with difficulty.
How the mighty have degenerated !
• * • *
We can very well afford to admit, in a soft
undertone, that the Australians have had some good luck
in three matches where they needed all they could get.
The draw with Yorkshire was in their favor, seeing that
the County had but one more wicket to fall and 43 runs
to make, but—Jackson and Rhodes didn’t play for York-
shire. The downfall of Briggs and the break-up of the
fine weather were misfortunes for England in the third
test, and the Midland Counties team seems to
have made a close fight under difficulties. Lilley, the
wicketkeeper, who did good work behind the wickets and
subsequently scored 37 runs, injured his hand on the first
day, and was of no more use to the Midlanders. Then,
on the third morning, when they wanted 144 runs with 7
wickets to fall, the wicket had been spoilt by more rain.
The cable said “wet night, attendance small,” which,
under the interesting circumstances of the game, must
have meant that the weather was gloomy. There was no
excuse for Melb. Aob to “ pad ” the truth in this style :
“ Rain fell again this morning, making the wicket soft,
but while it remained wet and greasy the bowlers were at
a disadvantage." The “ greasiness ” evidently was a
loyal Australian fiction, tor six batsmen made only 29
run 3 among them, whilst A. O. Jones was “ playing
beautiful cricket.” The Age, which invariably caters for
local prejudice, slung in the statement that “ At the con-
clusion of the match the Australians were congratulated
on having pulled it out of the fire.” “ Out of the water”
would have been a more honest phrase.

“Krek”: I have to correct “Spero Meliora ”
(8..1/7/’99) about the Longreach buck-jumping exhibition.
He says the two blackboys gave the cleverest goes.
Well, as one of the goes consisted of getting thrown clean
from a routinsr horse, I think that though the “go ’’ was
the nigger’s, the “ cleverness ” was the horse’s. Certainly
the other blackboy rode well, but the winner of the
second prize was every whit as good, and had a more
vicious mount.
In a list of J.’sP. lately gazetted for
Southland (M.L.) province were included the
names of four dead men—one had been dead
four years—and one man who left the country three
years ago. And the Southlanders are dissatisfied.
This paper reckons that nobody should be ap-
pointed to the cheap and nasty magistracy until
he is dead. As a rule the best J.P. is the deadest.
The stealing of the coins from under the
foundation-stone of Kalgoorlie Masonic Hall, laid
lately by Gov. Gerard Smith, merely repeats a
phase of very old Australian history. Theft of
coins from under foundation-stones was a pro-
minent lag weakness of early Botany Bay. St.
Phillip’s Church lost coins, St. Andrew’s lost
coins. But the champion foundation-stone coin-
loser was Gov. Macquarie. He laid St. Matthew’s
stone at Windsor twice, and the coins were stolen
each time. The stone was laid a third time and
the church stands, but old hands still doubt as to
whether the selected coins of the third era were
not similarly purloined. Apropos, what is the
sense of laying a stone and putting current coin
under it? In the stone’s indefensible position it
is an invitation to theft.
A Bulletin correspondent has a grievance
about Sydney ’buses:
Concerning ’bus-drivers : On the Oxford-street lines
where they have to compete with the trams the drivers
are, as a rule, decent sort of men. On the lines that
radiate from Miller’s Point along George-street to the
Railway and the various suburbs, they are mostly unmiti-
gated wild beast, and a very nasty breed of wild beast at
that. At Miller’s Point terminus fights are frequent, and
one famous and terrific scrap is the local standard of com-
parison. No other scrap within the memory of man has
equalled it. It is quite common for the drivers of depart-
ing 'buses to yell abuse at each other till out of earshot.
Also it is common for two crossing ’buses to pull up and
the drivers thereof to discuss the burning questions of
’bus-society with a loudness of voice and Old Testament
baldness that are positively startling. On one memorable
occasion at the terminus, two drivers adjourned to drink,
over an oath of eternal palship probably, and left the
teams “on their own.” One bolted through a crowd of
children (who were saved by the usual miracle that daily
rescues small children from the jaws of death), cleared the
pavement, and generally spread terror before, they were
stopped. Everybody about declared that something
** oughter be done 1 ” j one, indignant parent threatened
to stoush the driver, but mercifully refrained and the
affair was forgotten. In wet weather many of the ’buses
are a snare and a take-down. They leak horribly, the
windows won’t keep up, the wind howls through them
and the rain pours in. At night most of them are abso-
lutely without lights inside. The drivers bang the bell
immediately an unconvoyed woman puts her nose inside
the door, and drones “ fare, plise.” If a half-crown is
handed up, change is given for a florin. Few people
are known to have had a half-crown in those dark
’busses. The sooner the tram comes along and wipes out
the whole race of cheats the better for the credit of this
awful city.


Vic. Chief Sec. Peacock has introduced a
bill which, if it can only be pushed through the
‘‘other place,” will give the 48 Fat Landlords
“one in the eye ” for their treatment of the Alien
Immigration Bill last session. It won’t prevent
the unwashed Abdul and the unspeakable 'Fatta
from entering the province, but it will nobble
them when they get there by providing that no
hawkers license shall issue to anyone, unless he
proves that (a) he is natural-born or naturalised
(6) that he possesses a sufficient knowledge of the
Lnghsh language and coinage, and (e) that during
the previous two years he has resided at least 12
months m Victoria. The mild Hindoo and the
equally mild and equally objectionable Syrian
have a complete comprehension of the coinage
system, and a “sufficient” acquaintance with
Finghsh may mean anything; but the 12 months’
residence clause will do the trick, provided the Act
be thoroughly administered. That is, if the House
of Fat can be coerced into passing it.

The Crime of Fate.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, here’s a health to the square of jaw and the
large receding chin,
The petty thief and the murderer who follow the
paths of sin;
The lying knave and the swindler, the fool and
the beery clod,
Burned with the brand of Predestined Crime by
the will of the one Great God ;
And hang the murderer heaven-high, and bury
him deep as hell,
In the name of God, who created him and saw
that it was well. ‘
Bury him deep as a loathsome thing, cover him up
with lime;
But, was it the crime that stamped the face, or
the features that stamped the crime ?
To the lunatic who is not insane, for the eyes of
the law are keen,
Whose destiny worked to a six-foot drop—shall
we say what he might have been ?
He might have been this or he might have been
that, but God was his Maker still,
Who placed desire in his poor, weak brain, and
muttered “ Thou shalt not kill! ”
As a babe may hold to a plunging horse, he holds
to his fierce desire;
Will he find a curb in a hope above or a fear of
eternal fire ?
“Resist! Resist!” is the preacher’s cry, “let
Victory’s paths be trod.”
Resist by the power of his poor, weak brain the
will of a tyrant God !
To the slum-born babe who is birth-aecursed with
hunger and want and shame,
Who may not covet his neighbor’s goods, though
he covet in hunger’s name;
Drag him not to the Sunday-school from the filth
of the family barn,
Lest he find a startling parable in the good old
Bible-yarn,
And the Lord who fed the ravens on the a°nny
and the blood °
Of His weaker, smaller creatures, might be mis-
understood ;
And the ravens turn to fat-men in his little brain
agog,
While he illustrates God’s mercy on a sacrificial
frog.
To the little hands that are taught to steal—little
lips that are taught to lie ;
To all the weak and the damned of Earth, to Fate
( and the Reason Why ;
To the generation yet to come, and e’en to the
third and fourth,
Who foot the bill of their father’s sins to appease
a, just God’s wrath ;
To the unblown bugles and silent harps of the
souls who fell from Grace;
To an empty Heaven and crowded Hell, and the
joy in Satan’s face.
For the weak of will and the strong of will must
battle the deadly game;
And the strong shall stand and the weak shall fall
—and whose is the praise or blame?
To a true God and a good (rod —fighting for eaoh
man’s soul,
Who takes not the righteous as His tithe when
Peter calls the roll,
But holds as each man’s heritage till the breaking
of the dawn,
A seat by Jordan’s River for the curse of Being
Born ;
For the curse of Life Unasked-for, whose reins are
held by fate—
Demand no hell’s repentance to pass the Pearly
Gate.
To the music of his lifetime each man must foot
his dance,
With God or the Devil to pipe for him—accord-
ing to circumstance !
C.P.Q.C.
In a Northern river town a champagne
dinner was arranged for Parochialist Heydon by
an extreme Anti-Billite. Neither the host nor
Heydon appeared, and the dinner was enjoyed
by socialist Dacey, the local Mayor (an out-and-
-9, l l. t l . oran^ man ). and ari ELC. priest—an ardent
Bilhte. „ Needless,to say the Pope and King Billy
were kept carefully on the shelf.
S. A. factories - inspectors are generally
pretty frank; and they are'out now with some
remarks about sweating in Adelaide. Prom one
point of view the most satisfactory feature of the
disclosures is that the worst of the sweaters are
Asiatics. A Syrian firm paid one woman the
princely rates of 6d. per pair for mens tweed,
serge, and mole trousers ; 4d. per pair for dun-
garees, lOlrd. per dozen for petticoats, and from
Is. 6d. per dozen for shirts. Working 16 hours a
day the woman and her sick husband averaged
15s. per week. And the most unsatisfactory
feature ‘of the pitiful business is that the .Syrian
hawker who peddles these goods finds his best
customers in the houses of men who have most to
dread from this form of competition.

Dear Bulletin.—The fact that all armies are three-
parts ‘spit and. polish” until wanted, is proved by the
argument you give in a recent issue. There would be no
scarlet, fever ” ' unless show sodgers paraded well and
often. What hushmari jvants. to join anything but a rifle-
club, and that only because he gets a shooting-stick and
cartridges free, as well as a means of gambling ! I in-
spected a whole army once, on the very throne of. Solo-
nion, the Tukhtd-Suliman, and no two privates'Were
dressed or armed alike. The muskets ranged from
Mantons in a long descending scale to the jingal or
matchlock. Yet these men. made first-class soldiers,
after six months of the aforesaid “spit and polish.” Even
the band only knew one tune—“ God Save Her,” which
did for overture, quick and slow march, and was cut Up
for the trumpet and bugle-calls. What if the. com-
mander-in-chief stood by in a mixture of French, Eno'-
lish, and Cashmere insignia ? He was a soldier still, and
a gentleman, and his report was that “ Your (i e. my)
lordship s army is in splendid order,” and my— that is
his—lordship gave him half-a-pound of Queen’s gunpow-
der. Only last week I was told that the N.S. Wales
police have still the old Terry and Galisher carbine,
t ancy in 1890 a.d. a force turning out armed with alleged
breech-loaders that require tissue cartridges and a per-
cussion cap ! The best corps I ever officered was armed
with swords of all shapes and sizes, bought at two rupees
each (400 of them), not for use, but just, to show they
were enlisted ( burti ). The bestness, however, was that I
could stop their pay and sack them. Only four deserted,
and that after receiving a hint that their wages were a
dozen a week. Bolivar’s cavalry had only a blanket with
a hole in it for their uniform ; dervishes only want a
green or yellow turban : but they can fight. Some corps
only dress in a coat of oil, others in pipeclay patches,
polish” 6rn warriors are nowhere except per “ spit and
A Boarding-House Idyl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She said she was not one of those meek and mild
women, thank Heaven ! She likewise voiced her
objection to being regarded as a little tin-Joss-on-
wheels. The boarders obeyed her injunction
without experiencing any great wrench to their
respective judgments.
A small-faced little woman, with short brown
hair, solid-looking like a wig, and one of those
supple, round, boy-girl figures—that was Miss
Pouneer. Her Christian name, appropriately
enough, was Charlie.
As the foremost of the few lady residents in
that Melbourne boarding-house, she set the
fashion in morals and manners.
Both were original. Shocked, but pleased, the
new-chum blushed a fine, large, English blush
when Charlie invaded his room in the morning and
chatted to him while he added the finishing-
touches to his toilet. Charlie had hinted dark
suspicions concerning the virtue of people who
couldn’t go openly into one another’s rooms. So,
to preserve their reputations, everybody went.
Ihe actor and the poet shared the same room.
After their first meeting with Charlie they agreed
she was not at all the sort of woman they would
have liked as a sister. A week later they thought
so still. Each wanted her as a wife.
From that moment both eagerly noted marks of
preference for one or other of them. The poet
considered he had the better show.
He was a small poet—a pocket-edition, with
shimmering hair that gave him a likeness to the
notorious, tipsy, daffodilly bard, top-heavy with
the dew of vernal showers. Only Ralston’s dew
was “ Mountain.”
Ralston and Hetherway were friends. Hether-
way was sometimes drunk; Ralston occasionally
sober. J
As an erotic poet, claiming to have plucked his
burning verses from the Tree of Life, Ralston
knew women. He declared he could predict every
word that Charlie would utter. For the question
he would ask, he predicted “ Yes.”
The actor, being merely an exponent of other
men s thoughts, agreed that the poet might know
best. It is true that Charlie once boxed his ears
but Ralston could prove that she had granted him
a similar favor. Again, Hetherway appeared
radiant with the discovery that Charlie sometimes
dispraised him. He had read in a book that that
was a good sign. But Ralston quenched the faint
hope kindly, but firmly. Charlie was that extreme
contradiction to her sex who refuses to be a con-
tradiction. Hetherway retired to think this over.
Neither did much to attract his enslaver. Her
choice was probably made. Their business was to
await some word or look that would make it
known.
But the day arrived for Hetherway’s departure
to the provinces, and the oracle was still mute.
Hetherway was packing-up. That is to say, he
stood in the middle of his room and flung clothes
and books into a box in one corner. To him en-
tered the poet, drunk with unaccustomed sobriety.
Hetherway, attend ! I would not have thee
linger in thy pain.”
